THE RED PAGE.
Verses by Stephen Crane.
J I HOU art my love,
8 And thou art the peace of sundown,
J- When the blue shadows soothe,
And the grasses and the leaves sleep
To the song of the little brooks—
Woe is me.
Thou art my love,
And thou art a storm
That breaks black in the sky,
And, sweeping headlong,
Drenches and cowers each tree,
And at the panting end
There is no sound
Save the melancholy cry of a single owl-
Woe is me !
Thou art my love,
And thou art a tinsel thing,
And I in my play
Broke thee easily.
And from the little fragments
Arose my long sorrow—
Woe is me.
Thou art my love, - ~ »
And thou art a weary violet,
Drooping from sun-caresses,
Answering mine carelessly—
Woe is me.
Thou art my love,
And thou art the ashes of other men’s love,
And i bury my face in these ashes,
And I love them—
Woe is me. . .
Thou art my love
And thou art a temple
And in this tern pie is an altar,
And on this altar is my heart—
Woe is me.
Thou art my love,
And thou art a wretch,
Let these sacred loves-lies choke thee,
For I am come to where I know your lies as truth
And your truth as lies —
Woe is ine.‘ - ’ * ••
Thou art my love, . , ■
And thou art a priestess,
And m thy hand is a bloody dagger.
And uny doom comes to me surely—
Woe is me.
Thou art my love
And thuu.art a skull with ruby eyes,
A nd I love thee— '
Woe is me.
: Thou ant my love, ■> •
And I doubt thee. .
And if peace came with thy murder
Then would I murder—
Woe is me.
Thou art my love.
And thou art death,
Aye,,thou art death.
Black and yet black.
But I love thee,
I love thee. —
. Woe, welcome woe, to me.
What?
You define me God with these trinkets?
Can my misery meal on an ordered walking
Of stirpliced numskulls?
And a fanfare of lights ?
Or even upon the measured pulpitings
Of the familiar false and true ? i
Is this God ?
Where, then, is hell ?
Show me some bastard mushroom
Sprung front a pollution of blood.
It is better.
Where is God ?
When the prophet, a complacent fat taan,
Arrived at the mountain-top,
He cried: “ Woe to my knowledge !
I intended to see good white lands
And bad black lands.
But the scene is grey. ”
A little ink more or less I
It surely can’t matter ?
Even the sky and the opulent sea,
The plains and the hills, aloof,
Hear the uproar of all these books.
But it is only a little ink more or less.
A slant of sun on dull brown walls,
A forgotten sky of bashful blue.
Towards God a mighty hymn,
A song of collisions and cries,
Rumbling wheels, hoof-beats, bells,
Welcomes, farewells, love-calls, final moans,
Voices of. joy, idiocy, warning, despair,
The unknown appeals of brutes,
The chanting of flowers,
The screams of cut trees,
The senseless babble of hens and wise men—
A cluttered incoherency that says at the star :
“ O God, save us! ”
A newspaper is a collection of half-in justices
Which, bawled by boys from mile to mile,
Spreads ite curious opinion
To a million merciful and sneering men,
While families cuddle the joys of the fireside
When spurred by tale of dire long agony.
A newspaper is a court
Where every one is kindly and unfairly tried
By a squalor of honest men.
A newspaper is a market %
Where wisdom sells its freedom
And melons are crowned by the crowd.
A newspaper is a game
Where his error scores the player victory
While another’s skill wins death.
r, A newspaper is a symbol;
It is fetless life’s chronicle,
A collection of loud tales
Concentrating eternal stupidities,
That in remote ages lived unhaltered,
Roaming through a fenceless world.
“ Have you ever made a just man ? ”
“Oh, I have made three,” God,
But two of them are dead,
And the third —
Listen 1 Listen !
And you will hear the thud of his defeat.”
Ah, God, the way your little finger moved.
As you thrust a bare arm backward
And made play with your hair
Arid a comb, a silly gilt comb.
—Ah, God —that I should suffer
Because of the way a little finger moved.
From “ War is Kind."
Chopin.
CHOPIN is the most intimate of the pet
composers. So sympathetic and subjec-
tive is he, that he will mirror any complex
mood, shot like silk with opalescent,
incompatible emotions, of that strange thing
whioh is assuredly not the soul, decidedly not the
heart, above all not the mind. He belongs to the
interpretative order of creative artists. He is an
exquisitely sensitive psychological magnifying-
glass through which the dilettante in emotion may
study the almost invisible microbes that eat the
poet’s heart--that infinitesimal delicatesse of pain
and of passion for which the blunt majority has
no eyes. To interpret Chopin, one must be a
virtuoso in passion. Yet Chopin is no sen-
timentalist in the accepted term ; he is too
exquisite for sentimentality, which has for
me a Teutonic ring, a suggestion of home-
liness, of Schumann or even of Mendelssohn
—in short, an inextricable-element of slowness
impossible to lyric grace. Chopin’s fiery Slavonic
blood saved him from sentimentality ; he is a tiny
tiger rather than a pussy eat. Consider the
delicious Savagery that informs.his violent, splen-
did Polonaises, or the idealistically mordente
afietuoso feeling in his fifth Chant polonms, so
kindly set for the piano bv Liszt. But Chopin
misses the amiable sentimentalism with which he
is popularly accredited, chiefly by reason of his
impersonality. For he was not a conscious Will,
but rather a medium through which all the polite
passions expressed themselves in delicate inevit-
able art of a sugary femininity, a polished soul-
mi rror that reflected fine emotional phase? with a
dainty, unerring instinct for selection of the
prettiest.. None but Chopin could be so elemental
in miniature. In his iVutsic is pictured a whole
wild'liitle world of poet Led passions incuriously
dramatic scenes, each vivid or nebulous dream
perfect in its own completeness. He was essen-
tially a ton - painter, not a tone poet. For which
reason he does not demand intellect of his ex-
ponents so much as a fine sense of colour. His
dwn colour instinct was miraculous. Through
the cool white medium of the piano he produced
tone colours of infinite variety and intensity, of a
quite jewelled brilliancy—clear, sharp, and deli-
cate. His chaste devotion to the piano kept him
of necessity narrow and limited of resource in re-
gard to effect, but he left no recess of the piano’s
possibilities unexplored ; he was the Nansen of
the arctic, glittering piano.
He had a quite feminine love of musical em-
broidery and ornament. All his more fanciful
tone pictures are decorated with leaping, fantastic
arabesques of ethereal Jack-in-the-box-like runs—
pearled lightning, magic-strung, with a kind of
radiant spray of grace notes, a brilliant affecta-
tion which creates an atmosphere of fairyland.
Chopin’s music is ever ideally elfish and remote.
And there is in it a certain ecstatic quality, a faint
insistent intoxication which never fails. This one
feels most in the Nocturnes. Those warm dark noc-
turnes, fitfully moonlit or starlit, are like the tran-
scribed dreams of an inspired opium-eater. Each is
a tone picture wrought with a kind of enchantment;
the whole series is under an occult spell. Curi-
ously elemental in feeling, those nocturnes give
windless trees and shindig water and the summer
night, lime-sc’ented, with such a velvet languor
that they can hypnotise a winter afternoon into
softness. To me, the E-flat nocturne always sug-
gests Keats’ “Ode to a Nightingale.” The
F-sharp nocturne is quite Persian in style : it
paints the palm and the cypress and the rose
thicket, and the great stars burning low in the
Southern sky. Needless to say, Chopin is in-
tensely Oriental in feeling. His most expressive
and individual music has a perfumed gorgeousness,
an exotic grace, and a multiplicity of modulations
that breathe of a subtler notation and of a glowing
tropical climate. Yet he can be semplice in the
extreme : many of his tone pictures are wrought
with slight vigorous strokes ; but this impression-
ism results from sheer refinement of style, and is
the outcome of centuries of elaboration—indeed, it
is merely the disrobing of a melody which we are
wont to see go in tissue of gold. Chopin was a
master of detail, but he was saved from pettiness
by that fundamental Slav impetuosity and ferocity
which no French polish could smooth entirely
away.
Barbarism and civilisation war even more
piquantly in Chopin than in Tschaikowsky. It is
this lion-and-unicorn struggle for the mastery in
.his nature that gives such keen life to his music ;
without it, indeed, ha might easily have drifted
into a William Morris-like meandering among
lovely cadences, a dallying with delicious chords.
As it is, he only “moons” adorably in unearthly
music of Keats-like beauty of tone-diction (for this
once permitting him to be a tone-poet). And yet
they say that Chopin is Tike Shelley. When lie is
thoroughly awake he may be, but contrast the
sustained swiftness of Shelley’s lyric flight, alert
as a humming-bird’s, with the dreamful motion of
Chopin, who falls trancedly asleep witli the casual
ease of the Dormouse at the Hatter’s tea-party.
There is the vivid fire of life at first hand, spite of
all the hysterical idealism, in Shelley’s strenuous
verse : all the life in Chopin’s music is a reflection,
idealised, devitalised as a Burne Jones picture.
Chopin’s motto is the dreamer’s motto Jam
yesterday and jam to-morrow, but never jam to-
day.” And especially jam yesterday ! The mad-
dening memory overshadow's the intangible hope.
Jam yesterday, a dim, strange yesterday, trans-
cending limits of mere mortal time. You may
read this legend in the G minot Ballade,
and in the first movement of the B minor sonata,
as also in the F minor concerto. Chopin revels in
the luxury of sorrow ; his dreams die gorgeously
as ashes of sunset into a velvet night of gloom—
and they take an unconscionable time a-dying!
Sometimes the starry articulated notes of his
melody seem to float like water-lilies on a rhythmic
tide of harmonies that loses itself in a sea of deli-
cious sadness. That section in the G-flat which
comes just before the Funeral March in B-flat
minor sonata suggested this to me.
One of Chopin’s compositions has never ceased
to puzzle me. Under what alien influence did he
write it—the Ballade in A -flat ? It is so pecu-
liarly unlike himself in its boyish swagger and its
gallant light-heartedness ; it is so young, so cock-
sure, and so successful, drawing to a logical happy
close instead of ceasing gloomily in the customary
manner. The other three Ballades are perfectly,
even markedly, Chopinesque ; this one is a cuckoo,
a bbld, jolly cuckoo in the nest. Moreover, it is
most gaily masculine; while nearly all Chopin’s
works incline to be feminine, as indeed he was
himself. Witness his Berceuse, a little wrought
ivory piece of exquisitiveness, just sufficiently
trivial, whioh is, however, so delicately and ecsta-
tically amorous that it misses its mark as a cradle-
song. Indeed, Chopin never contrives to be any-
thing but amorous, whether in his dances, his
sonatas, or his nocturnes. His love (of course he
had none) was certainly his whole existence.
A n l, like every true Platonist, he had the instinct
of passion for publication. Of what use is Love
but as a soul for Art ? Love should ever move to
measure of music in delicate fetters of rhyme.
Chopin thoroughly understands the value of
passion for publication ; he sorrows in beautifully
rounded periods. And he has created a passionate
tradition of his own, a sweet individual note of
passion—a cobra without the poison, Heine with-
out the irony. This tradition is, strictly speaking,
a little bit rococo ; in the grasp of an old-fashioned
pianist it can even be Victorian. But nothing is
easier than to bring it up to date by encouraging
the tigerish element that sleeps so sweetly under
its surface sentiment —a charming savage instinct
which is bqth ineradicable and irresistible. We
may not like it, we may not choose but love it.
In Chopin’s music this semi-barbaric instinct is
the phoenix undying, refined, spiritualised. While
people continue to have hearts and senses and
tempers, Chopin will never be outworn ; for he is ,
too deeply elemental, spite of all his misleading
graces, to be ever out of touch with the Zeitgeist,
which is after all merely a phoenix. Indeed, so
splendidly can Chopin keep up the pace, that in
skilful hands he runs neck and neck with Tschai-
kovvsky in the expression of our more exqusite and
degenerate humanity of to-day.
So poignant a voice is his, that everyone has
heard it, or, at least heard of it. He is the gracious
catchword of the unmusical. Though Rubinstein
might be a Rhine wine, and Grieg a cheese,
Chopin is without doubt a musician. How rudely
his delicate self-centred romanticism has been
misinterpreted by mediocrity ! Mediocrity loves
Chopin—sincerely, I think. His leading traits
are so obvious and easily grasped, that the meanest
musical intelligence can detect at once whi< h end
his head is (so to speak)—a case widely different
from that of the hedgehog Brahms, or of the Skye-
terrier Schumann. This surface lucidity is his
misfortune. It insures his being taken au pied de
la lettre, and that is fatal: for the curious mocking
arribre pensee which it is the sacred duty of his
interpreter to find in him. whether it be there or
not, is the crown of Chopin. Personally, I think
it is not there. For his seemingly too luscious
sentiment is never quite overripe, never malici-
ously sweetened ; it always.takes itself seriously.
Wherefore fie requires an exponent with a sense
of humour, one who'can add the touch of malice,
which is indispensable to his feminine■ charm,
inasmuch as it puts a little sharp edge on his
sweetness, and makes him “ sweet as ci.vet ” ; one
who can mingle laughter with tears for yesterday’s
jam, and modify the conclusion of the whole
matter—So far from having lost my illusions,
illusions are all that remain to me.
IsRAt'EL, in The Dome.

As we were recently on the track of similes,
here are some from a recent book by B ;rnard
Capes, an English stylist. The excellent criticism
is by the Academy writer whose article on “ New
Books ” was reprinted Dec. 23.
The close air prickled with a multitudinous platter of
voices like blisters of fat breaking on a grill.
The immediate fall of temperature took him by the
throat like a shower bath. 1
She was skimming cream from a lire ad pari with her
fingers. Tie tips of these -budded through ifie white,
like nibs,of rhubarb through melting snow.
[This simile is absolutely bad. It is like producing a
rabbit from a hat. It is no office of a simile to s turtle, or
to exhibit a writer's cleverness, but graciously to interpret
one idea by another. And mere resemblance between two
remote things is no test of che aptness of the simile in
which these things may he suddenly allied by a literary
athlete. In fact the simile whioh springs from unusual
information in the author, and presumes it in the reader,
should be charily used. It would he quite wrong to say
that it must be never used. To forbid a writer to trawl
the deeps of spe%ch for new locutions would be absurd.
Only—he must use tact. When Edward Murk leaves the
social kitchen of the Landlust Inn at Merieourt and re-
tires to his bed we are told :
In the distance the voices of his late companions
droned like hornets in a bottle.
Now this simile is better. Its unexpectedness is not too
acute,, its call on the reader’s knowledge is not exorbi-
tant, and to the imagination it is distinctly apt aud.satls-
factory. Again, when Th6roigne tells Nieette that Murk
has left the village for Paris and will not return until the
spring, the poor girl conies round to her “ with a face
like hard ivory.” This simile does its work without fuss ;
it is brief, natural, effective. It is the antithesis of one *
in which Mr. C ipes describes the attitude of a waitress
who is rebuked for her peytness :
The girl stood as solid on end as a pocket of hops.
Here we are back to parlour manic.]
Our young writers are recommended to learn this
passage by heart.

I loiter round my ’cresses.

A Dumas story. After his son’s great success
with “La Dame aux Camlias,” Alexandre
Dumas wrote to him as to a stranger, congratu-
lating him on the book apd expressing a desire to
make the author’s acquaintance. “ 1 myself am a
literary man,” said he, in conclusion, “and you
may nave heard my name as the author of
‘Monte Cristo.’” Dumas fils was equal to the
occasion. He wrote immediately in reply, ex-
pressing the great pleasure he would have in
making his correspondent’s acquaintance, princi-
pally on account of the high terms in which he
had always heard his father speak of the author
of “ Monte Cristo.”
Other erses.
THE UNREALISED IDEAL .
MY only Love is always near,
In country or in town :
I see her twinkling feet, I hear
The whisper of her gown.
She foots it ever fair and young,
Her locks are tied in haste,
And one is o’er her shoulder flung
And hangs below her waist.
She ran before me in the meads,
And down this world-worn track
She leads me on ; but while she leads
She never gazes back.
And yet her voice is in my dreams,
To witch me more and more :
That wooing voice ! Ah me, it seems
Less near me than of yore.
Lightly I sped when hope was high
And youth beguiled the chase,
follow, follow still; but I
Shall never see her face.
Frederick Locker.
SHE CAME AND WENT.
As a twig trembles which a bird
Lights on to sing, then leaves unbent,
So is my memory'thrill’d and stirr’d :
I only know She came and went.
As clasps some lake, by gusts unriven, l
The blue dome’s measureless content,
So my soul held that moment’s heaven:
I only know She oame and went.
As at one bound our swift Spring heaps
The orchards' full of blooni and scent,
So clove her May my wintry sleeps :
I only know She came and went.
An angel stood and met my gaze
Through the low doorway of my tent, —
The tent is.struck, the vision stays:
I only know She came and went.
O, when the room grows slowly dim,
And life’s last oil is nearly spent,
One gush of light these eyes will brim,
Only to think Slue came and went.
James Russell Lowell.
THE LAST SCENE.
Here she lieth, white and chill :
Put your hand upon her brow !
Her sad heart is very still.
And she does not know you now.
Ah 1 the grave’s a quiet bed :
‘She will sleep a pleasant sleen,
And the tears that you' may shed
Will not wake her, —therefore weep !
Weep ! for you have wrought her woe;
Mourn ! she mourn’d and died for you :
Ah ! too late we come to know
What is false and what is true.
William Winter.
SHE WAS NOT FAIR.
She was not fair, nor full of grace,
Nor crowned with thought or aught beside,
No wealth had she of mind or face,
To win our love or raise our pride ;
No lover’s thought her cheek did touch,
No poet’s dream was round her thrown r
And yet we miss her,—ah ! too much,
Now she hath flown.
We miss her when the morning calls,
As one that mingled in our mirth ;
We miss her when the evening falls, —
A trifle wanted on the earth ;
Some fanoy small, or subtle thought
Is check’d ere to its blossom grown,
Some chain is broken that we wrought,—
Now she hath flown.
No solid good non hope defined
Is marr’d now she hath sunk in night;
And yet the strong immortal Mind
Is stopp’d in its triumphant flight.
Stern friend ! what power is in a tear,
What strength in one poor thought alone,
When all we know is—She was here
And She hath flown !
Bryan Waller Procter.
DIRGE.
What shall we do now, Mary being dead,
Or say, or write, that shall express the half?
What can we do but pillow that fair head,
And let the Spring-time write her epitaph
As it will soon, in snow drop, violet.
Wind-flower, and columbine, and maiden’s tea*:
Each letter of that pretty alphabet
That spells in flowers'the pageant of the year.
She was a maiden for a man to love,
She was a woman for a husband’s life,
One that had learn’d to value far above
The name of Love the saered name of Wife.
Her little life-dream, rounded so with sleep,
Had all there is of life—except grey hairs :
Hope, love, trust, passion, and devotion deep,,
And that mysterious tie a Mother bears.
She hath fulfill’d her promise and hath pass’d.
Set her down gently at the iron door !
Eyes ! look on that loved image for the last:
Now cover it in earth—her earth no more!
Thomas William Parsons.

WHAT THE DOCTORS SAY
“ Woolloston House, Castlemaine, Nov. 30, 1897. My
dear Mr. Malyon,—l have used your ointment Ligmolliene
with distinct success in my own case, but with greater as
an emollient unguent for the face of a young lady suffer-
ing from ‘ Acne,’ very common during puberty, and you
should push it in that direction. Mr. Best, solicitor,
speaks most highly of it in his own bad case of Eczema.
Kindly send me another box of it.—Yours truly, GEO. D.
WOOLLEY, M.D.”
What the Hon. W. H. EMBLING, M.D., M.L.O.,says
“Melbourne, May 27, 1899. Mr. W. H. Malyon.—Dear

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The Elopement.
The desert winds are blowing,
The desert sand’s afloat,
And comes a plaintive lowing
From dead-strewn lands remote ;
This barren waste around us,
Those granite hills so gray,
Shall now no longer bound us,
Our pathway trends away—
Beyond the long dark ranges
The glinting north stars lit,
The haze-world that so strange is
The nectar nymphs have quit;
T’where twilight world’s a-quiver
With willow wands on high,
We’ll cross the distant river,
My own true love and I!
Our faces to the south’ard,
What have we now to rue,
When western plains are smothered
Far from the lone Paroo ?
Look up, my blue-eyed beauty,
Look forward glad and free !
If not the voice of Duty,
’Tis love that calleth thee.
If some to west may wande 1 ,
And look with frowning brow,
With pleasure we may ponder,
Those bonds are broken now ;
And if the world deride us,
What’s all the world to this ?
E’en kith and kin may chide us,
These lips are ours to kiss !
Then hey for fountains flowing !
And ho for rivers wide !
Where briny winds are blowing
Across to Sydney side ;
T’ where autumn skies are clearest
We’ll cross the border line,
Tour hand in my hand, dearest,
Your sweet lips pressed to mine !
We’ve plighted, Love, for ever;
We’ve pledged in ruby wine ;
And nought but Death will sever
The bond that made you mine !
So if I be a rover
Thou'lt roam with me for aye,
And still be my true lover
When all is turning gray.
N.S.W.
EDWRD S. SORENSON.
Spring.
In the third season of the year
The weakling calves die fast;
Their luck withstood all previous shocks,
But Spring’s shock is their last.
The farmers all begin to boast
What harvests each will reap ;
The squatters call the shearers round
To tomahawk their sheep.
But soon the caterpillars swarm
Grasshoppers eat the feed ;
Farmers and.squatters, impotent,
Heave curses at the breed.
No rain descends ! The insect pest
Grows fat and multiplies.
The sad-eyed agriculturists
Gaze hopeless at the skies.
Grass, herbage, crops, now withered up—
Hope,cherished disappears.
The squatter finds he cannot sell
His wethers “off the shears.”
The “ mortgagee ” and “crop lien ” birds
To crops and lands fast cling ;
Though neither ever sang before,
Both warble in the Spring.
N.S.W.
E.D.S.
The Note that is Never Despised.
y.
O, singer of songs that are solemn,
Why chant us a joy-killing lay ?
Leave dead bells to dead men to toll ’em,
And grief to a gloomier day.
Strike notes that are living and true, man,
And themes that are simple and grand ;
If the key-note be only a woman,
A chorus is yours to command.
The rhythm of a sorrow may jingle
And linger awhile in our brains,
But love-laden numbers will tingle
The blood that is hot in our veins ;
So fashion your song old or new, man,
And trim with the magic ©f art—
If the key-note be only a woman
’Twill quicken humanity’s heart.
We don’t want the lay of the purist
In sentiments sampled and trite,
Nor the dull, loaded logic of jurist
In syllables solemn as night.
We long for a lay that is human,
Warm-blooded and wicked and bright,—
If the keynote be only a woman
We’ll catch up the tune with our might.
We’re thrilled by the throes of ambition
And moved by the moans of regret,
And touched by the tears of contrition
On faces grief-saddened and set,
Responsive to chords that are human,
Preferring the wild to the vvise ;
If the keynote be only a woman
We’ll chorus the song to the skies.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 6, 1900.
Our Australian Usurer.
A FEW days ago this paper happened on the
particulars of one of the ordinary cases of
usury which occur every day in every Aus-
tralian city. It was quite a usual case, and
there are hundreds and thousands like it, or
worse; still, the particulars are more or less
interesting. The borrower was a widow—the
usual kind of widow’, whose husband had
always attended to financial affairs, and who
knew about as much of business herself as any
other plain or colored female. She had
probably seen a cheque half-a-dozen times in
her life, and knew as much about the need for
a 2d. stamp on a receipt as Hannibal did.
She was left with a house worth £3OO and
some pressing debts, and she wanted to raise
£5O to pay the debts. Any building society
or insurance office would have lent the money
at 7 per cent, or less, but, like many other
widows, she didn’t know. She looked at the
advertisement of a loan-and-discount company
fiend, and w’ent to him. The alleged company,
by the way, consisted of one spidery individual,
so the lie and fraud started even before his office
was entered —it was w aiting outside on the door-
plate. He offered to lend the money at about
5 per cent., which looked reasonable enough.
So his client signed a vast confused document
which was utterly beyond her comprehension
and it panned out thus : £6O to be advanced in
cash less £lO deducted for interest in advance,
making the loan really £5O ; £36 to be charged
as a year’s interest on the £5O, and about £6
for costs of mortgage ; money , to be repaid by
12 monthly instalments of £3, and then, at the
end of the 12th month, the w’hole balance to fall
due. In other words, the borrower received
£5O on security worth £3OO ; and in return for
this she was to pay £36 by 12 monthly instal-
ments of £3, and after paying the £36 she
would still owe £56, including the “ costs of
mortgage,” which would be payable in a
lump. And if she couldn’t pay it either
the £3OO worth of property would be
seized, or else she would have to execute
another mortgage with probably £8 costs this
time, and agree to pay, perhaps, £5O for the
next year’s interest. And at the end of the
next year, and after paying 12 more monthly
instalments of £3, she would owe about £7B,
and it would be necessary to execute a third
mortgage, which would also cost a lot of
money in alleged law expenses, and start paying
larger monthly instalments to keep the interest
from swamping her altogether. In fact she
about sold herself, body and soul, for life in
return for £5O. And the unfortunate little
woman didn’t know, till it was fully explained
to her, that this wasn’t 5 per cent, or that she
had done anything unusual. She actually
believed that 12 instalments of £3 each would
pay off the whole £92, because the loan-and-
discount institution told her so, and she
thought that £36 was 5 per cent, interest on
£5O for the same reason.
* * * *
There are multitudes of people, mostly
women, who never reach the age of consent in
financial matters ; who never really know the
multiplication-table ; who hear about 5 per
cent., and are no more enlightened thereby
than if they heard of the plesiosaurus and the
differential calculus and the Fourth Dimension.
On questions of arithmetic about half the
population even of this enlightened community
ought to be in a lunatic asylum, though it can
be safely let out of the asylum on most other
matters. The Bulletin presumes, in default
of any better explanation, that they are that
way because Providence made them that way.
The theory of those who object to rigid anti-
usury laws is that every adult person who
doesn’t go about with visible straws in his hair
ought to be capable of self-preservation in
financial transactions, and if he makes a ridicu-
lously bad bargain he ought to take the
consequences. And by the same lucid method
of reasoning adulteration laws and laws
against the sale of diseased flesh should
be abolished ; the person who is foolish enough
to buy poisonous liquor and cancered meat
should stand the consequences. Also the laws
against murder should be wiped off the Statute-
book ; the person who is so utterly dull and
stupid as to let himself be killed deserves to be
dead. As for laws against embezzlement,
plain theft, and fraud of any kind, they are
obviously absurd. Anybody who is so un-
speakable a fool as to let himself be defrauded,
or to keep his money where it can be stolen,
calls for np sympathy when he loses it.
• * * •
The Bulletin believes in rigid anti-usury
laws for exactly the same reason which makes
it believe in laws against adulteration, the sale
of fatal meat, murder, embezzlement, assault,
plain robbery, and all other crimes. It could
never see how any person’s inability to defend
himself is a reason why the law' should refuse
to defend him. The law has already gone
back utterly on its own plea of individual re-
sponsibility. The ignorant woman who signs a
document making herself the monetary slave of
a money-lender because she doesn’t know' any
better, would be quite as likely, for the same
reason, to sign a paper making herself his per-
sonal slave and chattel, after the good old
fashion When human beings were saleable pro-
perty. Yet, in the first case, the law doesn’t
protect her against her own ignorance and the
swindlers who trade thereon ; it simply says
that she shouldn’t be ignorant, and she must
take the consequences. In the second case it
certainly would go back on its own doctrine of
personal responsibility, and would say that she
must be protected against the evil results of her
ignorance, and that she must not take the con-
sequences. The law hasn’t the courage to fully
accept its own doctrine. In cases of usury it
holds that every person must scramble for him-
self or herself ; that it is nobody’s business—
not even the law’s —to protect anybody else ;
that if the strong get the better of the weak then
it is the fault of the weak—they shouldn’t be
weak, and they deserve no sympathy. And its
practice in almost every other matter is a direct
reversal of this theory.
* * * *
The Federal Constitution provides that mat-
ters relating to bills of exchange, promissory
notes, banking, and trading and financial cor-
porations come within the scope of the Federal
Parliament. If sufficient strain can be put on
these clauses to make them cover anti-usury
legislation then this paper lives in the glad
belief that it may shortly see this matter dealt
with by Federal law'. It puts its trust in the
Federal Parliament because that institution is
built on a democratic basis, and because the
Fat element in the State Parliaments is too
strong. It hopes to see it enacted that no inter-
est shall exceed (say) 10 per cent, on the money
actually advanced, any attempt to charge
more than that, either by way of interest,
bonus, expenses, or under any other pretence
whatever, to be punishable by forfeiture of
both interest and principal —the interest to be
wiped out altogether, while half the principal
goes to the State and the other half to the
informer. The money-lender is one of the
great army of non-producers, and, as every
non-producer must live on the producer in
some shape or form, his extinction will make
life a shade easier for the actual worker. The
term producer is certainly a very w'ide one. It
covers everybody' who supplies the community
with anything to eat, drink, wear, or live in,
with mental food of any kind, with rational
amusement, with information, with aid in
sickness or with anj means of averting sick-
ness ; this paper would hardly even say
definitely that it doesn’t cover the black-
clothed person who produces a more or less
delusive hope about a hereafter. But it doesn’t
include the burglar, pickpocket, defaulting'
trustee, forgerer or usurer. No amount of'';
straining can spread the term over them.
Barking up the Wrong Tree.
Mr. A. Brittlebank, a well-known Aus-
tralian, formerly of Lane’s Paraguayan colony,
writes to The Bulletin from Bloemfontein,
Orange Free State :
It was with amazement that I read of the workers of
Australia volunteering to invade the South African Re-
public, where democracy is just striving to rear its head
in defiance of the threats of the money-grubbing and
land-grabbing aristocracy which now directs the destinies
of the British nation. Shame on ihe Australian people
in making an exhibition of their mean sycophancy and
contemptible toadyism to jingo Imperialism ! The demo-
crats of Europe and America will think that Australian
working-men are still wearing swaddling-clothes if they
are so easily led to believe in the humbug of a hireling
press and the Brummagem cheap-jack clap-trap served
up to a gullible public at £3OOO a year by mercenary edi-
tors hot out of the oven of British conservatism. Such
are the men who have been employed by the capitalists
of Johannesburg to create a crisis by constitutional
means, after their buccaneering raid had miserably failed
in its object.
The Jameson piratical attempt to seize the Transvaal
had in it the element of courage, and although it happily
failed, it was to some extent admirable because of the
pluck of the unscrupulous thieves, many of whom wore
the Queen’s uniform.
The present manufacturers’ crisis is devoid even of that
quality which gave tone to the previous attempt to steal
the goldfields of the Transvaal. The capitalist clique
which has amassed millions out of the soul of Africa can
only be likened to cormorants who, having eaten the gar-
bage, want to devour the platter.
The history of British rule in Africa is replete with
instances of broken conventions and fraudulent transac-
tions. The British annexed the Orange Free State, and
then abandoned it and declared it independent, because
they thought it not worth keeping on account of the cost.
When retiring from it they covenanted to supply the
Boers with gunpowder to light the Basutos, but the pro-
mise was never kept.
When the diamond-fields were discovered to be in the
Orange Free State’s territory the British authorities
promptly seized the land. Then they acknowledged that
the Diamond Fields were Free State territory by offering
£90,000 in payment for the stolen property —£90,000 for
a district which has yielded about £4,000,000 a year for
the last twenty years.
* * * •
The Boer farmers trekked north of the Vaal and
founded the South African Republic, which was also
taken from them but again given back by the British for
the same reason—it was not at that time thqught worth
holding. Unhappily gold was found on the Witwaters-
rand, and forthwith the land-sharks and adventurers,
diamond-buyers and unconvicted thieves, invaded the
district and started eompany-mongering on a gigantic
scale. They floated companies for hundreds of thousands
of pounds share capital, but the hundreds of thousands
of pounds were mostly swallowed up in promoters’ shares
and first robbers’profits. The ultimate results exceeded
the wildest dreams of the promoters and a city sprang up
which now rivals Australian capitals in grandeur and
magnitude. The thieves’ market or Stock Exchange
surpasses anything in Australia. But notwithstanding
the fact that the most liberal gold-law in the world
obtains, and that the customs duties are less than in the
Cape Colony, that many licenses are granted for the
nominal sum of ten shillings per month and that the size
of the claims is larger than in Australia by 150 feet along
the reef and 400 feet wide, and that the average yield is
almost constant, still these easy conditions are not
enough to satisfy the rapacity of the vultures who
exploit the mines. The strangers were welcomed to the
Boer domicile and now they want co steal the house.
Anyone two degrees removed from a fool knows quite
well that the franchise question is only a stalking-horse
in the main. Lust for political power and supremacy has
accompanied the gain of vast fortunes. The real grievance
is the rapid growth of Republican principles as repre-
sented by the Africander Bond. The Rhodes party is
disgusted because it has not political dominion over the
richest gold-spot on earth, and the power of might over
right is again endeavoring to assert itself by every device
which chicanery can invent and capital purchase. The
arch-enemies of democracy are all behind the scenes.
They own and control the press, with few exceptions.
They send lying telegrams all over the world about the
tyranny of the Transvaal Government. Malicious sophis-
tical statements have been imported into the leading
articles of the press till people at a distance actually
believe that the inhabitants of Johannesburg are living
under a reign of terror, and that life is not worth living
in the Golden City, whereas the place is as safe as any
town in Australia.
• * * *
The South African Republic is not perfect; it is too
easy. The gold-mining regulations are far too favorable
to the companies. There are practically no regulations
to safeguard the lives of the miners. There is not one
fearless-enough Government-inspector to see that the
few mining regulations are carried out. There are no
laws relating to ventilation or sanitary arrangements in
the mines, and there is an entire disregard on the part of
the companies to establish anything like hygienic condi-
tions. Everything is subservient to record-dividends;
the worship of mammon gives place to every other con-
sideration. Notwithstanding 50 per cent, dividends,
many of the gold-mining camps neglect even to provide
bathrooms for their employes, and these men when they
come up from the mines, begrimed with sweat
and stonedust from the rock-drills, have to wash
their bodies in hand-basins. On man3' of the outcrop
mines there are no sanitary arrangements whatever, and
the pestilential state of things below, where from 600 to
700 are employed from 7 a.m. till 4 or 5 p.m., can better
be imagined than described. Still worse, the mine water
is often contaminated with sewage and is used for drink-
ing purposes and culinary necessities when other sup-
plies fail, the result being disastrous outbreaks of
typhoid fever amongst both white and black employes.
It would be tedious to enumerate all the squalid condi-
tions which have grown out of the insensate greed of the
millionaire capitalists, such as the prohibition of football
and other sports on the ground that such exercises ex-
haust the energy of the men and take too much out of
these human machines and unfit them for the next day’s
work. But the Transvaal Government did not make
these conditions. They arose out of the greed of
the crowd which professes to be striving to secure liberty
(of all things in the world) for the British inhabitants.
The Government’s fault lay in the fact that it tolerated
these things in its anxiety to give no further handle to
the sharks who live on it and conspire against it, and in
its dread of the British Government which backs them up.
The celebrated petition of 21,000 signatures cost several
hundred pounds. The alleged spontaneous meetings
were simply organised attempts to set the people against
the Government, and were engineered and paid for by
the capitalists who wired misleading accounts to the ends
of the earth. They worked the cry of the starving poor
of Johannesburg for over two months and dropped it as
soon as it had served its purpose. Then a more astute
and accomplished literary prostitute was imported from
England to carry on the agitation till it reached a point
where the British Government could, with some show of
reason, protest.
• * » *
What are Australian working men thinking about
when they volunteer to abet the blood-guilty schemes of
a Tory Government in its designs on a democratic Repub-
lic which is doing its best to uphold the very principles
which liberty-loving Australian workmen are striving to
establish in Australia? I feel sure these militant, mis-
guided men would bow their heads in shame if they knew
the true aspects of the case, and it is to their eternal dis-
grace that they went into this war without first learning
what these aspects are. I should think Australians
would rather admire the magnificent patriotism animat-
ing the Dutch burghers who have left their farms and
families at the clarion-call to duty to defend with their
lives the integrity of their native land. These men are
of no common order. In their veins runs the blood of
the Huguenots and the old Sea Kings, men of our own
religion, God-fearing men who only want to be left alone
to work out their own destiny in thejr own way. They
are brave men who elect to pit themselves against the
trained legions of mighty England rather than admit the
interference of a foreign power in their own internal
policy. Such men may be exterminated, but they will
never be subdued. They will tight to the death as the
Australians would fight for liberty against the machina-
tions of capital over the rights of the masses, and what-
ever may be the outcome these patriots will be sure to
render a good account of themselves in the hour of trial.
Very few Australians realise the dire distress which the
crisis has produced amongst the English working popu-
lation of which Johannesburg and district is mainly
composed—the sacrifice of their worldly goods—the con-
sternation of women and children who have delayed their
departure to the last in the vain hope that hostilities
niiuht be averted —the mad rush to secure room in
cattle-trucks none too cleanly and open to the vicissi-
tudes of the weather (severe thunderstorms prevail at
this season of the year, and hundreds of the unhappy
refugees have had to stand in this sub-tropical downpour
of rain with crying children, wet to the skin, hungry
and miserable, during a 200-mile journey into British
territory). Such scenes of woe would bring remorse into
the breasts of all honorable men. When these poor
wretches reach their destination, penniless and starving,
their utter hopelessness becomes still more apparent.
Trade has been disorganised all over South Africa, there
is no prospect whatever of employment for the bread-
winners—the victims of capitalistic intrigue. The general
exodus has thrown everything out of gear. House-rents
in the coast-towns have gone up to £l2 per month for
three rooms, payable in advance. Yet in the midst of this
desolation and misery the capitalists who have brought it
all on are hanging on to their money-bags aqd the burden
of feeding these thousands of starving wretches falls on
the municipalities. These cruel wrongs have been in-
flicted on a helpless people to satisfy the ambition and
vainglorious thirst for supremacy by a handful of irre-
sponsible millionaires who have long since conveyed their
vile carcases out of the range of bullets.
A remarkable feature about this wretched franchise
question is the fact that if the same conditions were en-
forced in the Transvaal as obtain amongst the nomadic
shearing population of the Australias and the working
class in England regarding the residential qualification,
very few of the Uitlander workers could exercise the fran-
chise when granted, for the reason that they are subject
to twenty-four hours’ notice and are continually
changing their residence. And if the educational test
applied in the British colony of Natal were applied in the
Transvaal five-sixths of the Outlanders would never have
been admitted to the country at all.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Bogus “Atrocity.”
Now that the war correspondents of the Aus-
tralian papers have arrived at the Cape, and
information which is not officially faked by tlie
official censor begins to filter through, it be-
comes increasingly evident what a sinful liar
and slanderer that censor is. Something of
this, indeed, has already leaked out. Up to
date almost every alleged Boer atrocity which
came through by censored cable, and was pub-
lished amid a yell of execration in the most
conspicuous part of the daily papers, with a
heading in letters about as large as the editor,
has been flatly contradicted afterwards by mail
—in very small type with no glaring headlines.
Not one person saw the contradiction for 1000
who saw the original lie, but still the daily
papers, by reason of the fact that they did
publish the contradiction as soon as it arrived,
are able to claim that they are maintaining an
unbiassed attitude over the matter. The fact
that one attitude is nearly a foot long and the
other about half-an-inch is a mere side ques-
tion. But now the unpleasant contradictions
are coming in faster and with more emphasis.
Last Wednesday, for instance, Sydney Evening
News correspondent sent this along, and it was
printed in small letters, with no great blobs of
ink on top to draw public attention :
A few of our wounded were left on the scene of the en-
gagement, and were treated well by the Dutch, who,
when our ambulance arrived, apologised for their crude
arrangements, asserting that they were not so well pro-
vided for the comfort of the wounded as the British.
Indeed, so far as I have been able to learn, the Boers have
generally respected the Red Cross, and when they have
fired on the ambulance it has been because some of our
unwounded officers have taken refuge there ( !) About this
you will probably hear more anon, as I understand a
court-martial will be held.
And this, again, is “ Banjo ” Paterson, in the
S.M. Herald of last Thursday :
All the talk about Boers being savages is nonsense
They treat the wounded well. I saw a man to-day
who had four bullet wounds, and he had nothing
but good to say of the Boers. They assisted him
in every way they could. Here the ultimate success
of the English is looked upon as assured ; but there is a
deep political question underlying it all. The Cape
Ministry is looked upon as pro-Boer, and the British
organs call them all sorts of names. But the fact re-
mains that they hold office by a vote of a majority of the
local House. There are more locals against the war than
for it, and the extremist papers on the English side here
are urging that after the war the franchise should be
taken from the pro-Boer party. This seems strange in
view of the fact that the war itself is undertaken solely
to get the franchise for the Uitlanders. . . . We think of
the Boers as semi-savages. We have plenty people just
as rough as they are. We have 150 Boer prisoners here,
and 30 more coming in. They are square sturdy men,
much the type of our bushmen.
These writers, be it remembered, are repre-
senting papers which are strongly anti-Boer,
and they certainly were not sent out to write
up the campaign from the Boer point of view.
But their contradictions arrive slowly by
mail ; the lying atrocity gets here by cable
and has a long start. “Banjo” Paterson’s
letter draws attention to another remarkable
point. To the people here who have always
been led to believe that Britain and Australia
are fighting in the noble cause of free suffrage
and equal Parliamentary representation, it
sounds rather curious to hear that the strongly
British party at the Cape is demanding the
disfranchisement of the Dutch population—
somewhere about half the community. Is
Australia fighting to enfranchise the British
alien in the Transvaal (who, as an alien, has no
legal claim whatever to the franchise), or to
disfranchise the Dutch citizen at the Cape
(who, as a British citizen, has a fair claim to
the franchise) ? Or what is Australia fighting
about ?
♦
The Alleged “Loyalty” of Australia.
Canada, or part of it, appears to be really a
“ loyal ” dependency of Britain ; and the
“enthusiasm” there is fairly genuine. Aus-
tralian loyalty doesn’t amount to a row of pins.
In spite of the British yell for help, the ser-
vility of Australian Parliaments, and the bounc-
ing pay offered, there has been practically no
response to “ the Empire’s call.” City news-
papers may shriek about that “Empire,” and
city crowds line the streets to watch contingents
go ; but if you declare a public holiday to look
at a three-legged calf, and advertise the
calf as well as the contingents were ad-
vertised, and export the calf with flags
waving and bands playing, you w’ould
get just as big a crowd to watch it go. The
two or three thousand men gone and going
from Australia to shoot Boers would go just as
readily to shoot parrots, or Paraguayans, or
polar hears, if they had the same amount of
Govt, and newspaper backing, and the same
pay, and the same chance of sport. In every
community there are hundreds of young
bloods, with a happy ignorance of responsi-
bility, ready to go anywhere and shoot any-
thing for the fun of the thing. But the city
crowds risk nothing; the newspaper writers risk


nothing; and the Jingo politicians risk nothing—
not even their cash. They dig joyous hands in
the public Treasury, and blow the expense.
They won’t have Lo pay. The cost of the con-
tingents must, in the long run, come out of the
producers—the men on the land ; and, when
the bill is presented, it will take Australia’s
breath away. Before even the comparative few
who have already gone to the Transvaal get
back, the chances are they will cost this country
not less than .£500,000 —and maybe more.
What could Australia do with £500,000, of
lasting benefit to Australia? How many
interior roads and bridges could be made?
how many prospectors could be aided?—how
many pioneer farmers tided over critical
seasons ? Is it any wonder that, as a whole,
the sentiment of the Bush is strongly against
fhe war, and against the shameful part uncon-
cerned Australia has taken in it? They talk
of sending away a bush contingent. Doubtless
they will get it, of a sort, if the frolic is pro-
mising enough and the pay good enough. That
does not alter the face that the bulk of Aus-
tralian bushmen, ready enough to fight in
defence of Australia, would know themselves
disgraced if they permitted themselves to be
exported to shoot Boers or any other race fight-
ing for their own country —their Australia—
against syndicates and politicians.
Servant-G-alism.
Though Maoriland has successfully tackled
many long-standing big social problems, the
servant-girl difficulty is as bad there as any-
where else. Even Seddoh hasn’t found a
remedy for that, nor seriously attempted one.
The situation is about this : Given the fact
that, for some inscrutable reason, a girl com-
mits social suicide by becoming a servant;
that she loses the ordinary right which belongs
even to the dust-cart man of having a courtesy
title before her name, and becomes plain Mary ;
that she is the butt of the comic papers, and
the subject of the familiarities of the butcher’s
hoy ; that in the mind of the average man in a
good position she ranks about with the
aboriginal, so far as matrimonial purposes are
concerned, and that socially she is on a level
with the dog and the cat, inasmuch
as they all have their habitual resi-
’ ® nce ’I 1 the kitchen considering all
hese things, why are servants so beastly
scarce? A number of people have lately
een holding forth on the old stock grievance,
a , nd , k' )e Maoriland papers have risen up and
s led much gloom on it to no purpose. The
‘‘fu*" answer appears to be that as long as
. ’am slavey ” has to herd with cockroaches
’n the kitchen during her hours of ease, she
wi be scarce. No girl has a natural craving
or cockroaches. Maoriland should be able to
na a remedy if any place can. Women there
plentiful, and better educated than the
veiage. If the State would start a university
-servants, and turn out a new class of slavey
1 i a B. A. degree—the kind t hat would be an
rnament to a Dignified family, and that
no one would expect to flock with beetles
in the kitchen in her spare time, it might
be the beginning of better things. Govern-
esses are plentiful enough, but if the go-
verness was required to share the kitchen
with the beetles, and was always called
Jane, she would probably be quite as
scarce as the slavey. Maoriland did a big
thing when it practically solved the questions,
of strikes and Old-Age Pensions. If it can go
one better and solve the servant-girl problem it
will be famous all the world over. When it
invents a new kind of slavey, and barristers
and merchants and squatters get into the habit
of marrying the desirable new slavey by way of
showing that the social ban has been lifted,
then the trouble should be nearing a solution.

Down Went McG-inty.
The Law in the person of Judge a’Beckett
got into a state of quite unnecessary excitement
in Melbourne lately, and vindicated its majority
in what it probably imagined was a very
vigorous fashion ; as a consequence of which
one McGinty went down—for five years.
McGinty aforesaid was the husband of a local
hotel licensee, who had been charged with
common or side-door Sunday trading. During
the hearing of this case the husband and three
young men—apparently customers—made cer-
tain statements in denial of the alleged Sunday
trading, and these led to their being charged
with, and ultimately convicted of, perjury.
Iwo of the alleged prevaricators were strongly
recommended to mercy ; as was also McGinty
himself, lhen the Majesty of the Law arose
in all its terrors, ordered the three so-
called accomplices six months’ hard labor
apiece, and paralysed the unfortunate
McGinty by sending him to gaol for five years !
McGinty and the other three were witnesses
for the defence, and the only reason why thev
were convicted of perjury was that they denied
the Sunday trading, whereas the jury found
that it really existed. Of course when one
party to a case flatly asserts a thing and
another flatly denies it, one of them is com-
mitting perjury, and when the jury decides
one way it thereby definitely finds that the
people who swore the other way are perjurers.
And the Victorian law has a perfect right to
deal with them as perjurers, if it only dealt
with them all alike. But it doesn’t. It is
always the witnesses for the defence who are
prosecuted for perjury; never those for the
Grown. Almost every day, in licensing and
other cases, magistrates and juries refuse to
accept the sworn statements of policemen and
other Crown witnesses, yet no one ever
hears of these presumed perjurers being tried.
Therefore it appears that the Victorian Crown
Law Department holds that perjury committed
to obtain the conviction of an innocent man is
lawful, and perjury committed to procure the
acquittal of a guilty one is a hideous crime.
And for these reasons Victorian justice is a
scandal and a disgrace, and a thing that would
stink in the nostrils of every decent man, if the
average decent man had enough intelligence to
recognise a moral stench when he met it.
More Coupon.
“ Cockney ” again-speaks on the coupon ques-
tion :
My reference to the petty provincialism of Parliament-
ary meddling with the coupon system appears to have
perturbed the small minds of sundry small shopkeepers
one w^lorn tells you—as though he were imparting
valuable information—that “ the ultimate result of the
system must be that many other coupon companies will
spring up, and every shopkeeper will ultimately be com-
pelled to give coupons, which simply means that the
customers and the employes will both be sweated to keep
another horde of unprofitable middlemen.” Perhaps it
isn t worth while to reply to a small shopkeeper who has
the impudence to object to “unprofitable middlemen ”
but. here goes. The coupon system is a mere passing
incident in the cut-throat competition that goes on for
ever among the little traders in every little street. If it
is a popular system with some customers then it
must encourage cash trading, seeing that coupons
are only obtainable for cash. The germ of long life is not
in the S3 7 stem. It is born to die and be born again in a
new form—just like its mortal enemies. But if, whilst it
lives, it tends to strengthen some shops and kill others
off, it is indeed a blessed system. Small shops with
small shopkeepers in carpet slippers waiting in the back
pailor for customers, as spiders wait for flies, represent
an awful loss of useful labor to this young country. Any
temporary means of diminishing the number of unprofit-
able middlemen must make for “ the development of our
vast natural resources.” There is something to be said in
favor of aged female shopkeepers, of course, but even
among aged females there is cut-throat competition, and
it doesn t matter a straw whether the woman across the
street is ruined by Mrs. Jones’s coupon system or by the
superior ‘ window dressing’ or other business tactics of
that more brainy trader.”
Rising in Judgment.
Even a Government’s best doings rise up in
judgment against it when election time comes.
Probably among all good deeds of the Seddon
Ministry the one that saved the country most
in immediate cash was its rescue of the” Bank
of New Zealand. This, from Auckland
Observer, is a fair sample of how that act
looked when last general election was on :
But the deed has been done, and to-day the taxpayer
is paying the piper. Wherein lies the advantage to the
colony? We utterly fail to see it. Better, far better,
had the State held aloof and allowed the Bank of New
Zealand to pay the full penalty of a long course of riotous
living by perishing ignobly along with its co-partner in
dissipation, the Colonial Bank. Whom did the State
succour and befriend? Certainly not the poor confiding
dupes of shareholders who invested their capital in the
Bank of New Zealand, for they have been ground to the
dust and stripped of all they had. It was the directors,
to whom they confided their interests, and who ran the
institution mainly, if not wholly, for their own
personal aggrandisement. These were the people whom
the State put its revenue in pawn to raise upon their
feet, and whose burdens the taxpayers of Maoriland
now stagger under. What did the Bank of New Zealand
do for the colony to deserve such generous, self-sacrific-
ing treatment : It lived like a vampire, battening for
years upon the blood of the people. It levied a species
of blackmail upon industrial enterprise. It played the
role of the usurious shent-per-sbenter. And it gambled
wildly with the moneys it was trusted to conserve.
Every trading concern felt, its blighting influence, and it
farmed the colony like a huge monopoly. Vast tracts of
land were looked up by it from settlement, industry was
throttled, progress was stayed, and the country lay stif-
fened under a thrall which nearly broke its heart. In-
stead of hurling this Old Man of the Sea from our shoul-
ders, the Government have made him a perpetual State
pensioner.
Of course the-B.N.Z. isn’t a State pensioner ;
it pays every cent of the interest on'the money
which the State advanced to it. And the old
directors weren’t put on their feet; they were
simply chucked out. And the taxpayers stagger
under no burden. But the amazing circum-
stance is the demand to know who was benefited
when the State practically took over the great
bank. There were about £9,000,000 of deposits
in the B.N.Z. when it so nearly closed its doors
in ’95. If the “ignoble” institution had been
allowed to go, about half the employers in
Maori land would have paid no wages next
Saturday because their ready cash was locked
up pending the long process of liquidation.
About one-fourth of them would probably have
gone broke through the locking up of their
ready money. About a fourth of the population
would have been thrown out of work ; in the
general distress property values would have
gone down some £20,000,000 to £40,000,000 ;
the 8.N.Z., forced to realise its properties in
the market which its stoppage had wrecked,
would probably have paid its depositors about
7s. fid. in the £; and it would have taken ten
years before the province looked up again out
of the ruin. That isn’t a fancy picture ; it is
what happened in Victoria because that pro-
vince, when the trouble came along, had
Patterson instead of Seddon. If Seddon
hadn’t rescued the B.N.Z. the same Tory press
which now curses him for doing the rescue
would have been demanding that he should be
hanged, drawn and fried as a man who meanly
sacrificed his country to his private animosities,
because some of the B.N.Z. directors were his
political opponents.
A Soul-Wail.
Moan of bitter grief from the anti-Federal
Brisbane Telegraph (11th December), under
the heading “ Our Last Independence Day”:—
Those persons who have sold Queensland to be governed
from Melbourne will rejoice with that particular sort of
siinperinsr jrladness which characterises idiots that this
may he the last anniversary kept in celebration of the
independence of Queensland. The period of that inde-
pendence has been forty years. Should our independence
terminate now, it will aid in o-ivinf; color to the supersti-
tion that there is something- fatal about the number
forty. We are told that once upon a time it rained for
forty days and forty nights. That was the time during
which Moses was in the mountain ; and for forty years
Israel was in the wilderness. We all have heard of the
forty years’ war ; and when the Crimean war broke out
serious persons accepted it as a matter of course because
just then there had been forty years’ peace. Queensland
has had forty years of independence; but should the union
of Australia take place within the year the independence
of Queensland will be a thing of the past. There are
persons who will he idiotic enough to believe that
although Queensland has wrought such wonders during
the past forty years because she was independent, she is
going to do immeasurably better during the next forty
years because she is to be put under conditions more
restrictive and humiliating than those from which she
was liberated forty years ago. It seems cruel to damp
their joy by reminding them, as we have so often done,
that the majority vote of Queensland against the pro-
posed union covered the southern and central districts,
which contain 83 per cent, of the white population of this
country.
The organ of many foolish parsons here meets
with its invariable ill-luck. It is evidently
thinking of the Thirty Years War—there is no
notable Forty Years War in history. Also it
was 39 —not 40—years between the Waterloo
cam pai gn and the Crimean War, and that 39 years
wasn’t 40 years of peace ; the period of alleged
peace was broken by three gory campaigns.
And there was no majority vote of Queensland
against the Federation Bill ; there was no
majority vote against it in the Central district,
there wasn’t even one in the Southern district
—there wasn’t oue anywhere outside poor little
paroehial-souled Brisbane. The spectacle of
the Brisbane Telegraph raising its gaunt and
bony lie above the waters of affliction, and
struggling to assure the public that the big
Federal majority of a few months ago is all an
optical delusion, is an extraordinary spectacle.
—+- ~
Instead of the Australian, who mostly has
never seen a gun fired in anger, going mad with
righteous indignation at the news of how the
coarse, unfeeling Boer fired on British hospi-
tals, it would be not amiss if he would resort
to a shaded spot, and take off all his clothes,
and sit on the flat side of a cool half water
melon, and put the other half on his head to
soothe his boiling intellect, and do a serious
think. No doubt the Boers have fired on
British hospitals. Almost certainly there is
equal truth in the Boer assertion” that the
British fired on their hospitals. Hospitals are
fired on in every war, by the most humane com-
manders. Nowadays towns are bombarded at
two or three or four or five miles range, and
when a small town is the target at that range,
the gunner is hardly responsible for what part
of it he hits. Often he doesn’t hit it at
all—then the Jingo papers say he is a
ridiculously bad marksman. Sometimes he
does hit it but strikes the hospital part of it—
then they imply he is such ariahsolutely infallible
marksman that he couldn’t possibly have done
it save by malice aforethought, premeditated
murder, and the deep, dark devilishness of his
had heart. The situation is that of a man about
Sydney G.P.O. with instructions to bombard
the little township of Coogee, and destroy it
all he can, but not on any account to hit one
particular cottage. As a case in point some
Sydney warriors lately bombarded the local
cricket ground, yet in their case it wasn’t set
down as a proof of any murderous designs on
the cricketers. There is no especial reason to
suppose the cable is lying when it alleges that
a British hospital was shelled but when it im-
plies that this is a proof of malice and
inhumanity, and that the circumstance is any-
thing unusual, it is lying—by implication at
least.
Ie Australia goes on as it is doing it will find
itself presently let in for international compli-
cations. At Broken Hill the spirit of loyalty
lias broken out in many dozen bottles, and the
people there, having a wild idea that a Boer is
exactly the same as a Dutchman, and that a
Dutchman is a German, tried to keep up the
unity of the empire by wrecking the German
club. They were staved off by the police, hub
the public fury has become so bad that local
Germans are trying to save themselves from
loyal and patriotic stoush by advertising in the
papers that they are not Boers, and don’t
sympathise with those people at all.
Apparently, the crowd which is so anxious
about freedom of speech and personal liberty
in the Transvaal doesn’t mind if these
iiriicl.es became a little scarce at, home:
Considering some previous events at Broken
Hill this episode opens up an interesting
point. It was at that same town that a pre-
vious Lyne Government had, Sleath and
Ferguson arrested on a charge of inciting
people to do damage to British persons and pro-
perty, and it was so anxious about the matter
that it took them all the way to Deniliquin in
order to ensure their conviction, and sent up
great legal persons to prosecute them, and got
them a long spell in gaol apiece. If the same
Lyne doesn’t take equally vigorous measures
now, the only possible inference is that the
attempt to do damage to German persons and
property doesn’t matter, or else that what was
very sinful when done in the interests of Aus-
tralian miners is quite commendable when done
in the interests of a mixed lot of capitalists in
Africa.
<>
Victorian Chief-Justice Madden spoke a large
slab of wisdom, at Ballarat Sessions, lately,
before ordering a youth, convicted of immorality
with a girl under 16, to be discharged, and to
so remain pending good behaviour. Said his
Honor: “The intention of the (Crimes) Act
was to prevent men of mature judgment taking
advantage of immature girls. But, personally,
I have only known of one case in which the
culprit was so old as 27 years, and the ages of
the majority ranged from 16 to 20 years.
In all the cases recently tried before me, the
girl invariably admitted having been guilty of
misrepresentation in regard to her age.' A
great many girls also admitted that they had
themselves sought and invited the action which
led to criminal proceedings. The experience of
the Courts is that the operation of the Act has
been productive of an enormous amount of
harm.” Which, coming from Judge Madden,
is a choice morsel for the W.C.T.U. and the
Y.W.C.A., and the rest of the keyhole-aud-
gimlet brigade to chew, for on them rests the
onus of procuring an Act which does much
harm and little, if any good, and which is
grossly unjust towards those boys who need
protection from seduction even more than their
alleged victims.

When the N.S. W. Council threw out an ini-
portant part of Premier Lyne’s financial pro-
posals on the last day of the session the
lengthy grey politician had a fine chance to
assert himself—and didn’t. He might have
told the Assembly in an awful voice that the
closing of the session was unavoidably post-
poned; then have hastily drawn up a new Bill to
tax something or other by way of filling the
gap which the Council’s action had caused, in
his finances, passed it through the Assembly
with a rush, and sent it up. And if the Coun-
cil rejected it his business was to tell the
Assembly in a still more awful voice that the
closing was postponed some more, and to bring
in another Tax Bill. But Lyne didn’t even
make a serious complaint, therefore the Council
now knows it can treat his tax proposals very
much as it pleases.

The difficulties which Justice Cohen finds sur-
rounding the awarding of costs in the late
Caulfield-Norton libel-action tried in N.S. W.
Supreme Court prove that the readiest and
most effective way to reach a solution is not by
establishing more confusing precedents, but by
an amendment of the law which will enable
juries to say whether costs are to be divided,
and how they are to be divided, or which party
shall pay for both. In many actions costs are
the main consideration, and on the face of it it
is futile to employ twelve special jurors to
determine the minor damages while one
Judge is left to determine the major costs.
In the case under notice the jury, after
protracted labor, found Norton should pay
Caulfield one bright farthing under the
style and title of damages, but did not
say, and were not empowered to say,
whether, under style and title of costs, Norton
Should not pay Caulfield several hundred
pounds besides, or Caulfield pay Norton
several hundreds or thousands. The matter
would not be so bad if Judges had a plain track
to go on in taking over the work where the
jury leaves off, but according to Judge Cohen
and many of his predecessors, the claim for costs
opens up a maze of distracting difficulties
“ C.M.G.” on patriotism, &c. :
I find even liberal-minded Englishmen (men who
despise the customary cant) with whom I have talked
war quite unable or unwilling to do justice to mv Aus-
tralian point of view. That Ido not “adulate” the
British and abominate the Boers is considered a reproach
to me because my parents are English. Any sentiment
of loyalty I possess is entirely Australian ; my interest in
England is nowise different from my interest in Germany
France, or America. It is, in fact, purely literary, never
personal ; and because of this my critics call me
traitor, just as they would call me infidel if
they were religious and I refused to enthuse
over their particular brand of Salvation. Within
me there are latent possibilities of an Australian
Jingoism, and I shrink from abusing the Englishman’s
Jingoism on that account, but I am called a hater of
England because I do not sing “Soldiers of the
Queen,” and throw up my hat in the streets—regard:
less of the fact that some dishonest person would
probably get it when it came down again. Assevera-
tions of my Australianism provoke incredulous contempt
—what is there to take hold of in this pitiful Australia
without history, age, or accomplishment? I answer there
is Australia, and the Englishman who scorns me for
loving a country that is young mocks at his own
ancestors who were true to England in her youth. Any-
how, Australia is as old to me as England is to any
Englishman, for she is older than I, and God he thanked
I do not consciously allow my great-grandmother to mould
my opinions for me. It may be better to be visited withi
the sins of the fathers than with their virtues. From in- :
herited patriotism in young Australians spring blood-'
lusting contingents to battle, not for Australia in
Australia, hut for England in Africa. There is much of '
egotism in the English patriotism one meets in Australia. 1
The Englishman, it appears to me, confounds amor
■patriae and amour propre, and the people who yelled
with laughter when Dickens heaped derision on America*
are for ever lusting to be “ cracked up.”
John Joyce, the lately-deceased member
for Lyttelton (Maoriland) started life as a police-
man, and worked up by his own efforts to be a
lawyer of some eminence in Christchurch. To
the end of his days he had the portly carriage and
deliberate stalk of the bobby looking’ for
passing drunk on bis beat. About ten years amo
Joyce made a great stonewalling speech of nine
hours—at that time the fame of Jawbone Neild’s
great effort was still fragrant, and the desire to
emulate it was strong. In that performance
Joyce apparently used up all the political remarks
he knew and all he was ever likely to know
Thereafter, he was a silent member with a flower
in his button-hole, who made interminable notes
in the House of Reps., and r—er used them for
any visible purpose.
The Losers.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At dead of night
In the English land,
A boy in affright
Touched his mother’s hand.
He cried “ O Mother ! ”
And the woman stirred :
“ Where is my brother ? ”
But she spake no word.
Her lips were set,
And her face was drawn ;
The pillow was wet
That her head lay on.
She knew—that mother
With her heart of woe—
The absent brother
Eell at red Glencoe.
“ Killed by a shell,”
Said the list. Ah me,
’Twas short news to tell!
But what sight saw she ?
A mangled body
In the ground concealed—
Some fragments bloody
Erom the battlefield.
Fruit of her womb,
Son that sucked her breast,
Was this then his doom—
This for him Life’s best ?
God’s will ? The Lord
Of the churches—He
With red, ruthless sword
Knows no clemency.
Fine words were spoken
Of the battles won :
Her heart was broken,
And her life was done.
A Boer woman,
In the far Transvaal,
Heard stern lips summon—•
In the homely Taal—
Her husband old
To arise and play
The man’s part bold
In the bitter fray.
A fierce glad glow
In his old eyes shone :
’Twas good luck to go
Where his sons had gone.
He rode forth, hiding,
All his homesick pain,
But homeward riding
Came he ne’er again.
And homeward never
Came his tall sons twain.
The sad mother ever
Weeps—and weeps in vain,
For they, the brave,
- Sleep beneath the sky
In that red grave
Where their kindred lie.
* * * •
Why keep the sham on ?
God has left his Throne,
And Moloch and Mammon
Are our gods alone.
V.J.D.
