Bijou Rickards’ Variety Co.
Pantomimes are very much alike except
some that are a little different by mistake and are
sorry for it afterwards. Bernard Espinasse’s
“ Little Red Riding Hood,” at Her Majesty’s, is
to a great extent a relapse into the ordinary ways
of Xmas spectacle. When the Firm staged
“Matsa ” a few years ago, it was a notable depar-
ture from the old ways and old customs ; “Matsa ”
wasn’t panto, at all—neither the ordinary kind
that is so much alike, or the unusual kind that
isn’t quite so usual as the other kind. But since
then the regulation panto, business has super-
vened once more. In the present case everything
is a blaze of loveliness and pomp so far as the
clothes and the paint are concerned. The weary
old Boer war is dragged in again, but then the
war is dragged into everything just now ; and the
array of troops who storm the golden fortress
where Kruger is supposed to be rolling on his ill-
gotten wealth is a very fine spectacle. Also the
graceful Carrie Moore, who takes her plump
and pleasing way in front of them, is a very
lovely officer in a neat brown uniform. The
donkey is a rather less successful ass than the
mule of last year’s “Forty Thieves”; still he
isn’t a bad kind of animal in his way. The ballet is
gorgeous, and must have cost as much as the price
of a small war. And—to go back to the first
point—the scenery is beyond admiration. The
Haunted Forest where the trees arise and curse
and dance with red eyes glaring through
the gloom like an insurrection of railway
signals, the Glow - worm Dell, the fortress
where Kruger is supposed to keep his sinful
and unjust loot, the palace in Act 111. and the
land of Arcadia, also, the big cloth with the
picture of Lake Derwentwater, are all matters of
surpassing loveliness. And the verbal part of the
pantomime is—well, it is.
* • » •
The writer has come to the conclusion that
it is no good kicking against the stereotyped
character of pantomime. There is nothing in
barking at Providence, or in trying to chew the
established order of things, or in throwing dry
bones at the inevitable. There are some facts in
this world which obviously are to be, and’the only
courseistoletthem be as disrespectfully as possible.
When Harry Shine appears as the red-headed,
red-nosed woman in skimpy clothes, The Bulle-
tin recognises that the woman with a flaming nose
and a sunset head is an institution which mustn’t
be disturbed. Also, the fat boy is one of the
Eillars of the constitution, and if the large silly
oy were taken away society might crumble on its
base, and the absence of the nice girl in tight-
fitting male togs would probably cause the empire
to fall asunder. As for the wicked nobleman, he
is always there more or less, and if he were left
out the price of beer would, no doubt, go up out
of sight. All these things are presumably for the
best, and it being impossible to say for certain what
would drop if they were taken away, or what the
thing that dropped would drop on, they had better
be (left. The Bulletin’s representative had one
exciting moment in his life on a certain Xmas eve
on a ferry boat, when the engineer had dived below
to oil something, and.an affable but inquiring drunk
started turning all the handles about the engine to
see which handle it was that made the boat blow
up ; and he doesn’t like rash experiments. Now,
when he sees the same old handles sticking out of
a pantomime, he leaves them alone with en-
thusiasm.
* * • *
Miss Carrie Moore gives another lift to her
rapidly-rising reputation in the part of Blue Boy
—particularly where he is really Boy Brown, in a
cornstalk uniform, leading the great assault on the
golden castle, which is defended by eight Boers
with animals’ heads. Miss Dorothy Vane’s Red
Riding Hood, too, is a fetching vision in short
white socks and what falsely purports to be a
glimpse of childlike bare leg above. Both ladies
were in good voice on the opening night, and they
have practically all the good songs of the panto,
between them. Harry Shine’s red-headed woman
is clever, but she is so very like many other red-
headed women that the charm of novelty is not in
her ; and in Lauri’s part of Baron Splosh the jokes
grow in a thin crop on a barren soil. This notice
wanders back disconsolately for the third time to
the scenery and the clothes. There is soul-food
and mental refreshment in these pictures by
Goatcher and Gordon, and there are so many of
them that they make up for all deficiencies.
** * *
Freeman Wills’ version of the “ Tale of
Two Cities ” at Sydney Royal, concerning which
this paper spoke last week in a cautious frame of
mind, seems to improve with more extended
acquaintance. The two rather weak acts move a
little faster, and the two very strong ones get a
better grip on the soul at a second acquaintance.
There is a fascination, too, in the final tableau,
where the black figure of the dissipated lawyer on
the guillotine is silhouetted against the redness of
the dawn, looking like a crow outlined against
the red bottle in a chemist’s window. Apparently
the Reign of Terror had at least one good work
to its credit; it promoted the honorable virtue of
early rising. People who used to lie in bed late
in the days of a bloated and stodgy monarchy
got up early to be beheaded, and others got
up early to see them beheaded. r l he rest
of the nation got up very early indeed to
make sure that no agents of the new Government
were prowling about their premises in search of
evidence. Sydney Carton, before he fell into the
hands of the Terror, was a long-haired gentleman
who drank all night and lay in bed half the day.
He was reformed by the purifying influence of
republican institutions, inasmuch as he went to
bed sober in prison, and got up in time to be exe-
cuted at next day’s daybreak. It was the first
time he had got up in time to be executed, and
also about the first time he hadn’t gone to bed with
his boots on and his hair in a knot. And if there
had been no French republic he would probably
have died widely disrespected, at a bloated old
age, and imploring the public generally to blow
the giraffe off the counterpane.
** * *
Nothing has happened since last week to
clear up the many mysteries that attach to Hol-
loway’s tremendous moral shock and complication
at Sydney Lyceum. The small, saintly girl who
goes blind under impossible circumstances, and
the ghostly figure which rises up unaccountably in
the church, and the heaven which opens just as
the humble and famished, but God-fearing mother
is about to be thrown off the monument, and
reveals the deceased child’s spirit cantering on a
cloud, are just as mysterious now as they were
before. The great advantage attaching to the
“ Power and the Glory ” is that nobody dreams of
inquiring into anything. The ordinary small
anomaly might provoke inquiry, but when the
public is stunned by having 18 miracles thrown at
its head at once it gives the thing up and takes
the show as it comes. And it comes with such a
blast of paint and props and gaudiness that, taken
all round, it is a very interesting impossibility.
** * *
The bright red scenery of Africa, en-
livened by an occasional dry rainstorm, is still
visible in the mixed 1881 and 1899 play at Sydney
Criterion. The play is bright in spots, the spots
being mostly Rider Haggard’s and the Gallery
Boy’s. When the dramatist gets away from these
two, he is lost, as in Oom Paul’s Council scene,
which is of the melancholy farce order, also
slightly irreverent. This paper has a profound
respect for cheerful blasphemy, but when the
blasphemy is dull and idiotic, it turns pious
at once. The Englanders in the play talk a good
deal of Boer cruelty to the Kaffirs ; but when a
comic Kaffir causes a Boer council man to fall off
his chair on to the hard floor, the council man
merely resumes his seat and hasn’t even a curse to
spare, so the evidence is all against a true bill.
The Kaffir in question is a gleam of sable bright-
ness in the performance. Harry Diver isn’t a
gleam of any sort; the play seems to have got on
his soul. Fortunately, at his worst moments, the
Australian Contingent of 1899 rushed on to the
stage in 1881, and as the members of said
contingent were at that time mainly little boys
who blew tin trumpets, the cowardice of the
full-grown Boers in running away from them is
once more firmly established. Jess and Bess are
two nice girls, who emerge from scenes of carnage
in spotless pink print, and, as there does not seem
much reason why Diver should fall in love with
Jess rather than Bess, or Bess rather than Jess,
and as Diver does not seem to know much about
it himself, the love part pf the drama has not that
air of convincingness which it bears in the novel.
Great praise is due to the conduct of the British
flag, which sticks out stiff and starchy in the first
act. In the ensuing scenes, however, it hangs
limp and nerveless like the tail of a reproved dog.
Why the flag thus caves in doesn’t appear.
*# * *
Maud MacCarthy’s last concert wisely
“drew in” from the echoing spaces of Sydney Town
Hall to the smaller precincts of the Centenary,
where a full house that would have been lost
among the Town Hall chairs, looked cheerfully
like good business. The last hearing of the young
fiddler confirmed previous suspicions and good
opinions. She is an intelligent, expressive, care-
fully-trained player with very thin arms that may
be responsible for the missing qualities of tone and
dash. Miss Marie Narelle, a songstress with an
excellent dramatic intention, magnetic presence
and a nice voice rather lacking in liquidity, and
Miss Kathleen Morven, a throaty contralto,
helped out the concert. It was announced
as the last opportunity of hearing the talented
“ child ’’ over who-e age people still argue when-
ever their conversation becomes musical.
*
At Sydney Palace Miss Eva Sinclair does
some charming dancing. Little Baby Bowers is
also becoming quite an expert dancer, while her
doll song “ Pull the String ” is a gem of the same
water as Dorothy Yane’s in “LaPoupee.” The
wee performer scored a great hit on New Year’s
Night, when an unfeeling and enthusiastic
audience called her back after giving three heavy
items. The new features are the Bell Ballet, the
Newsky Family and Professor Davy’s giant
Marionettes. The Bell Ballet opens with an
empty stage, and out of the wings run eight
grotesque looking pairs in fearful and awful
dresses of red and white. As they run they
jingle bells pendent from their shoulders,
and when they have formed a semi-circle
they shake or tinkle out of themselves the
refrain of “My Bonnie Highland Laddie.”
The Newsky Family consists of five tall, fair
queens with long, white veils trailing from their
glittering coronets. There are also two men
and a boy who look like white Tartars —
’specially the boy. Their music is solemn
and reminiscent of high ritual and Gothic
arches, and their foreign song is a chant-like
melody that calls up memories of lost ideals. This
writer likes the Nevvsky music. Sometimes the
boy bounds around like one possessed, doubles
himself up, and revolves all round the stage on his
heels in a squattihg position. One of the grown-up
Tartars bounds around in the same manner, but he
can’t of doesn’t pirouette like that awful boy.
Then the whole family, including the five tall fair
queens, go through a novel and rather pretty
figure, and last of all the head of the family steps
to the front and sings in broken English “Soldiers
of the Queen.” There is no getting away from
that melody anywhere. The Marionettes are big,
nearly life-size, and are pretty good, but do
nothing that other Marionettes have not done
before. Perhaps it was the same Marionettes the
writer saw before somewhere, but anyhow it all
seems quite familiar.
** * *
“ Little Christopher Columbus ” is evi-
dently going to be a long, solid success at Melb.
Royal, in consequence of many deserving circum-
stances. For one good thing, the performers don’t
hang around waiting for encores after their respec-
tive songs or leg-shakes. A show that begins at
7.45 and finishes on the far side of 11 p.rn. has
little time for doing things twice over in response
to the vociferous demands of people pro-
vided sometimes by themselves and some-
times by the management. Therefore the
prettiest items happen only once each dur-
ing the evening, and anyone who wants
them twice must return on a future date armed
with the necessary ticket. A considerable portion
of Melbourne will pay a return visit to “Little
Christopher,” and find it an improving burlesque-
opera, or comedy-extravaganza, or whatever it
likes to call itself. There’s a quiet unassuming
charm in the music; also, John Sheridan is a
good-hearted married woman who never fails to
be a trifle funnier than before, and the lean-legged
Hooligan is a fresh young comedian, and the
principal ladies, in tights or otherwise, ate of the
O.K. order. In fact, the co. deserves a first-
class certificate for general efficiency and diligent
attention to the business in hand. Small parts
are neatly filled as a rule, and the ballet produces
beads of perspiration upon the present writer’s
bad bald head. If it were not likely to make the
other seven angels jealous this hairless scribe
would take oath and testify that No. 1 in the
ballet —the slim vision who slipped and made a
“marvellous recovery” last Saturday night—is
the most graceful dancer of her school lie ever
saw. She’s a poem on ten toes —presuming that
all her toes are there.
*« * *
“Les Cloches de Corneville ” at Melb.
Alexandra is at least a well-rehearsed and respect-
ably staged production of an admirable old opera.
Also, the first week’s performance was satisfactory
throughout in the musical parts, neither did any
of the acting suffer by comparison with the
awfullest amateur efforts on local record. Worse
outrages than this have been perpetrated in free-
dom’s name, even by professional companies.
The Vic. Operatic Society, which is largely
unprofessional and has frequently amused
itself by assisting a hospital, can continue
to enjoy periodical outbreaks as far as The Bul-
letin’s permission goes. If this journal were asked
to pass sentence of death on the V.O.S. as a body
the solemn words would not be spoken. One or
two individuals might get sentenced to banish-
ment, whilst the rest were let loose again with in-
structions to improve their “make-up.” The
principal crimes, roughly speaking, in the present
treatment of “ Les Cloches ” are Grenicheux’s
comic face and figure, the Bailie’s hollow attempt
to seem old and fat, and the Markee’s way of ex-
pressing himself in prose. Sidney Ra,lfe, as the
baritone Markee, is a rather impressive person
when he doesn’t talk, but in his conversational
aspects he is weak and flabby. The alternate
Gobos get through it passably (every Gobo being
passable), and stage-manager George Leopold is a
very capable miser as a matter of course. Of four
ladies chosen for the two principal billets only
half the number got a show last week. _ Miss Ray
Jones, who has been seriously professional at one
time or other, sings and plays Germain in a pretty
little style, and Miss Kate Hardiman is a spirited
amateur Serpolette, with a strong serviceable
voice. The season of “ Les Cloches ” will end on
Saturday, if it doesn’t stretch into next week.

The Xmas co. at the Bijou includes Tom
Costello and his one-legged family, Spry and Aus-
tin, the three plump sisters on the slim wire, Alma
Gray, the two Englishes, and many other mixed
matters. Food for the Jingo is supplied in Arthur
Hahn’s cold, forgetful rendering of “ The Absent-
Minded Beggar,” after which Will Whitburn
comes along as a weird, ramshackle warrior, and
warbles “Didn’t we fight for England in the
future, and ain’t we going to do it in the past.
Whitburn’s conception of a Gay Gordon on the
warpath is irreverent, but droll.

This means, to begin with, that more than half of the
allegedly “ permanent and reproductive works ” charged
to loans are pure swindle, put there to enable a cowardly
and politically dishonest Ministry to avoid the trouble
and responsibility of raising enough revenue to meet ex-
penses. It also means that the people, having
just as little grit and honesty as their rulers,
prefer this to paying their way. But the above, bad as it
is, doesn't represent the whole trouble or anything like it.
A great number of other items which have received the
benefit of the doubt, are not really reproductive, or are
so in a very small and limited fashion. Lyne’s loan
estimates, taking them all round, are about the worst
political fraud ever laid before the people of N.S.W., and
on their evidence it is the duty of N.S.W. to sack Lyne
as soon as Parliament meets, and keep him carefully out
of office for the remnant of his miserable existence.
* * • *
One thing that is badly wanted if N.S.W.
finance is to be put on a decent footing is the abolition of
the two ridiculous suspense accounts which figure in the
public balance-sheet. In addition to the admitted
deficit there has been taken from the trust funds a sum
of £694,000 which isn’t described either as deficit or as
public debt. It is simply mentioned as the Centennial
Park and Post Office New Street suspense accounts.
Nominally there are land assets against it, but in reality
the land is not worth more than half the amount, and the
rest is shortage. The Government hangs on in the hope
that some day the land will increase sufficiently in value
to cover the amount, but it never will —if
the accounts are kept fairly. That is to say, if
these accounts were debited with land tax and
with municipal rates the bill would probably keep grow-
ing so fast that it would continue well ahead of any
increase in land-values that is ever likely to take
place. Thr Bulletin proposes a way out which is also a
fake, but not so bad a fake as the present one. It is to
sell the land now for what it will fetch, which would per-
haps be £200,000, or less than one-third of the nominal
value. Then to invest the money under a special Act of
Parliament in Government securities, and keep it in a
new suspense account, the interest received on these
securities going to make up the shortage. Also, the
land-tax received on the properties to be paid into the
same account. Between the interest and the land-tax
money the £200,000 which might possibly be received
would increase at the rate of about £7OOO a year at first
and much faster as the fund accumulated, and the bor-
rowed trust funds would be restored intact in from 30 to
36 years. Under present conditions, it doesn’t look as if
they would be restored in a century.

In Canada no life-assurance office is allowed to
estimate future interest on its funds at over 4£ per cent.,
and a Bill is now before the Canadian Parliament enacting
(in view of the decreasing interest-rate) that the estimate
must be gradually reduced to 3£ per cent. It isn’t any
too large a reduction either—in fact it would be better to
make it 3 per cent. But the material point is that Canada
has sense enough to fix a legal standard of solvency—the
slipshod legislators of Australia haven’t. There is no
legal standard of solvency as regards life-offices in this
land of Let-It-Slide ; and in view of the large number of
small new life-offices that are starting, the matter is a
most important one. It must be borne in mind that the
most utterly rotten life-office existing can be perfectly
solvent on paper if it does one or both of two things—
values its assets at a sufficient amount above their real
worth, or estimates that they will earn a large enough
rate of interest. Therefore there should be a State
valuation of the assets and a legally-fixed maximum

The Boers have put their mine inspectors to
manage those Rand mines which they are working. A few
of these inspectors are English by birth ; some are Dutch
(and the educated Dutch provide some of the world s best-
known mining managers: G.D. Delprat, of the ii.rl.
Proprietary, for example). At most of the mines the old
managers-'remain in charge, the Govt, having granted
them and their engineers permits to remain and look
after their properties —further proof of the utter barbarity
of the Boers, of course.

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, Engineer, Ironfounder,
&c of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, reports that the
great demand for spiral riveted pipes has necessitated the
increase of manufacturing facilities at his Footscray
works, and he is now in a position to supply orders at
very short notice, for all sizes of pipes from six to twenty
inches in diameter, with either cast-iron bolted
joints, flanged joints or the ordinary spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. Ferguson also manufactures boilers,
pumps, fluming, patent Nelyambo earth-scoops or tank-
cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making
machinery, bolts, nuts, forgings, and all sorts of iron-work.

The United Brothers gold mine, situated at
Glen Wills, about 25 miles from Omeo, Gippsland, has ail
improved appearance, and even better things are looked
for as deeper ground is reached. The mine possesses an
up-to-date mill. The stone is conveyed automatically to
hopper, thence to Gate’s Crusher and on to 20 head of
stamps of the A.T.B. pattern. A small but complete
plant of Luhrig Vanners and Classifiers treats the battery
tailings and continues to win a large percentage of valu-
able concentrates, which are a satisfactory source of
revenue to the co. This Luhrig plant was supplied and
erected under contract by Messrs. W. and J. Lemprieie,
606 Little Collins-street, Melbourne.*
« * * »
During the plague outbreak on the Indian gold
mines (writes a Bulletin correspondent) the numerous
tailings-heaps were often used by the natives as ceme-
tries for surreptitious burials. It is, therefore, pleasant
to know that the plague microbe (commonly supposed
to exist in the soil) has been proved by bacteriological
research, incapable of subsisting underground in the
mines, or in tailings after cyanide treatment.

all on Kolar, and they employ an average of 16,800 coolies.
Lately the coolie camp has been scourged by the plague.

The renort of the English, Scottish and Aus-
tralian Bank for the year ended 30th
document with some encouraging features about it. The
“S 1 J " n : 5 Pr f ’
” >96,’ „ 27,950 | „ ’99, „ 1 5 ’ 41 . 1
The’E. S. and A. Bank made an arrangement wi hits
creditors in ’93 and worked under it with bad results till
1896 Then it made a further arrangement and obtained
a large reduction of interest. Under the new systemi it
appears to be beginning to do rather better, though the
earnings as yet are only equal to £2 15s. sd. cent on
the shareholders’ capital and reserve fund. The
principal other good points in the balance-sheet are . (1)
That ordinary deposits have increased during
year by nearly £200,000 ; (2) That the old re-constructed
deposits have been reduced by over £48,000, though see-
ing that the amount is still over £3,200,000, this isn t a
very large reduction ; (3) The board sees its w-ay topay
off £156 491 of terminable reconstructed deposits next
year The business, too, looks otherwise in good health.
By the terms of the last arrangement the first charge on
net profits is £15,000 a year to go to reserve fund, there-
fore all the profits for 1897 and ’9B were employed,
and the E.S. and A. has now a reserve of £16,904. Ure-
sumahlv another £15,000 will be similarly applied this
year, which will put up the reserve to £31,904.
In addition to this, the E.S. and A. bas made
£4l 391 by the repurchase of its deposits below their
nominal value, and this sum is kept as a special reserve
against further possible depreciation of the assets. After
writing off £6582 for estimated losses there is still a
depreciation fund of £34,809. It is a notable fact that the
F S and A is the only hank which has fully explained
what it did with the profits made by the repurchase
of its reconstructed deposits. Other institutions either
put them in as ordinary earnings, which makes it im-
possible to ascertain for certain what their ordinary
profit-earning capacity is, or have applied them silently
to writing off losses, without explaining what losses had
to be provided for. * * »
Dividends, however, still look an immense way
off. The first call on the earnings, of the E. S. and A.
Bank, as already mentioned, is £15,000 to go to reserve
fund. Next the 3 per cent, deferred inscribed stock
(£875 676) is to get an increased rate of interest not
exceeding 4) per cent. Then one - fourth of any
balance is to go towards the redemption of deferred
inscribed stock ; and the shareholders don’t get a look in
till after that. And the year’s earnings need to reach
about £64,000 before there is any prospect of a 5 per cent,
dividend. Still, the gradual reduction of the deferred
inscribed stock is improving the condition of things, and
when it is all wiped off prospects will be greatly im-
proved. * * * *
The Freehold Assets Realisation Co. (Mel-
bourne), the concern which took over the wreck of the
old Freehold Investment and Banking Co., publishes its
fourth balance-sheet. It is, on the whole, a rather favor-
able document —that is to say, it is a very dreadful.docu-
ment, but not so dreadful as might have been expected.
The co. keeps the assets of the old hank in its balance-
sheet at their book value, and as it sells them it writes off
the loss. Then it buys up depreciated deposit receipts
with the money. Last year it sold £76,402 of assets (book
value) for £l7,BsB—loss £58,544. Then it bought up
£82,448 of its deposits for £18,838, and thus made a profit
of £63 610. Obviously while the co. can sell its pro-
perties at 4s. 8d in the £ of their nominal value
and use the money to buy up its liabilities
at a fraction less than 4s. 7d. in the £ it isn’t getting
any worse, and the prospects for the remainder of
the depositors are improving a little. The proper-
ties are selling better, too; last year they realised
4s. Bd. in the £ of their book value, while those sold in ’96
only realised 3s. 7d. in the £. In 1897 the paper liabilities
were less than the paper assets by £59,612, but the
shortage (on paper) is now reduced to £10,209. Appar-
ently, therefore, the depositors who hold on to the bitter
end may get something near the face value of their
deposits—or, if values go down again, and it happens that
the worst assets are left to the last,, the man who refuses
4s. 7d. in the & now may be very glad to get Is Id. in the
£ a dozen years hence. Liquidations are uncertain animals
to deal with.
* • » •
The announcement that the Australian Estates
and Mortgage Co. and the Union Mortgage and Agency
Co. are about to combine implies the end of a strangely
insane and purposeless-looking piece of company finance.
In ’94, the Union Mortgage and Agency Co.—then not
feeling any too well—sold a vast amount of property to the
Australian Estates and Mortgage Co., which was formed
for the special purpose of taking it over. The latter co.,
having no capital of its own, paid for the assets by issuing
£1,259,000 worth of shares, practically all of which were
held by the Union Mortgage and Agency Co. The pro-
perty was taken over by the new co. at, it would appear,
much above its real value —not that that made
anv difference ; the old co. only got scrip in payment,
and, if it sold 10s. worth of property for £l, it
naturally followed that the £1 share it received in payment
was only worth 10s., and things were as short as they were
narrow. In fact, the old co. really sold a lot of its pro-
perties to itself under another name, and paid for them ,
by issuing its own scrip to itself, and instead of taking the
profits direct, the new co. declared a dividend out of
them and paid the dividend to the old co., which was the
same co. all the time, and then the old co. could pay a
dividend with the money to its shareholders, if it felt
equal to it —which it didn’t. Out of £2,196,317 assets of the
old co. £1,629,930 consisted of shares and debentures in
the new co. So the old co. existed principally for the
purpose of holding shares in the new co. It had hardly
any other business worth mentioning. The only visible
result of it all was the extra expense of running two
managements instead of one. The new co. did some
ghastly financing. It borrowed £1,200,000 at 4J per cent.,
getting ±9B for every £lOO ; and ±300,000 at 4£ per cent.,
getting £97 for every £lOO ; with the proud privilege of
repaying both sums at £llO for every £lOO. Also, it
borrowed ±400,000 at 5 per cent. The new co. paid a 1
per cent, dividend to the old co. in ’96. The old co. has
paid no dividends since ’93. And now the London
management which did this .weird splitting-up of the
Union Co. in ’94 at much expense for transfers, registra-
tion, printing, stamp-duty, &c., has struck the great idea
of combining the two companies again at more expense
for transfers, stamp-duty, registration, printing, &c. It
says proudly that it has struck this idea in order to save
the needless expense of two managements.
The balance-sheets of the two companies for the
year ended 31st December, 1898, are just published. The
old co. (Union Mortgage and Agency Co.) made a loss of
£3214 ; and the new one (Australian Estates and Mortgage
Co.) made a loss of £16,890. The doings of these two
concerns for the past four years have been :
Union Mortgage and Australian Estates and
Agency Co. Mortgage Co.
1895 Profit £10,394 1895 Profit £16,387
1896 ~ £9,087 1896 „ £2,414
1897 Loss £4,603 1897 Loss ±933
1898 ~ £3,214 1898 „ £16,890
Things are really worse than this for £12,299 of the
profits shown by the Union Co. consist of the 1 per cent,
dividend paid to it out of the profits of the other co. in
1896, so this amount appears twice. And now these two
concerns are going to combine losses.

The B.H. Junction directors have appointed J.
O. Moulden manager, though the directors pondered for
days over the appointment, owing to his youth. Mr.
Moulden is the youngest man who has ever held a front-
rank B.H. managership. He got his theoretical training
in English mining-schools—he is a son of a S.A. director
of B.H. co.’s—and for three years has been understudy to
general manager Greenway, of Block 14 and the South.
It is suggested that now the Junction may be run some-
what on the lines of the latter co.'s —that is, with a mini-
mum of information being given to shareholders as to
the value of the ore handled. Still, if shareholders don[t
mind being kept misinformed as to how much of their
metal is saved and bow much is not recovered (if, that is,
they don’t want any means of testing the value of the
methods in operation)—well, it’s their business.
• * » *
There was a time when every Broken Hill mine
had a “ Captain ” Somebody or other as manager. That
was the Australian period (Australian wages miners, of
Yic. and S.A. especially, are still largely Cornish). Then
every mine but one had an American manager, and
several of them were officered throughout by ’Murkans.
And now only one “ Captain ” remains, and there isn’t a
single ’Murkan manager or chief-officer on the field. It is
the large-English period. And the sharesstill drift out—
and the diva, follow them.
B.H. Junction still has its head-office in Melb. ;
but that is only because the Vic. company law is preferred
and because of the influence of Melb. Stock Exchange.
So, after the formal statutory meeting has been held
there, the real shareholders’ meeting takes place in Ade-
laide, where most of the shares are held, and where the
directors meet and all the business is done. Acting-
chairman R. C. Baker, addressing the Adelaide share-
holders recently, gave no more cheerful account of things
than did the report and balance-sheet, lately discussed
here. To the criticism that the writing-down for deprecia-
tion had been severe it was replied that “all the leases at
the Barrier expired in about 4* years ; and what would be
the value of the whole plant and machinery on a mine
that did not belong to them ?” Which suggests that this
bogey of the approaching expiry of the leases ought to be
officially laid at once ; at present it is likely to be used for
the purpose of squeezing out the weaker holders. There
was only one further fresh item of interest in Chairman
Baker’s address : the announcement that £1179 had been
received on account of tailings taken from the dump, and
that this amount had been included in the £49,280 “ pro-
ceeds of ore and concentrates ” —which, without the
explanation, seems a fairly effective way of making it im-
possible to tell how this particular half-year’s operations
panned out. Altogether it is quite legitimate to take a
less gloomy view of the mine’s prospects than the report
seems to convey. In such an erratic mine as the Junction
the encountering of poor ore in the lowest level explored
is not a circumstance to justify a funk.
* * * *
Every month or two somebody plumps in the
statement that the Broken Hill mine leases have about
five or six years to run—and then. Especially if a Labor
or Radical party gets into power anything, it is whispered,
may happen. Henry Copeland, who knows as much
about N.S.W. mining law and its history as anybody,
incidentally threw' some light on this in the Assembly the
other evening. Thus : “ Gold leases can be renew'ed with
the consent of the Minister subject to no fine. The lessee
has to pay a fine for the renew r al of a mineral lease, and
that fine was introduced into our legislation by a blunder.
Our Act was copied almost word for word from the Act
of ’65 then in force in Vic., and in that Act it is provided
that a fine of £2 10s.—not £2 10s. per acre—shall be paid
to meet the cost of a new' lease. When our ’74 Act was
passed, however, the words, ‘ £2 10s. per acre’ were in-
serted by mistake, and we now impose that fine though
the intrinsic value of the land after the removal of the
machinery and the plant may not be more than 2s 6d.
per acre.”
* • * *
B.H. South, which has been only fairly well off
for tractable ore for some time past, has had a turn for
the better during the last week or two.
The B.H. Consols was the first Barrier mine to
get into English hands ; and a pretty poor bargain it has
proved. The mine is on a flat away from the main lode.
More by good luck than anything else some patches of
exceedingly rich ore have been met with now and then—
just enough to keep the co. pegging away, with the help
of occasional calls and occasional reconstructions. At
present the co. consists of a trifle of 600,000 shares. Nine
months ago great things were going to happen ; the mis-
guided people w'ho read some of the daily papers’ mining
news were led to believe that a greater than the B.H.
Prop, had been discovered. However, the 12 months
report, just to hand, disclosesa profit of £1459, which, it is
added, is the most the present co. ever made. As this
means a shade over a ha’penny a share, it can easily be
reckoned up that the co. has not had a particularly good
time. Of two parcels of ore sent aw'ay by the B. H.
Consols last year, one, weighing 2 tons lcw't. 3qr., re-
turned 19,5170 z. silver, and the other, weighing 3 tons
lcwt 3qr., returned 29,6770 z. This is the sort of stuff
that fascinates the maiden aunt with a few pounds to put
into shares ; and the maiden aunt is usually left again.

During the last days of the Reid regime
Hurley, M.L.A., asked the N.S.W. Secretary for Mines if
he would bring in a Bill to make it compulsory for all
companies extracting metals from ore by any process
whatever to keep books showing the number of tons
treated, the method of treatment, and particulars of the
mine and soil from which the ore was obtained. Also, at
the end of each year, the companies’ responsible managers
to make statutory declarations that the particulars were
correct. Something of the sort is done now, but in a very
small, inefficient way. The scientific advantage of sucn
action would be enormous.

A big Sydney and Melb. crowd has bonded the
whole of the Northern coking-coal properties. Said to
be over 300,000,000 tons of coking coal available, and the
quality, according to Schlapp, is 12s. a ton better than the
best Durham coke. If the co. succeeds m getting the
Chillagoe and Broken Hill contracts it has a big thing.
• * * *
Mr. S. Dixon, an enthusiastic and well-informed
advocate of forest and water conservation, lately went
into N. Queensland in the interests of a S.A. prospecting
co. He was impressed about equally with the great value
of N.Q. mining fields, the importance of the magnificent
cedar and other forests (Atherton “ scrub,” in Chillagoe
district, he mentions in particular), and the utterly in-
adequate measures existing for the conservation ot any
of the timber. Remembering the clean sweep made Of
the forests about every other big Australian mining
centre, Mr. Dixon has been doing Queensland the good
service of trying to wake up Brisbane politicians to action
while yet there is time to do something effective.

metallism forced two weary Sydney editors to apply for
leave of absence, has, in true monometallic spirit, gone
dredging for gold. He has taken up 20 miles of the Bin-
gara (N.S.W.) River, and hopes to catch a yellow fortune
at flood.

So uncertain a quantity is the tin-market that
John Moffatt, the brains of the Herberton and Irvine-
bank (N.Q.) tin district, some time ago made forward
contracts at ±lOO. He has since seen the price go 50 per
cent, above that. But he has been too long at the game
to grumble. He was tin-mining in the Tingha and Emma-
ville district before he went north.

Mount Morgan report for year ending Novem-
ber, 1899, is just out. The 7d. dividend is to be dropped
a while to 6d., as “ ±50,000 more will be required to com-
plete the second section of the new mundic works.
Total ore treated for the year was 118,658 tons, or 16,407
tons more than the average of the last two years. Ihe
average yield of all ore treated for the last year was
14Mwts., or ljdwts. less than the average for the preced-
ing 12 months. The decrease is due, it is alleged, not to
the poorness of the mine at present, but to the treating of
large bodies of inferior ore hitherto left m the mine.
Mundic ore gave a return of 9Jdwts., but this does not
represent final results, as much of the material dealt with
retains gold awaiting further treatment. The total
118,658 tons gave 87,4690z5. The directors are sanguine
of good results for the next year.

* * • *
New Chum Railway (Bendigo) has main work-
ings at 3200 ft., at which level it is breaking stone—an
Australian record. A good deal of work has been done
at this depth, too ; there is one crosscut 293 ft. long, 9ft.
high, 6ft. wide. In spite of the heat, this was driven with
two drills 40ft. a fortnight—work which would take some
beating. The reef in the neighborhood has been tested
in several places ; it runs from Bft. wide (this is at a point
a little above the 3200 ft.) to 20in. The stone is good-
looking stuff; and the cap gave between 5 and 6dwt.
The neighboring Shenandoah is getting good results from
the 3133 ft. level.

There was a shocking coincidence in connection
with the Bendigo engine-drivers’ strike. Reputed mil-
lionaire Lansell is rhe principal shareholder in the mines
whose drivers are refused the standard-wage of Bs. 4d. a
day. Just when a public appeal was being made to him
to grant the few pence extra, mainly on the. ground that
the driver has a position of much responsibility, an acci-
dent occurred in Lansell's Little ISO mine through a
person unused to a winch setting the machine going and
finding that he could not stop it. One of the miners at
the bottom of the shaft had his arm over the bar of the
drill when the run-away bailing-bucket crashed on it, and
left the limb hanging to the shoulder only by skin. And
then it was half-an-hour before they could get the wind-
ing-machine to work in order to bring the mutilated
miner to the surface. But even that did not touch the
millionaire’s heart; and when, the next day, thecitizensr
deputation appealed to him to grant the few pence extra,
he roared a “ No ’’ at them. Bring along that legal mini-
mum wage.
The miner whom the run-away bucket hit is a
good plucked chap. When his arm was jagged off, the
first thing he did was to grope round the shaft for his
mate, whom he believed to he underneath 1 Then, while
waiting* for the cage, he sat on the bucket that had
done the damage and helped his mate (who had escaped
serious injury) to hind a piece of rope round the stump
in order to arrest the bleeding. For much less than that
a colonel in South Africa has been made the subject of a
thousand messages and paragraphs. Also, this Bendigo
hero—his name is plain Johnson —has been the support of
his old widowed mother. What is a patriotic public
going to do for him and her?
Already under the new S. Aus. Act providing
for the issue of gold-dredging leases in the N. Territory,
10 applications have been approved, chiefly for 5000-acre
lots on the Howley, McKinley, and Margaret River flats,
and Yam Creek. The lessees are to pay 6d. per acre a-
year, with 2i per cent, of annual profits derived from the
leased lands”with a statutory allowance of 5 per cent, for
depreciation of machinery.

So well satisfied is the South Australian co.
(mostly ore-dressing-machinery men), which has taken in

a s°-
Following upon the provision of public batteries
in W.A., an agitation has begun for their government by
local committees representing leaseholders. Three
things in particular are growled over. First, there is the
order of crushing. This difficulty has arisen at the open-
ing of batteries, and will probably more or less disappear.
The arrangement was that the order should he decided
by lot. Promptly, men who didn’t have any stone to
crush came along and joined in the ballot, on the off-
chance of selling their right if they drew an early date.
Then it is alleged that standard rate of wages is not being
paid ; the Dept, is being petitioned independently on
this head. Also, it is complained that there have been
delays through shortage of water, which might easily
have been prevented had there been local governing
bodies or even boards of advice. The latter, at any rate,
might be tried without much danger and with the likeli-
hood of gain.

From Dawson City (Klondyke) to The Bulle-
tin :—Just a word re this goldfield and its people. No new
discoveries this year. Old creeks holding out fairly we .
The new field at Cape Nome, 2000 miles from here, has
decreased our population from 30,000 to 13,500 in three
months. Too many people here still for the good of the
field. Wages, lOdol. for 10 hours ; work scarce. Beef,
cents (35.) per lb. Australians can’t work without beet,
and so have to take a back seat when Swedes or Ameri-
cans are to be had ; said Swedes can work all the year on
bacon-and-lieans ; Americans nearly as bad. Very few
Australians left in Klondyke now : not more than twenty
of the Australians who came in the ’9B rush went out with
more than £l5O. Col. Steele, officer in charge N.W.
mounted police of the Yukon, got notice to go out to
Ottawa, leaving his office in charge of Major Parry, of
Vancouver. The citizens held a mass meeting, ana
passed resolutions protesting against the removal of Lol.
Steele and altogether condemning the action of Mr.
Sifton, Minister of the Interior, in taking away' the only
honest and reliable man holding office in the Klondyke.
Sifton remained firm, and Col. Steele left yesterday for
the outside. The people of Dawson are not .accustomed
to deal with honest officials, and showed their apprecia-
tion of this man’s straightness by presenting himmtn a
gold brick, suitably inscribed and valued at 2000 aoi.
(£500), also a £5OO bag of gold-dust. There are four
papers here—one daily, one weekly, two bi-weeklies ;
each and every one costs one shilling a cop,y, and torn
shillings a line for advts.—Wm. P., 27/8/’99. *

AT THE "AT-HOME.”
HER: “ Isn’t that a falsetto voice?”
HIM: "No. False set o’ teeth.”
One “ Zealandia ” writes sarcastically in
N.Z. Times (Wellington):
As one who saw the contingent off on their long
journey, I cannot help feeling a glow of pride at the
excellent results attained on their first appearance under
fire. I note that they killed eight of the enemy. A very
creditable performance, but one that would be con-
siderably enhanced if the shooting statistics were sup-
plied. I would suggest that Major Robin keep an account
of the number of rounds issued per man, which, if divided
by number killed, will give a “figure of merit” that
doubtless will be a source of pride and interest to
many. I see that a well-known firm of jewellers in
England, are supplying pencil-cases made from the
cartridge cases fired at Omdurman. Could not
something similar he turned out in Wellington,
the manufacturer, as at Home, giving 10 per cent, of the
profits for a charitable object, as, for instance, the relief
of the widows of the deceased Boers? Since the result of
the contingent’s fire is so accurately noted, I would
suggest that the eight cartridges used for these particular
eight Boers be retailed at a considerably higher figure
than the others. If the deceased’s name and that of the
trooper who fired the shot could appear on the case, its
value would be considerably increased. However, there
would doubtless be considerable difficulties in the way of
this last suggestion. In the meantime, I hope that the
further movements of the New Zealanders will be as
accurately reported in your excellent paper. Such re-
ports compare most favorably with those of the work of
the Imperial troops, which, however, is of secondary in-
terest.
If the country is going into the souvenir business
what’s the matter with freezing the dead Bo< rs
and selling them, the proceeds to go to some fund
or other. A frozen Boer should sell well just now.
* * * *
According to the early yarn of the British
surrender at Nicholson’s Nek, it was more acci-
dent than anything else. Some junior officer,
said the chopped-and-censored cable, raised the
white flag—to the great disgust of all the men and
all the other officers, who wanted to go on fighting
“like lions” and moving “as steadily as if on
parade,” and so on until they were wiped out.
Noiv it turns out that one of General White’s
staff-officers, a full-blown Major and Deputy-
Assistant-Adjutant-General (no less !) did the
flag-raising.
* * » *
Sydney Daily Telegraph has found tvvo
mysterious individuals who yearn to give respec-
tively .£5OOO and £3OOO towards the cost of
another contingent. Well, why don’t they give
it ? “Five thousand pounds ”is easily said ; and
there’s no responsibility as long as you keep your
name quiet. There has been one handsome
Transvaal cheque in Queensland ; and the donor
absorbed patriotic cheers, and then the cheque
wasn’t paid, and there were explanations and
recrimination, and in the end the.long-suffering
taxpayer settled the difficulty as usual.
* * » *
The Bulletin has pleasure in suggesting
that all “ loyal ” Ministries and Parliaments in
Australia, who are spending the taxpayers’money
so enthusiastically “for the Empiah,” should
prove their bona-fides by spending a little of their
own. So far, after two months’ hard pounding,
the total Australian “Patriotic Fund ” subscrip-
tion is little more than a fourth of what was
raised for the Soudan contingent by N.S.W.
alone; and politicians’ subscriptions have been
singularly absent. Would it not be a graceful act
on the part of Premier Lyne to move that half
Ministers’ and members’ salaries be paid to the
Patriotic Fund while the war lasts? Then the other
Premiers would doubtless follow suit. At present,
the people, do all the paying and the politicians all
the shouting. Isn’t it about time the politicians
did a little paying too ? Premier Lyne confesses
to the reporters that “ his best and most patriotic
instincts ” have been aroused ; but he has not yet
announced that he will soothe those instincts by
exposing six-feet odd of fervour to a Boer bullet.
And how much has he subscribed to the Patriotic
Fund ?
¥ * *
Tile most interesting item, so far, in the
list of N.S.W. Patriotic Fund subscriptions is :
“ A Sydney Native, being usual subscription to
The Bulletin, £\ 65.” Which reminds this
paper of another native subscriber in a town in
northern N.S.W. He gets the paper through a
local agent, and about once a month he calls
round with a yell of wrath and stops the disloyal
rag. Then for one week he leads a life that has
lost its savour, for he is wondering what dreadful
things this truly dreadful paper has been saying,
and what evil it has been disseminating when he
didn’t have his eye on it. After that his small
girl buys the paper surreptitiously for a week or
two at another shop. Next he subscribes regularly
at the other shop. • Then a day comes (it comes
every month usually) when this organ stands
with greater weight than usual on his poli-
tical corn, and he goes down to the new shop
purple with rage.to stop the discreditable publica-
tion for good and all. The local inhabitants
always know, when they see him going down the
street apoplectic and of a bright blue color, what
his mission is, and they remark to each other,
“There’s Smith goin’ down to stop his Bulletin.”
The chief sufferer is the small girl who has to trot
long distances now to get the paper, it- having
been stopped in disgust at all the reasonably
adjacent shops. The Bulletin is proud of the
fact that it is stopped oftener and with more in-
dignant remonstrance than any other paper in

Consecutive advts. from Melbourne Age :
SENSIBLE, domesticated young woman desires ac-
quaintance (correspondence first instance) edu-
cated man, view above. Inquiry, P. 0., S. Melb.
ITNCONVENTIONAL young Woman, wishing to
lead altruistic life, seeks to correspond with
strictly honorable, cultivated man having similar
desire. Ehre, Age Office, Melb.
There are many varieties of them, from the plain
kitchen girl who wants a husband to the untram-
melled soul who desires to lead the altruistic life,
but the end of all these things is a judgment for
7s. 6d. a week for vittles for the infant. The
common, sordid, damp infant puts his shapeless
footprint in everything.
THEIR VIEW OF IT.
SHE: “It must be dreadfully dangerous to be a
nurse at the seat of war.”
HER: “ I suppose it is. But perhaps the Boers
won't ill-use women.”
SHE: “ Oh, I don’t know. I saw, the other day,
that they were threatening Ladysmith.”
HER: “And they had seized Harrismith.”
SHE: “ How dreadful. Have they still got him
and how does his poor mother feel about it? ”
A cable-item which appears to have been
sent along in a festive moment was that one to the
effect that the Scots’ Greys had dyed their horses
brown before going to the front. The charge of
Her Majesty’s Dyed Cavalry would be a weird
event.

MARRIAGES.
Sheehy—Mackenzie.— Oti the 18th May, 1896, at St.
George's R.C. Church, Drummond-street, Carlton,
Victoria, by special licence, by the Rev. Thomas Quinn,
Jerh. (Der'mot) MacKenzie Sheehy, eldest son of Jerh.
Canty Sheehy, Esq., of Baltimore, Cork, Ireland, formerly
officer in English Navy (H.M.S. St. Vincent), and subse-
qu ntly of Eureka Stockade, Ballarat, and more subse-
quently merchant in New Zealand and Victoria; grand-
son of Jerh. Sheehy, great grandson of John Canty, the
eminent stone bridge architect; cousin of Mr. Mahony,
R.N.R., of her Majesty's Imperial Coastguards, and Mr.
J. Mahony, chief officer of the ship Armadale, now in
this port ; and Mr. Jerh. Sheehy, merchant, Boston,
Mass., U.S.A., to Mary Ursula (Cissie) Mackenzie,
youngest daughter of John Andrew Mackenzie,
Esq., of Glengairn, near Ballatar, Aberdeenshire,
Scotland, veterinary surgeon and high class farrier
(founder of St. Joseph’s Band, Launceston); grand-
daughter of Donald MacKenzie ; great granddaughter
of Donald MacKenzie, chief of the Mackenzies, of
Aberdeenshire; sister of Messrs. Kenneth and Donald
MacKenzie, of Wellington-street, Launceston, and the
late Mrs. Parr; cousin of the late Very Rev. Fr. Macin-
tosh, Rev. Fr. Duncan Mac Nab, Rev. Fr. Colin MacKen-
zie, Rev. Fr. W. MacKenzie, and Mr. John MacKenzie,
Deputy-Governor H.M. Gaol, Broken Hill, South Austra-
lia ; and Messrs. Kenneth, Donald, John, and Colin
MacKenzie, and Mrs. Munro, and Mr. Donald Thain,
Bathurst, New South Wales; and Mrs. Gordon, of Stone-
haven, Kincardineshire. Cork and Aberdeenshire papers
please copy.
IN MEMORIAM.
Sheehy. —-In fond and affectionate memory of my
dearly beloved wife, Cissie (formerly Miss M. U. Mac-
Kenzie, of Wellington-street, Launceston), youngest
daughter of the late John Andrew MacKenzie, Esq., and
sister of Messrs. Kenneth and Donald MacKenzie, and
the late Mrs. Parr, who departed this life at Melbourne
on the 21st December, 1898. A truly devoted and
affectionate wife. R.I.P.

It is printed in out-back N. S. W., as an illus-
tration of the weakness of a piebald administra-
tion, that at Packsaddle, on a much-used track
leading to Mt. Brown, an artesian bore struck a
good pumping supply of water rising to within a
few feet of the surface, but that the bore-hole was
left unprotected for about five years, during which
sand and dust drifted in and filled it to a depth of
between 900 and 1000 ft. ! Some Govt, really
ought to make up its mind what department
should have full control of public watering places,
and then the Minister entrusted with the job
should thoroughly overhaul the branch.

godly banking official,

One Giddons, a humor-
ous meteorologic, quotes from his
last year’s forecast of the “ Vic-
torian season, 1899,” and congratulates himself on
its accuracy. Giddons prophesied “ November
will be dry and December unsettled and showery.”
So, considering that November was mostly wet
(especially in Cup week), and December has been
warm and dry as a rule, Giddons feels quite jus-
D'fied inlaying down the weather for 1900.
“SYDNEY STREET-SINGERS.”
“ Let us gather up the sunbeams
Lying scattered round our path.”
On a recent Sunday in Sydney Domain,
two “cullahd gemmen ” were endeavoring to
show the right track to a lot of white trash,
while further on a Chow implored his giggling
audience to come to “Klist.” Which looks like
getting back on the Chinese missionary cranks.
AN OCEAN GRAVE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Rocking and rocking, to and fro
Rocking a world to sleep;
And ever they come from a restless life
To die in the quiet deep.
Swinging and swinging, back and forth
Over the dead below,
Their sin and their care they have left above
To the depths of Peace they go.
Waiting and waiting, while ages fail,
Waiting for sorrow to die ;
I am the bride of unending Time
The Tomb of the world am L

. The war-fever man of Melbourne Even-
ing Hehald raves with a glassy stare in his eye
about the “ massacre ” of the British—a few
hundreds —at Spytfontein. And re the alleged
slaughter of 2000 Boers it is merely said that they
were defeated and lost heavily. There may be
such a thing as “British fair play”—it is fre-
quently alleged that there is—but the correspond-
ing Australian article appears to be as scarce as
hen’s hoofs.

Two letters on Broken Hill :
Dear Bulletin. —Re “Essay ” on Adelaide (B. 2/12/’99).
Broken Hill is a fairly cosmopolitan place with a large
preponderance of Cr’oweaters. The difference between
these and the Eastern States natives is marked enough to
be startling. S.A. reps, haven’t got enterprise enough to
close their mouths in fly-time; they don’t fill any posi-
tions of importance except in the unspeakable Municipal
Council, and they get there by solid party votes.
Result: a town that would shock a Bashi Bazouk—
unclean, unlighted, mangy, awful! The Crow population
(mostly Methodist) scratches itself into activity about
Christmas time, forms itself into mobs, and then the
raucous roars of “ Carol ” singers, very unsteady on their
legs, put a finishing touch to the horrors of this God-
forsaken spot. Igo solid for pagan immigration.— Line
o’ Load.
And the indignant office Crow responds :
Sydney-siders at B.H. are mostly policemen. Lately
there were 22 reported burglaries and not one arrest.
Certainly B.H. is unlighted. The “ unspeakable local
Council” asked for a. bill authorising it to light up ; it
was the Sydneyside Parliament that bashi-bazouked the
measure. S. Australians don’t fill any positions of im-
portance—in the public service. Sydneyside depart-
ments, of course, send round Sydneysiders. But it was
one S. Australian who discovered the field, another who
discovered 8.H., another is present chairman of B.H.
Prop., others are chairmen of Block 14, Junction, South,
and British. The management of Junction, South, Cen-
tral, Block 14, and others used to be in the eastern capi-
tals ; and a beautiful mess things drifted into ! Han-
cock, May, and Warren, three “ Crows,” are responsible
for the process of zinc-extraction which is keeping Broken
Hill on its feet; and every manager, bar two, on the line
of lode got his mining experience in S. Australia. So
there !
* * * *
Chief Justice and Acting-Governor Stout,
of Maoriland, came in severe collision with him-
self the other day. As C. J. he granted an order
for the extradition of an alleged escapee from
New Caledonia. Then, when the order came
before him as Acting-Gov., he refused to sign it
on certain high legal grounds, and made justly
severe remarks in one capacity about himself in
his other capacity for granting the order. In fact,
Stout practically rushed at himself and kicked
himself with great contempt.
* * * *
“Melb.”: Never did an Australian
Church so thoroughly satisfy ray artistic ideal as
the big Gothic St. Mary’s about to be opened in
West Melbourne. The congregation is in love
with it as congregations seldom are with a new
place. When lighted up at night it is Zion, the
perfection of beauty. The yellow windows have
an indescribably pleasing appearance with their
glow on the light-brown sandstone, with its white
facings. Barley-sugar, somebody called the effect.
“Here let my soul rest, for I do love it well.”
The Hotham Catholics declare that St. Mary’s b
matchless on this continent.
DEAD LUCKY—HIS POINT OF VIEW.
TOMMY : “ My word, Cis, I'm glad I’m not rigged out like that.”
CIS : “ Why?”
TOMMY: “ Just think how it would be if I was in for a spanking."
“Constance Clyde,” who isn’t an Aus-
tralian girl herself, makes a few bright remarks on
that personage:—
Have been studying the Australian girl for a year or
more, and have come to the conclusion that the climate
or something has completely unhinged her alleged intel-
lect on questions of sex. Her idea of man is of a being
always simmering with wild and passionate desires, and
every office she has visited is redolent of dark experiences
wherein only the virgin purity of her glance saved her
from some foul design. If the Australian girl happens to
be alone with a man, and he does not try to kiss her, she
thinks it wonderful self-control on his part. It never
occurs to her that perhaps he did not kiss her merely
because he did not want to. Ever after he is enshrined
in her heart as a great, good man who wrestled with a
fearful temptation, and catne forth unscathed. The
Australian girl goes in for men friends, especially married
ones. She labors under the delusion that their wives are
frantically jealous of her, and this fills her with a mourn-
ful joy. Again, it .is an acknowledged fact that every
A.g. receives thirty proposals of marriage per diem ; she
tells you this herself, and she ought to know. She goes
about the world in terrible fear lest someone will make
love to her or want to marry her. It is dreadful, the
anguish the poor thing suffers—and so unnecessarily, too.
There was a time when I credited the A.g.’s story and
wept with her at the havoc she had wrought; but the
worm has turned. (When I say the worm I mean me.)
Now, whenever a new acquaintance breaks the ice by
casually remarking that she had ten offers the day before
yesterday, I calmly reply that I had 20 during the after-
noon. After which she treats me with great respect—as
a more brilliant liar than herself.

Extract from speech on education by more
or less bon. member in S.A. Assembly:
Especially this comes in regard to the matter of female
pupil teachers. The work, as far as female pupil teachers
is concerned, is heavier, because the sewing—and we all
know those various artistic and very curious work —a
great tax upon their various energies—and there is no
doubt about it —by a very close observation of work of
this character and the supervision of it—shows that all
the responsibility of it largely depends upon the female
pupil teachers, so that you can see, and of course the
Department—the Department is not singular in this
matter —fh -y seem to be very anxious to get as great a
number of female pupil teachers as possible. It is the old
question of male and female labor, and of course the
Government, like other shrewd people—they are ngither
better nor worse -it is the commercial spirit of thf age,
and they are practically actuated by that principle.
Launceston has a morgue in which to
accommodate the Tasmanian sleeper who, after
all, doesn’t sleep so very much harder than his
living fellow-islanders. And a local doctor lately
described it in this indignant fashion :
The City Morgue ! Twelve feet square, eight feet in
height. The room is floored with wood, walls lined with
wood, the roof roofed with wood. The door has a key,
and can be locked at pleasure by medical men inside.
None dare venture here but medical men and—corpses !
There is a frosted window which can never be opened,
and this is protected on the outer side by wire netting,
so that if by misadventure the window be broken the net-
ting will still prevent exit of the thickened atmosphere
into the street. There is the exit and entrance for air,
the keyhole, but that is filled by a large Corporation key ;
if it were not so, the odor might escape and blister the
red paint on the Corporation cart wheels. At one end of
the room is an old door resting on trestles. Having
travelled, you recognise it at once as a couch or bed.
Under the closed window is a slab, made of two large,
flat, disused paving stones, resting on a plank, which has
become springy. Behind the door is a nail—a real nail,
put there by the City Council chat the doctor may hang
his coat up ! What more do you want ?

In a far nor’west Queensland town recently
a poor woman, tramping with her husband and
infant in search of work, was run in by the power-
ful arm of the law and fined 275. by her outraged
country for stealing a tin and an old bag off an
ash-heap. A full-sized information was laid
against this hardened criminal by a local official,
and the law decided that dangerous characters
like her and the infant had to be Put Down.
* * * *
A witness in a recent Melb. divorce case,
when asked if she had ever noticed any undue
familiarity between respondent and co-respondent,
replied enthusiastically that she had once seen the
co-respondent deliberately tread on respondent’s
toe, and, on another occasion, attempt to jmb her
head in a bag. Which recalls the undue
familiarity in “Jude the Obscure,” when the
temptress playfully pelts the hero with pigs’
entrails.
* * * *
Apropos of these medical examinations of
Transvaal would-be warriors, it is interesting to
note (by New York papers) that certain American
medicoes make a handsomething out of putting
through a course of training men rejected by
various insurance societies ; bolstering them up
by special treatment and medicine for the time
being, and so dragging them through the examina-
tion of some fresh society. The insurance com-
panies have lately dropped to the game.
THE ELOPEMENT OF LYDIA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Young'Lydia Fox, sweet, fair, and kind,
In marriage bonds had been assigned
To Johnson of the bleary eyes,
So she eloped with Samuel Wise.
Another love did Lydia please,
A love for things with claws and fleas ;
Her soulful sympathies went to
A dog, a cat, a cockatoo.
Thus when her erring feet did roam
But half-a-mile from her lost home,
She stopped in grief, with streaming eyes.
“Return, and bring my Tom, Sam Wise !”
Pleased that his love should prove so kind,
Sam turned, and raced the evening wind,
And soon on shoulder broad did bear
A cat that gaily chewed his ear.
Two furlongs moved the happy pair,
When Lydia sighed : “ Dear boy, I care
For reputation not a rap!
Take that, but bring my little Snap.”
With slower step and dubious face
Did Sam the traversed ground retrace.
And after much ado and stir,
He caught at last that howling cur.
But when, their journey almost through,
She called to mind the cockatoo,
The swift retreat of Samuel Wise
Could scarce his purpose dire disguise.
Long time, with glance now sweet now stern,
Did Lydia watch his wished return ;
At last his manly form was seen
Advancing o’er the meadows green.
But lo ! beside the noble bird,
Whose curse across the flat was heard,
Upon Sam’s back hung merrily
A form that Lydia shrieked to see ;
Dark lightning flashed from Samuel’s eyes,
As at her feet he laid each prize.
“ Three times my steps I turned to get
For my soft-hearted love each pet.
“ And as my strength was somewhat spent;
When me to fetch this bird you sent,
To save more journeying and ado,
I brought along the old man too.”
CONSTANCE CLYDE.
LA BELLE JAPONAISE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Her figure’s like a Venus tanned ;
Her black hair’s rolled up high ;
Her full round lips are browney red,
And slants her almond eye.
She loves to dabble in a tub,
And splash in water clear.
Her little feet are just the hue
Of pale colonial beer.
The trouble is you can’t converse,
Her English is so slight ;
The lips aforesaid only say
“ Goo’-day ” —or else “ Goo’-night.”
Yet let us drink !—“ The light brown lass
With slanting almond eyes ! ”
Although I bet she’ll bust the town —
She comes to civilise.
