A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, January 2, 1900.
My Dear Myee.—
On Boxing day, at the A.J.C., the Members’
Stand was a howling wilderness. The V.R. box
was empty even of its only Two, and not more
than six Society women cleaned the lawn with
their graceful tails. Mrs. Harry Levy was an
elegant figure in a white silk blouse with a white
skirt and white feather hat toned up with pink.
A pretty Jewish visitor from Maoriland looked
like a picture, not by an old master, in a big black
hat and white mull muslin. But thinking it over,
it is rather hard to remember anyone who didn’t
wear white. White frocks went about in bat-
talions. One style that was done to death was
the tucked and insertioned white muslin, fastened
up the back, with a white muslin fichu folded
flatly round the shoulders so as to take in the top
of the arms. I counted these in dozens. They
have a t v -s -ed-fowl look that seems rather apropos
of the.', .ls season.
Eveiy le woke up after lunch when a newly-
married couple arrived. That doesn’t seem any-
thing to be lively over, did you say? Yes, but
this couple are very well known, particularly the
bridegroom. The question was—how was the bit
of Society present going to receive his wife ? The
pretty daughter of a solicitor attached herself to
the couple immediately. Then there was a pause.
And then two well-known matrons walked down
ostentatiously from the stand, and marching up
spread the light of their countenances on the shy-
looking bride so that all the world might see.
After that, of course, there could not be much
Question about the bride’s reception by Society,
lefore, there was none.
In a month the two leading society women,
both pretty and both quite young-looking, are
widows. Their .absence from society is very
noticeable, for a race meeting without Mrs.
Willie Mitchell and Mrs. Burdekinand her pretty
daughters is dull by many degrees.
Do women punt? Ask a certain Sydney man.
On Tuesday a Melbourne woman requested him to
put a pound on her fancy, at fifty to one. She
didn’t give him the pound just then, and he went
away and forgot. It was an unknown horse, and,
of course, walked in. When the race was over,
the puntress rushed up with a bevy of other
women, a,nd hit him on the back, bubbling over
with excitement. “ You brought me that luck.
You must have champagne with us all.” And he
went sadly and had champagne, his champagne, if
paying for a thing makes it one’s own.
On New Year’s Day Randwick lawn was flut-
tery with muslins and bright with rose and tur-
quoise sunshades. Perhaps you don’t know that
a rose or a red parasol is rather good for keeping
off sun-brown. White muslins swept everything
before them again, except what they carried round
in the under flounces ; and I noticed several
striped all round with wide torchon and worn
over colored slips that looked dangerously like a
bodice cottoned on to the best white petticoat.
Scarcely any best Society turned up. His Ex.
turned the light of a pale-pink shirt and whitey-
grey belltopper and suit, and Captain Smith’s
illuminating expression thrown in, on the
meeting for an hour or so, and women cen-
tred rather hopelessly round the V.R. box.
Even the most persistent Mrs. Pushington found
it hard to open a V.R. conversation without the
assistance of a “Ladyship,” and a pet charity,
and that was the reason, you may be sure, why
the V.R. box had a lonely, man-haunted look all
the afternoon.
In frocks that smote, I noticed Mrs. Jack Want
wearing a beautifully cut grey voile, with a big grey
hat and black feathers ; somebody in powder-blue
voile, with heavy string-colored guipure edging the
polonaise in big Vandykes, and transparent string-
colored yoke, and a bewitching cream straw hat,
trimmed with deep blue fringed scarfs, andaglimpse
of orange flowers; Mrs. Ted Simpson looking
smart and mercurial in an all-white toilette ; Mrs.
Monty Severn in cream alpaca skirt and cream
silk and insertion blouse ; and Miss Mollie Slat-
tery in turquoise-blue muslin with a black lace
yoke and black hat, receiving good wishes re her
just-announced engagement to a young solicitor
from Bananaland.
But, if otherwise uneventful, there was one
thing that marked the meeting out from others.
That was the steeplechase, in which four horses
behaved as followeth : One baulked straight off
and was seen no more. A blue-and-white horse
got a long way ahead of the others, then it fell
down. A white horse took the lead until it fell
down. A red horse that was made to win, as you
will see presently, took the lead itself, but a little
way further fell down. The horse that had
falien first now got up, and got ahead a long
way. Suddenly it seemed to think it was time to
go slower—in the interests of the public, let us
say—so it fell down again. By that time the blue
horse was going again a long way ahead of the
red, so it ran thoughtfully out of its course a bit,
then went and leaned up against a hurdle for a
minute. That gave the red horse time to get on.
Then the race began between the blue and white
horse, that was now going again, and the red.
Everyone thought the red was beaten, but no.
Quietly and mysteriously the jockey of the other
horse was seen slipping out of the saddle. A
riderless blue-and-white horse galloped all over the
place, a blue horse was discovered rearing up
against a steeple, and the red horse strolled home
alone in the cool of the race. The way those
horses helped each other was a wonderful lesson in
the kindness of animals to animals, as animals. .
I really think it is about time to air again
afew of the interminable grievances every
woman has against the way the Randwick
Races are run. Nobody ever gets served in
the tea-rooms until the next race is just
about starting. Everyone is worked up to a wild
fume, and the people who don’t rush out in mad-
ness and put their money on the wrong horse, gulp
their scalding tea down in sad silence, and eat
sandwiches off a saucer with an air of resignation
that hasn’t the slightest effect on those it aims at.
On Boxing Day a party of country people, come
to have a good time, sat at the same table with me.
After the usual fatiguing struggle for a waiter’s
eye they managed to get themselves heard.
“ Strawberries and cream, please, for six.” “No
strawberries. No cream.” Dismay of country
people. “Ices for six then.” “Noh’ices.” More
dismay. A big leap downward is noticeable in
the next order. “Coffee and buns for six.” “No
coffee. No buns.” The head of the table lost his
temper then, and was heard demanding a whisky
and soda while the others thought it out. “No
whisky. No soda.” “Well, but blank —but
blank —what have you got?” “Tea and cyke,
tea and ’am sandwich.” I saw these six afterwards
looking as if they had been blowing themselves
out with tea and A.J.C. “cyke,” very subdued
and disconsolate, not a bit like the jolly people
who went to get strawberries.
And where in all this strangely-ordered world is
there a more marvellously bad method of catching
trams when the Races are over. To reach the
platform, you plunge through a bewildering mass
of cabs, ’buses, carriages, dog-carts, sulkies, and
when the platform is crowded with race-people
the trams begin to come along. They look as if
they were going to stop. Women dart forward,
stumble, shriek, fall back, and are fallen on. The
tram has moved on. Next tram repeats. Time
after time everybody makes a wild bolt forward,
and the tram glides mockingly on to the extreme
other end of the platform. At last, God in His
infinite knowledge knoweth why, a tram comes
along that stops. A thousand people all try to
get in at once. Crushing, swearing, screaming,
hitting, kicking. At last those who can get in,
and those who can’t, vow this will be the last time
—a vow consecrated, you know, to the racecourse.
And this goes on every meeting, and will go on
for ever, I suppose, and I wouldn’t trouble to
mention it only that it relieves a sense of injury
to see it in cold print.
The Pantomime—“ Red Riding Hood,” by
Bernard Espinasse—started on Boxing Night. A
brilliant house, of course. The gods piled up in
such masses that there seemed to be legs and arms
growing down from the ceiling ; stalls full, and a
good deal over ; dress-circle showing white arms in
great quantities. Very dressy was the d.c.,
although it was not “ Society.” Mrs. Rennie was
there in black, a conspicuous first-nighter. Miss
Edith Crane was in blue with a neck and sleeves
filled in with skin-colored lace, and had her hus-
band, Mr. Tyrone Power, alongside.
Have you noticed how, just about this time of
year, when Society retires for its annual com-
plexion-pick-up, another layer rises to the surface,
well-dressed, well-looking, unknown by name to
social scribes, but able to fill Society’s blank with-
out a wrinkle. Pantomime first night was
crowded with these. Looking round, you saw on
all sides pretty women and girls, and nice-looking
men, whose names are not written in the book
of Potts Point. Where do they come from, all
these nice unknown folk? Or are they with us
always, and merely obscured during the rest of
the year by the Nicest’s gaud and glitter.
One is apt to see last year’s pantomime through
a haze, and to forget entirely the year’s before,
but I am sure this is the most beautifully-dressed
one seen in Sydney for a very long time. The
frocks are not of the silver and gold order. Soft
silk is the chief feature of the dressing—tender-
colored, squeezable silk, flowered or plain, simple,
but making a delightful effect from the front.
Dolly Vane wears it in a little pale grey frock under
a white silk pinafore. (By the way, Red Riding
Hood’s frocks are merely an item. Nobody looks
at anything but her artless white socks, bare legs,
and ankle-strap shoes. White socks are a hard
test, but Dolly Vane stands them well.) The
ballet wears silk in scarlet and dead white, and in
marigold yellow and white chemisettes, and
underskirts of white silk. Speaking of good
material, what a waste Lauri is. The panto, gives
him about two chances to be funny, but he doesn’t
seem in a hurry to seize either. He is a most
quiet and serious-looking Lauri. A song of his is
introduced every night, and I am wondering if it
is that song that has stopped him from being
Lauri.
Carrie Moore, in our Lancers’ suit, rouses the
house to wild enthusiasm. Certainly, a sack of
potatoes dressed in khakee would send a thrill
round just now, but I think everyone is mindful of
the trim slight figure in this particular outfit.
Mark Foy made all the soldiers’ suits—several
hundreds of them; Gordon Highlanders, Dragoons,
Lancers—that figure in the great battle tableau.
Between acts ! An old panto, writer rushes up
to the author of “Red Riding Hood ” and slaps
him on the back. “Old fellow, let me”—he
begins, and the other’s face lights up expectantly
—“wish you a happy New Year,” he ends,
maliciously. It suggests that weird old joke,
ascribed to Disraeli—the reply to the begging
letter: “ I received your request for a donation
to your highly-deserving charity, one which has
always appealed to my warmest sympathies, and
have great pleasure in subscribing ” (here the foot
of the page was reached, and a wild-eyed treasurer
would turn over in frantic haste to see if it was
£lOO or £1000) “ myself, your very obedient
servant” (remarks from treasurer about a “ heart-
less upstart, ” etc.)
Sartorially, “The Power and the Glory,” now
running at the Lyceum, is rather a triumph for
the wicked young woman that Miss Roxy Barton
tries to seem to be. She flaunts most alluringly
in her third frock, which looks like a black and
white sprigged muslin made up over bright rose
color, with rose flounces showing up the right side
of the skirt, touches of black velvet, and a rose
toque with a black bird holding it off the forehead.
Ida Gresham, who is very badly treated in a well-
made brown print, is chiefly to be noticed for
the wonderful way she keeps her gold hair
smartly coiffured all through the days of
anguish, starvation, and illegal imprisonment.
Little Beatrice Holloway turns out to be one of
those rare anomalies, a good stage-child, with
lovely rounded cheeks and a red-brown mane, and
a most natural manner and voice. In a perform-
ance like this, when three of the women talk with
plums in their throats —and one of the plums is an
egg-plum—the sweet, quietly - produced young
voice was something to listen to. Miss Roland
Watts-Phillips isn’t billed as the heroine, but she
bravely sacrifices herself, all the same, to a crino-
line and green sateen flounces, and elastic-side
cloth boots, and tenders them all her fun as well.
As for the men, l am afraid, or should I say glad,
that you are not susceptible enough to fall a victim
to the most taking of a rather unattractive group,
the baker-boy—Mr. Tom Cosgrove—whose way of
saying, “’Ave a little common,” is one of the
light reliefs of this studiously ordinary melo-
drama.
In dying moments of the N.S.W. session
Ms.L.A. relieved their souls by offering moral
support to Colonel Neild re his alleged volunteer-
ing for the Transvaal. Hideous stories of Boer
marksmanship and ferocity and their deadly
preference of officers of rank (especially men of
tall, commanding physique) were told with studied
unconsciousness among little groups when the
Kernel happened to be within earshot. Bogus
wires came down freely “from headquarters ” to
the effect that the Paddington warrior’s offer was
accepted, with assurances that the welfare of the
wives and families of the slain would be duly
attended to. The victim of this thoughtfulness
took it with the same resolute calm with which he
takes the whole military business, and responded
to the universal congratulations on his chances of
distinction by reciting “A Tale of Balaclava”—
from the Nocent Waterspout series—at the break-
ing-up.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ The holiday evening crowds were better worth
attention than a pantomime. In the tram a ten-
year-old talked war with his father, who was con-
voying Sonny and the younger ones on a tour of
the shops. Young Hopeful barracked for the
Australian Contingents and their khakee suits.
Dad preferred the red-coats of Old England.
‘Wouldn’t it be a have for the Bow-ers,’ said the
youngster after a thoughtful pause, ‘ if they mis-
took our men for sheep ? Tlieir clothes are just
the color of wool. And how would it be to come
over the kop-gees on their four legs ? ’ Poor inno-
cent ! how local fire-eaters would resent the sug-
gestion that they should go into battle disguised
as wethers !
“In most of the big shops, at the entrance to
‘Yuletide caves,’ &e., was inscribed, ‘Children
not admitted without parents or guardians.’ Rue-
ful ragamuffins stood around wistfully eyeing this
testimony to the truth of the saying, ‘ the gates of
heaven are narrow.’ Yet most of them got in
somehow. ‘Take me in with you, lydy,’ they
whispered, and were seldom refused. Once inside,
they were apt to precipitate enquiry by testing
mouth-organs and trying bikes. A girl, who had
escorted three or four successive batches of urchins
into the haven where they fain would be, at last
saw a shop-walker inspecting her with a suspicious
glare. The flustered shepherdess summoned her
black sheep by a warning whisper. The queer-
eyed one, whom his chums called ‘ Squinney, ’ was
equal to the occasion: ‘Mummer,’ he squeaked
ostentatiously, ‘ Ginger’s punchin’ me ! Clip ’im
on the jore, mummer ! ’
“Saturday before Christmas, blind men were
playing like good ’uns on accordeon and cornet
respectively, up Smith Street. Tired women
handed over their thrippennies to the sightless
ones and trudged back home instead of tram-
ming it. Poverty and generosity.apparently run
in double harness. Just now, weary wage-earners,
who would never be invited to Govt. House if they
filled a page of the visitors’ book daily with the
name of ‘J. Smith,’ spent their holidays at home,
and devoted their holiday money to the alleged
needs of prospective soldiers’ widows. But the
crowd who grab gubernatorial favours and hospi-
tality, and pose as impassioned Imperialists, trot
off to the Paris Exhibition and knock down their
cheques without a thought of the victims crushed
in the machinery of their beloved Empire.
“Juvenile Melbourne was ‘breaking up,’ and
showing off for many weary days before Plmn-
puddingness. Everywhere one went one met
battalions of neat little girls and boys with un-
comfortably clean collars on, bearing calf and
morocco-bound prize books. On one day there
were reports of speech days and prize lists of
forty-four private schools (mostly called ‘colleges
or ‘ academies ’) in the Argus. All these are
highly ‘select’ institutions whose young lady
pupils mostly speak of ‘mykin’ kyke.’ The danc-
ing academies, painting classes, and so on, also
had their shivoos. At one fashionable dancing
place one was amused to hear the lydy instructress
command her pupils to ‘ put yer ’ands on yer ’ips
and ’op.’ Which they did with much grace.
Education is certainly making great strides —or
’ops—in Melbourne.
“Uncle Tom Brassey knows a few things
and falls off many more. He has fixed his
departure for the day the second Victorian con-
tingent leaves, whereby in years to come the
incidents will get mixed up, and he will be able to
state that 20,000 people lined the streets when he
left and the cheers shook the mortgage on Fink’s
Buildings—if there is one. A mighty programme
has been drawn up for the old man's send-off.
The vice-regal carriage, with its noble owner eii her
inside or falling out, will be driven from Guvment-
ouse over Prince’s Bridge, then round the corner,
over the adjacent piece of waste ground and down
to the Morgue, where His Excellency under the
auspices of the coroner and his orderly will em-
bark in a dingy, and scull himself downstream to
the Falls Bridge, amid the sing-song of the school
children, who are to be posted along the bank with
life buoys and grappling irons. The Sunbeam,
lying at anchor off Britton’s Hotel, will then be
boarded by its illustrious owner, who is to clear
out of Victoria via Coode’s Ditch and the
abattoirs. This may not be the exact programme,
but it is as near as the writer can remember.
“An intermittent affliction descended upon
Melbourne the other day in the person of an old
man from waybaclt who wants twenty pounds for
a horse with ingrowing hoofs and a sort of
spiral movement in his off hind leg. There are
hundreds of men in and about Melbourne who
want <£2o for a horse, but this man has made him-
self conspicuous by merit and sheer force of
character. He is an old man, almost as old as his
horse ; he is as obtuse as a ton of putty, and under-
stands nothing but that the Government wants
horses for <£2o to mount the second contingent on,
and he wants £2O for a horse. That is enough for
him,, and his nag, having been refused by the
military assessor as fit for nothing but glue, he
believes there is some mistake somewhere, and is
moving the powers. He parades his pathetic old
crock all over the place, seeking influential persons,
and when he finds his influential person he corners
him, and clings like a disease, and maunders about
the qualities of that horse as a charger and a
picturesque attribute to an army till night falls.
He has chased the Premier and all his Ministers;
he has interviewed the Mayor and the Town
Council, and waylaid Tom Brassey and Fitz
Gibbon, and the W.C.T.U., and is still actively
hawking his horse in the city and suburbs. Now,
whenever an old man towing a limp horse
comes working round a Melbourne corner, stocks
fall and great men are seen escaping in seven
directions.
“Captain Cecil is the first of the A.D.C.’s to
take up his bed and walk from these parts. That
sleeping member of Uncle Tom’s Cabin spent six
months recovering from a broken leg and another
six months in chewing his toothpick and wonder-
ing how the awfully beastly nuisance of an acci-
dent had happened. The little Aide took his
small self off by the mail-boat on Tuesday—un-
honored and unsung. With all his faults—or
want of them—he commanded this scribe’s admir-
ation because he had a habit of getting out of
riding the Brassey back wheel as often as possible.
And if he is sent to S. Africa, as he hopes, he
ought to be safe from all intentional injury. No
intelligent Boer would knowingly fire at anybody
who looks so kind and gentle and harmless.
“ The Madden family will treat the Frankston
folk to no more platform tricks. In their ap-
proaching deputy vice-royalty, stage exhibitions
are to be taboo. Once upon a time Guy Madden
—the bulky son and heir —sang a Coster song at a
cheerful local entertainment; Miss Lesly Madden
did skirt-dances in the good cause of charity, and
the flower of the flock turned a pretty profile to
the Frankstonians, as she played accompaniments.
But never again.
“Mr. William Zeal, the President of the Vic-
torian Legislative Council, sent out a Xmas card
of his own devising, with a Union Jack on it, and
some patter-rotic doggerel. A booklet from
Brassey has not made an appearance this year.
Last year the old gentleman sent out a little
pamphlet bound in imitation parchment consisting
of his favorite bits from authors —chiefly the
hackneyed passages in Shakespeare. He had it
printed in London. Those who received copies do
not seem to have valued them much, for the writer
saw a couple for sale second-hand at sixpence each
the other day—imitation autograph and all!

PEOPLE YOU KNOW.
No. 2.—The Postman.
A rural postman writes : “ I desire to add this
unsoh'eited testimony to the sterling- qualities of
Dr. Tibbies’ Yi-Cocoa. I am a rural postman,
and exposed to all kinds of weather. I have used
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa daily this last eleven
months, and I believe that I can do my work with
less fatigue since I have used it. I can honestly
recommend to my fellow postmen Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa for its sustaining qualities.”
Of late years many new manufactures, rejoicing
in a multiplicity of names, have been brought
before the public notice, but there are none which
have bounded into favor so readily as has Dr.
Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa. Throughout the country its
sales have increased with great rapidity, and an
article which only a few years back was unheard
of has now become one of the most popular
beverages of the people.
Merit, and merit alone, is what is claimed for
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa, and the proprietors are
prepared to send to any reader who names The
Bulletin (a postcard will do) a dainty sample tin
of Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa free and post-paid.
There is no magic in all this. It is a plain,
honest, straightforward offer. It is done to intro-
duce the merits of Vi-Cocoa into every home. Dr.
Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa, as a concentrated form of
nourishment and vitality, is invaluable ; nay,
more than this, for to all who wish to face the
strife and battle of life with greater endurance
and more sustained exertion, it is absolutely
indispensable.
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa can be obtained from all
chemists, grocers, and stores, or from Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa, Limited, 269 George-street, Sydney.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Reported in M.L. papers that George Reid
has sailed for the Bluff. Fancy George wanting
to go to any more bluff than he has already.
Tom Price, who succeeds Batchelor as
leader of S. A. Labor Party, was a stonecutter on
the staff of laborers who erected S.A. Parliament
House.
The lost and said to be recently-discovered
balloon-explorer, Andree, was a man of magnifi-
cent physique ; whereas his companion, Eckholm,
was small, feeble and elderly. They dare in all
shapes!
A prisoner named Nelson, at Northam
(Westralia) nearly succeeded in suiciding the other
day by pounding his head against the gaol wall.
In fact, it isn’t quite certain yet that he hasn t
succeeded.
General Oronje had one son killed by the
British in the war of independence. A second was
killed by Jameson’s Raiders, and the third and
last fell before Mafeking. The childless old man
—he is very old now —doesn’t love the Englander,
and what wonder.
The new S.A. Education Minister,
Batchelor, began his own battle as a State school
pupil-teacher. Premier Holder went further than
the pupil-teacher stage; he had got fairly high
up among head-teachers when he stepped out into
journalism, and so into politics.
George Thornton, M.L.C., was 79, on
Dec. 28, having been born in Macquarie-street,
Sydney, in 1820. He was M.L.A. over 40
years ago, and has been 22 years in the Upper
House. Also, he was Minister for Mines for six
weeks, in ’B5, and was once Agent-General.
S.A. Premier Holder does not propose to
give up “local preaching” while he is in office.
In fact, one of his very first public appearances
after stepping into Kingston’s shoes (Sudden
Solomon really hadn’t time to get a pair) was to
deliver a sermon. He is a Methodist, and even
in the pulpit can’t help juggling with figures.
Sir Walter Hely-Hutchinson, the present
Governor of Natal, stayed at Sydney Govt. House
in 1874. He was then the Hon. Walter Hely-
Hutchinson, 25 years of age, and was understood
to be learning the business of a Governor. He
accompanied Hercules Robinson to Fiji when the
Islands were annexed, and was asked to assist the
private secretary with some clerical work, but he
found the weather too warm and didn’t. But he
was the son of an earl, and influence helped the
tired man along to his present breathless elevation.
ON HTS OATH.
LONG-SUFFERING J.P. (to witness): “ Why,don't
you answer my questions. Are you afraid of
telling an untruth?”
SALVATION HARMY HOFFICER: “Oh, dear no!
Hallelujah! ”
The latest news about “Oliver Bainbridge,’
the wild-haired world-traveller who was never out
of Australia till recently, is that he took a Maori
girl for a drive at Rotorua (M.L.); that the
native she wasn’t formally married to hit him on
the curly locks with a club when he came back;
and that the brown man was fined £5 and costs.
Prahran (Victoria) has at least one live
oity father. A bitter and needlessly prolonged
correspondence was going on between a ratepayer
and the municipal officials re the alleged stoppage
of a drain, till Councillor Arnott grew angry, and
making for the scene of trouble with a broom he
put things right personally in about ten minutes.
The authorities refuse permission to the
Duke of Connaught to go to the war :
To Africa Con. would have fared
To share the soldier’s lot.
Folks think he ought—he could be spared—
But all the powers have declared
That Con. ought not.
William Pearman, “common seaman,”
had his foot completely torn off by a rope, on a
Yarra (Melb.) steamer, the other day. Carried
to the hospital, he bore his pain smilingly. “The
pluckiest patient ever seen in the hospital,” say
the doctors. But William is not “ A Soldier of
the Queen,” and so his gameness is neglected even
by Bill Purtell, the penny-poet.
Sam Mauger, who succeeded John Han-
cock as M.L. A. for the bone-mill city of Footscray
(Vic.) has been a hard grafter in many quarters.
He is an uncle, it he isn’t actually a father, of
the present Factories Act and anti-sweating legis-
lation in Victoria. But alas! he isn’t a cheerful
democrat like punter ’Ancock, and he is painfully
and wearily anxious about his immortal soul.
Victorian ex-Minister for Railways
Williams: “ Mr. Shiels was kicked off the
Treasury benches on a former occasion like a dumb
dead dog.” This was presumably to prevent the
public imagining that Shiels was kicked off like a
voluble dead dog talking at the top of his voice.
But do they kick off dead dogs where Williams
comes from ? That unwholesome kind of object is
best removed with a long fork and Condy’s fluid.
Said in some quarters that Theo. Fink
really left the M'Lean (Vic.) Government through
a belated rising of his wrath at the patronising
tone adopted towards him by Treasurer Shiels,
who called him “an intelligent man.” To em-
phasise virtues supposed to be taken for granted
is a minor form of insult. Shiels’ letter seemed to
suggest that it was necessary to mention Fink’s
intelligence because it wasn’t visible.
Brewer Tooth, who tips £IO,OOO to the
Boer-slaughter fund, was a well-known figure in
political cireles about 20 years ago. He was a pious
sort of beer-seller and thought the next greatest
institution to his own brewery for the amelioration
of man was the Church of England. He once
stood for Parliament at East Sydney but was
badly beaten. It was probably well for him. It
has to be dreadfully bad beer that doesn’t pay
better than politics.
Dan O’Connor was charged in a Sydney
police-court the other day with calling at the
Australian Gas Co.’s office and booting a cler.
because the Gas Cos. cut off the gas from “ Tara.’
(Incidentally there was fine material for an Irish
poem on “The gas that once through Tara’s
halls.”) Dan explained that he merely put up his
foot in self-defence; but admitted that when the
Gas Cos. threatened to bring a policeman he defied
the co., and said that the police, “knowing his
pre-eminent respectability,” would never arrest
him. After that the case was dismissed.

bar Holder, who is 49, are between 50 and

HIS TROUBLES.
FATMAN : “Look here, my man,
could you eat one of those pies?”

The teetotal cranks of Maoriland have not
carried prohibition in any new district, and their
representation in the House is diminished as the
result of the late general election. The outlook
was, however, so gloomy that Millionaire Martin
Kennedy sold out his .£40,000 interest in Staples’
Brewery, Wellington, just before the election
and local option poll. The proverbial cloud has
since come o’er his brow. Ten per cent, gone and
no good investments offering ! Kennedy, like
many others, miscalculated the strength of the
Liberal-cum-Beer party.
Alexander Sutherland is back again in
Melbourne after hobnobbing with the learned
pundits of England. His big book on “ The
Development of the Moral Instinct” won him the
entree into the society of the “thinkers” with
bald skulls and bulging emu egg foreheads, for it
has been heralded as a work of philosophical
importance on the Spencer Darwinian line.
Sutherland is dark, slight, shapely and vigorous-
minded ; and he knows so much that the hairs of
his head might be catalogued like a library. He
lives a solitary life at Mount Martha, with nature
and his books and his theories.
“ S.S.” : Your indignant “ Melbourne
Britisher” is hard-pressed for an excuse for
English behaviour in Africa when he cites poor
old Kruger’s countenance to put a good face on
the attempted steal. Kruger is not a beauty,
according to the idea of the Johnnie and the little
miss, but his mug is far from being the mass of
hideousness described by “ Britisher.” The face is
heavy, but it has, besides strength, a dint or two
that make it preferable, for a person seeking a
companionable individual, to that of Queen Vic-
toria with the ominous droop in the corners of the
mouth. Besides, we get Kruger, like Cromwell,
with all his warts, whereas the retoucher does his
best to make smooth and pleasant the face of the
Queen.
It is amusing to find Shiels and McColl
together in the same Cabinet. When Shiels was
Premier and was defending himself against a no-
confidence motion, he had prepared for McColl
one of those smart, impromptu retorts which he is
in the habit of brewing and bottling for use when
required. McColl has a small treble voice. An-
ticipating an interjection from him in the course
of his speech Shiels had purposed retorting: “ Do
I hear the squeak of the trapped rat ? ” It would
have been somewhat appropriate in McColl’scase.
But it went astray. W. G. Carter, then member
for Williamstown, made an interjection. Shiels
mistook the voice, and out, pat, came the retort :
“Do I hear the squeak of the trapped rat?’
“No,” roared Carter, in a deep bass, “you hear
the bark of the British bull-d. g.” But Shiels has
the trapped rat now—in his own rat-trap.
William Pember Reeves, who is reported
to be resigning the Maoriland Agent-Generalship
in order to take up literature and politics in
England, did not get his present position without
a severe struggle. The late Patrick Buckley—
Attorney-General in the Seddon Govt, at the time
Reeves was Minister for Labor and Education —
wanted the appointment badly, but Reeves
threatened to raise Cain if he didn’t get the billet.
Buckley also proposed to raise Cain and all Cain’s
relatives. There was a long tug of-war; but
providentially a judge died and gave an oppor-
tunity for placing Buckley on the Supreme Court
Bench, thus leaving Reeves’s course to London
clear. Poor Buckley only sat on one assize before
he died.
“ Malle,” on a British general :
Re Wolseley’s reputation (Bulletin 30/12/’99). Writer
has served under him, and has heard his alleged reputa-
tion freely discussed by officers and men, who, as a rule,
think little of it. One V.C.’d Scotchman, who won his
decoration in the Indian Mutiny, summed him up : “ He’s
a damned good commissariat-general, and that's all can
be said for him.” His “ great ” victory at Tel-el-Kebir
was simply the result of surprise and the bull-headed
pluck of the men he commanded. In the Nile expedition,
he waited until the river was too low before deciding on
that route; result, Khartoum had fallen before the
British relief-column could get near it. Wolseley is very
stand-off, and his teetotalism makes him unpopular. He
refuses to serve out grog during a campaign ; enteric
fever and dysentery killed more men than the enemy in
Egypt, and many of the doctors thought that the usual
“ tots ” would have reduced the mortality. The stored
rum was afterwards lost in a big fire at Cairo, and it was
whispered that the fire had covered a considerable de-
ficiency. The idol of Tommy Atkins is “ Bobs.”
Abdul Wade, the Afghan manager of the
well-ad v" rtised and prosperous Bourke (N.S.W.)
Carrying Co., is a short, sturdy, Jewish-
looking man with a pleasant smile and
magnificent teeth. He has made thou,
sands of pounds by the development of
the'camel carrying business in
N.S.W. and S.A., though but
a few years ago he worked on
a Vic. farm for a few shil-
lings a month. He relates
that during his early strug-
gles he was engaged by a
fellow - countryman to go
hawking in Melbourne.
His first day out a charit-
able lady, sympathising
with his youth and his
heavy burden, bought
nearly the whole of his
merchandise, which
meant to him a commis-
sion of 30s.— more money
than he had ever had in
his life before, at one
time. As he left her
door, his heart swell-
ing with pleasure, he
heard his benefac-
tress say, “Poor
fellow ! ” It then
broke in on him
that a feeling of
pity only on the
part of the pur-
chaser had brought
about his good for-
tune, and nothing
would again in-
duce him to
carry a basket.
“We Afghans
don’t want to get
a living by awaken-
ing the pity of women,’’concluded Abdul,
“ so I went to work that enabled me to
feel that what I earned I really
did work for.” Wade is married
to an Australian girl, but
is a strict Mohamme-
dan, and out of his own
purse supports mosques
at Adelaide and Bourke.
And his early vow has gone
to pieces, because he gets his
living out of a concern whose
methods have awakened the pity of almost every
woman who knows anything about them.
As showing how even the hardest-headed
citizen can harbor a bee in his bonnet : When the
N.S.W. Soudan “patriotic” fund was in full
swing, and after reading that millionaire Tyson—
of whom the old man was always jealous—had
given £lOOO, the late squatter Locky M‘Bean, of
Woorooma, walked round the room muttering
“Ef the gude leddy (the Queen) wants it, Ah’ll
give her a thoosan’—Ah’ll guv mair nor thot, puir
leddy. Hech ! but Ah’ll guv her a cheque thot’ll
show urn." He kept up this wrestle all the after-
noon, but evidently the old Adam triumphed, as
hiscontemplated contribution was never sent along
Bishop Samuel Thornton, of Ballarat
(“ Samuel Ballarat ”), regards himself as a man
with a large, liberal mind because he holds that it
is not altogether an unforgiveable sin to work on
the Sabbath. Walking on the path surrounding
Lake Wcndouree one Sunday, and seeing nobody
but a very small boy about, Sam “Ballarat”
began to put his pet retriever through its facings,
jumping the animal over a stick, etc., etc. Pre-
sently he noticed that the small boy, a very prim
youth wearing a black knickerbocker-suit and an
expression of severity far beyond his years, was
closely observing him, and, thinking to edify the
youngster, he made the dog repeat his tricks.
Then turning to his audience of one he ejaculated:
“ There, my boy ; isn’t he a clever dog?” The
boy eyed the bishop more sternly still. “ Sir,”
he said, with preternatural' dignity, “ I think
you forget what day it is !’’ After that he
walked off austerely, leaving a crumpled Yiishop
on the gravel path.
Evangelist Moody—Sankey’s mate —has
gone over to join the old ones. Moody came
from Northfield (Massachusetts), and claimed to
be a direct descendant of another famous evan-
gelist, Parson Samuel Moody, of York (Mass.).
Parson Sam Moody will be interesting to Aus-
tralians in these contingent days as senior chap-
lain of the army of 4000 men which the New Eng-
land provinces sent to take Louisbourg in Cape
Breton (then the strongest place in America) from
the French. Sam Moody carried among his
scanty baggage an axe with which to “ hew down
the altars of Antichrist, and break in pieces the
images in the church of Louisbourg.” When,
without the help of a single regular soldier, an
engineer, or anyone who had ever seen a siege,' or
even a regular fortress, with a country store-
keeper named William Pepperell for their
general, they actually took the town, though it
was defended by a regular French regiment and
a body of Canadian militia, Moody went to work
with his axe in the church, and would have
wrecked the interior if some cooler-headed victors
had not persuaded him to preach instead of chop-
ping. This capture of Louisbourg is memorable
as being the real prelude to the War of Indepen-
dence. It was the first time the provincials
really pitted themselves against European regular
soldiers, and beat them. The late Moody, of the
firm of Moody and Sankey, had very little, after
all, to recommend him but his bull voice and his
long-dead ancestor.

Aboriginalities.
Silly letters often go from parent to
teacher, but the teacher writes rubbish sometimes.
For instance, Mrs. Skimmi’lk, a dairy-farmer,
writes to Miss Beltham :
Dear Miss Beltem.—l am varry angery at way you
eained my Willie remember he’s only a child not forteen
or you wouldent get him. When hes stubben an says he
wont do what you tell him, you wont to (“ coax”) him
tell him youll bring him something from Sydney a
Chrismas. That’s how I get him to do things. I’m
supprised at your ignorants not known how to spell my
Elcys name, fancy you spelling it Elsie, you ought of
known that without been told. When you keep my
children in at dinner-time you wont to give them there
dinner first, thats what Id do. anyhow keeping them
in is black tack an if I dont see an improvement after this
111 learn them myself, yours respectably, Mrs. Skimmilk.
Whereupon la Beltham :
Dear Madam. —Many thanks for hints. Now one lot of
good advice deserves another : Always milk your cow
from the milking-side, i.e., the “off” side. The first
thing to do on sitting down is to wash your hands w r ith
milk-squirts, being careful to hold the bucket (between
vour knees) directly under the drip; and as milk is
good for the complexion, it won’t hurt to wash your face
while you’re about it. Next dig your head well into
Daisy’s flank, and then—why, then—you milk. I would.
Never place your milking-stool immediately behind your
cow, for sundry reasons, one of which is that j r ou couldn’t
dig your head in so comfortably ; and though Daisy is
mostly amiable enough on the milking-side, she is always
frightfully sarcastic behind. You would no doubt label
her sarcasm “ black tack.” When you feel sure that
Daisy has “ let down ” all her milk, you hit her on the
nose with the leg-rope, and request Strawberry to “ bail-
up.” The procedure will be the same as in Daisy’s case,
except that you needn’t wash your face or hands —they
will do very nicely till next milking-time. If you find no
improvement in your complexion after a course of this
treatment, you ought to boil it. I’m sure I would.
-Gratefully'yours, Letticb Beltham. P.S.—Love to
Elky.”

“ Gh>be ” : Making for Wilcannia, I have
swagged it from Wentworth to Menindie ; and
called at every selection and station en route, but
could get no tucker. “We don’t give rations,”
was the invariable station-answer, and the selectors
had nothing themselves but bread and tea. Sat
down and meditated, with the result thatl put on
my best clothes, collar, necktie, and cap, tucked
my trousers into my socks, threw away rest of my
swag, and took the road again. Struck a station
at 6 o’clock, asked for the manager, and told him
I was a literary man on a bicycle tour, and that
my wheel had broken down a few miles up the
road. Had dinner, bed and breakfast next morn-
ing, after which I was driven eight miles to the
nearest pub. to wait for the coach. I walked that
day to the mail-change and told the boss of accom-
modation house that my mates were coming up the
river in a boat taking photos, and that I had no
money with me. 1 was asked to have dinner and
stayed all night. Next night I put up with a
selector, and next again struck a station about 1C
miles from Wilcannia, where I “put up” the
bicycle yarn again. Was most hospitably enter-
ained, and was driven to Wilcannia next morn-
ing. So much for appearances !

Cereal: Ido not know
of any part of Australia where
dhourra—Egyptian rice corn —is
grown. Says an American expert:
Rice corn is rapidly coming to the front in Kansas as a
food for man and cattle. It is like sorghum, and the
grain resembles rice. Analysis by Prof. Patrick of the
State University shows it to be superior to corn in its
nutritive qualities. The yield ranges from 15 to 40
bushels per acre, regardless, almost, of rain or drought.
Only the heads are harvested, and are threshed like
wheat. When ground it is like corn and wheat mixed.
The advent of rice corn will make assurance doubly sure
for an ample supply oQiutritious feed.

INSTRUCTIONS.
WANTED FLEAS 1
Fleas are found on mammals and birds, in the hair or
feathers. They are also found in their nests or holes.
As soon as their host get 3 cold, the fleas leave it.
If the host be small, place it in a small box or linen bag.
with a few drops of chloroform or benzine. The fleas wil
then be found dead on the bottom of the box, or among
the hair, when the same is turned back.
If the flea be touched with a small brush, moistened
with spirit, chloroform or benzine, it will stick to it,
readily floating off in the tube of preservative. The best
preservative is 50% of alcohol. Acetic acid can with ad-
vantage be substituted for this, when the specimens have
not got to travel a long way. The action of the acid,
however, destroys the corks of the tubes in a short time.
Each tube of fleas should be labelled with the date,
locality, and name of the host from which the fleas were
taken.' If the name of the host on which the fleas were
found is not known to the collector, the host itself should
be sent with the tube. A number corresponding to one
on the specimen should he attached to the tube. Speci-
mens sent for identification only will, of course, always
be returned. If it is impossible to send the host, the
name of the person possessing it should be given, in order
to apply to him for the name. Each tube should contain
the flea’s from one host only. A good method of record-
ing-data is a label written in pencil and put inside the
tube.
Large mammals cannot well be treated with chloroform
or benzine. If the hair of these, however, be turned back
shortly after death, the live fleas will be seen running
about. These can be killed by touching them with a
brush moistened with spirit, chloroform, or benzine.
Small mammals can be treated in the same manner as
large ones, if the first method is found to be too
cumbrous.
Another very good way to catch fleas living on mice
and other small mammals, is to place the live mouse in a
bag. The animal should then be killed by breaking if s
neck from the outside of the bag, and searched as before.
Traps should only be used in which the mice are caught
alive.
Bats, rodents, and small mammals generally, are all
good hosts.
As many specimens as possible should be sent, there
frequently being several species of flea on one host.
All communications to be addressed to
THE HON. N. CHARLES ROTHSCHILD,
Tring Park, Tring, Hurts.
(England).

“ Treb.” : aboriginal superstitions
(“ E. 5.5.,” 8., l/7/’99). Purchased from W.A.
black what he termed a “gun-kok,” a piece of
very heavy black wood, flat on one side, convex
on other, and about 2ft. 6in. long, with hole in
smallest end. It is carved all over—the flat side
with representation of a snake—and if whirled
round the head with a string in the hole makes a
loud, humming noise. The warrigal said the
carving was done with a piece of quartz, and en-
joined me in an impressive whisper not on any
account to show it to a gin or she would certainly
die. This injunction, however, I made a point o.
disregarding—without any evil results.

When drought had dried up all the herb-
age on Mrs. O’Dillon’s selection, she sent the
cattle—about 20 head—travelling down Barwon
way for grass and water, Jack, the son and heir,
in charge. They got on well till Jack struck
Walgett and a “ two-up ” school, when he speedily
went broke and started to play up the stock.
“Here’s a cow says tails. You’re out another
muddy cow. My kip 1 Here’s a cow says I can
head ’em. Heads another ruddy cow.” But he
sent a truthful account to headquarters : “ Deer
muther we had dam bad luck with the cattle, i
loosed old Straubry and Bauly and aight stears.
i was tailing them on foot round Wolget when
they started goin’ an’ I couldent head them.
“ Werrimee ” : One difference between
the kangaroo and the wallaby : kangaroos have
no defined tracks leading to water or feeding-
ground. The wallaby always uses one little
track, beside which wallaby-shooters often wait
for the game while the dogs are hunting; for a
hunted wallaby always beats along his own track.
Hence the phrase, “on the wallaby”—never “on
the kangaroo ” —as
applied to travel-
lers who follow the
devious ways
through the
bush

Black Peter was a good shot, and very
proud of it. Patou, of Malton, entrusted him
with a breechloader, and Billoo, the home of
Peter’s youth, knew him no more. Thus did h<
explain the wherefore of his defection: “Missa
Elton no g&od ; he give it me ‘ mutchleload ’ gun.
Misser Paton he all right—gib it broke gun.’
“ Broke gun? ” I asked ; “ what name that fella?”
“ Oh, you break’em dis way” (and Peter went
through the motions.) “Putin two fella you np
one inside, mend ’em again, an’ shoot kangaroo.
Break ’em again, put in two fella more young one.
mend ’em an’ shoot again. My word !—budgere-
fella gun that one 1 ”
“Snell”: The capacity of the adult
brahmapootra rooster is imperfectly understood.
Many may have thought to utilise him as a fog-
horn, but it remained for my friend Brown to
employ him as watchdog and general caretaker.
Brown’s brahma is a tall, austeie bird, with the
hauteur of a vice-chancellor, and the ungainly ap-
pearance of along bony boy who has outgrown his
clothes. About twelve months back, Spike, the
rooster, developed an extraordinary truculence,
and now if left at large will attack anything
brought before him, living or dead, a tomcat or a
chimney—he is not a bit particular. It was the
sight of a Metropolitan Board of Works employe
kneeling in the yard with his hand up a hole that
tempted Spike from righteousness and the paths
of peace. The opportunity was not to be resisted,
and the brahma went for that man, beak and claw,
and for two minutes there was a whirling cataract
of battle in the excavation. Then Brown
responded to the shrieks of the victim, and tore
his brahmapootra from the unhappy laborer,
who was kicked so full of punctures that he needed
medical attendance to the value of £7 75., for
which Brown paid. Since that encounter Spike
has never recovered his temper, and his lust for
blood is so genuine and his attack so impetuous
and overpowering that it is dangerous for any
moving thing to venture into Brown’s back yard
when the bird is loose. Brown now keeps him on
a chain like a dog, and when small boys guy him
from the fence he fights his kennel with ungovern-
able fury, and litters the neighborhood with
feathers. If Banjo will trot out Saltbush Bui s
emu, Brown is willing to match his towering fowl
against it for drinks.

hear from him. — Xerxes.
A paragraph from the history of Bulla* ook.
“ 80-peep ” to The Bulletin
It was the Holy Romans of Navigator's, and not the
Hibernians of Bungaree proper, who bombarded 0. E.
Jones with bad eggs at that memorable Ballarat East
election (“Ben Sun,” B. 18/11/’9B), and hence the appella-
tion, “ Bungaree savages,” came to be applied to the
Bullarook people generally. The first batch of immi-
grants to Navigator’s settled there in the early 50’s, and,
as each settler owned about two acres of land and half-a-
dozen pigs, the assaults and batteries and “ coort” cases
caused by the depredations of Hoolahan’s “grunters”
upon Riley’s potato-patch may readily be imagined. On
the arrival of the second batch of immigrants the resi-
dents of Navigator’s went en masse to Ballarat to wel-
come their “ frinds,” and, after a mighty carouse, the
lot started for Navigator’s, but fell into such an almighty
shindy by the way that nearly the whole brigade turned
up at'the police-court next day to answer a charge, of
riotous conduct. The plea of “Oi wasn’t wan o’ thim,
yer ’Aimer,” from each battered and bandaged and
plastered warrior, made even the presiding magistrate
laugh, Times have changed ; the railway now runs
through Riley’s potato-patch; but to this day the faces to
be seen on Navigator's platform mostly exhibit the un-
mistakable map of Ireland.

“SPELL-OH!”
W’en the taste in Life turns sour,
An’ yer best girl goes to hell—
Spell-oh !
If- yer feels like killin’ ’er,
An’ per’aps the bloke as well
Spell-oh !
Don’t you paint no townships red :
Pass it slowly through yer ’ead—
“ S'pose I’d married ’er instead ! ”
An’ spell-oh !
W’en yer work ’as got yer down,
An’ ’s a-worryin’ of yer, like—
Spell-oh !
W ’ea yer Read’s all muddled up,
An’ yer brains is Out on strike —
Spell-oh !
W’en the bullicks loon’t “gee-back ”
Tho’ yer cuts ’em into steak,
Stash the ’ole go’bli’me fake—
And Spell-oh !

“ Malle ” : We were practical anti-aliens
on Bingellic; and Ah Cow, who grew tobacco and
cabbagee, was the only recognised pagan on the
“crick.” One night we made a ghost out of a
pumpkin and candle, planted it outside his door
and pelted stones on his roof. Cow called out in
a deprecating tone, “ Wha foil ! ” Answer, more
stones. Then he opened the door an inch, poked
a gun out and fired at things in general. Next he
saw our ghost, and giving a yell like a lost soul
he barricaded the door with kerosene tins and all
his other furniture, and proceeded to burn joss-
sticks and let off sacred crackers until anyone but
a Chow would have clicked. We could see the
fleas and things prising up the bark on the roof
trying to get out. Next day, Ah Cow shifted to
an alleged hut further down creek, and erected a
ring fence of joss-sticks round his old residence to
keep the Evil One yarded up.

Chow’s inhumanity to Chow : Fossicker,
noticing Sling Shot’s humpy burnt down (S.S.
had been “ too sick ” for months): “ Where’s old
my countlyman two-tlee week agobulnem housee
eboolvting b’longin’ Sling Shot. You sahpee
him close up cooked-no walk-my countlyman
no talkee him ; pattern way longa bushee, bym-
bye die.” Fossicker: “ H’m ; rough on old Sling.

“ Long-Un” concerning village settle-
men t s .
The S.A. village settlements nowadays pin their
faith chiefly to wheat-crops. The average settler
doesn’t think much of the communistic business,
and wants a block of his own to work on. Govt,
had an idea of this kind a year or two back, and
surveyed 10-acre blocks for the settler’s very own
—after he’d started paying rent—but these blocks
are still untenanted, and the disheartened villager
says “ It’s all for Johnny ” —meaning a benevolent
Govt., to whom they owe over £BO,OOO. Things were
shockingly mismanaged in the earlier days of these
places : channels (cemented) were laid down (or
rather up), and when it was found that water
wouldn’t run up-hill, a Govt, surveyor’s services
were called upon to run levels, and the mismanager
ninny ignored these, with beautiful results. Was
passing admiring remarks to a settler re an im-
mense bell, rigged up to waken the slumbersome
settler, and also to call him in to smoko and
afternoon tea, &c. The latter remarked, “Yus,
that bell cost £lB, and when the Commissioner
came up and saw it he s wore ; and he ain’t much
of a man for langwidge either.” Asked a horny-
looking Scotchman (a manager) the reason some
of his men had seceded from the Association.
“ Well,” he remarked, “ they couldn’t agree with
the Commissioner. The fact is, they ain’t eddi-
cated up to this equality business yet; they mostly
want as little as they can get for as much as they
gave ! ” Writer cordially agreed with the manager
that the settlement was no place for those un-
common men. Before Govt, started these Murray
settlements, wheat-growing was never dreamt of*;
now there is scarcely an acre of river-frontage
unleased, and thousands of acres of cropped land
growing the finest wheat in the province. So they
have done some good after all.
* * * *
Although there is a provision in Q.
Mining Act prohibiting employment of
females underground, there are many small
fields where the inspector ceases from
troubling and the warden is at rest. In
Piccadilly, for instance, on the N.Q. coastal
range, a fossicker with a family of six girls
works a show ; the two eldest do the hammer-
and-drill work, while the others look after
the windlass, etc. And it is a very uncom-
fortable pot-hole, too. Reef about lin.,
country hard as steel, stopes just wide
enough for an attenuated carpet-snake to crawl
under. And after all it only just pays tucker,
though a young man driving from an adjaoent
claim might strike that which is alleged to be
more precious than gold.

A genuinely Australian edition of Bala-
clava :
The Chief Inspector of Stock, Brisbane, received the
following wire from one of the Western Divisional
Boards: “600 head cattle in charge of a drover named
Dargin, perished on the main stock route to Hungerford
Road, on Boorara run. The bodies are a nuisance to the
travelling public.”
That is how the Six Hundred charge in Australia.
Poor, bony 600.
When two men are working by themselves
in a lonely shaft, and the man at the top loves the
wife of the man below, or wants his share of the
mine, or objects seriously to his politics, or his
religion, or tiie way he parts his hair, there isn’t
much show for the man underneath. A strange
yarn from Coolgardie (W.A.) points the moral of
this. Two men owned a little mine, and one day,
it is alleged, the hidden animosity of the man at
the top blazed forth, so whenever the man below
tried to come up he hit him on the head with a
stick as soon as be appeared above the surface,
Finally, the man below gave up appearing at the
surface. Then, he says, his mate, after keep-
ing him below for two days, took to throwing
dynamite down the shaft at him. It was Thurs-
day night when the mate first hit him on the
head, and it wasn’t till late on Sunday that he
escaped, during his friend’s temporary absence —
possibly in search of more dynamite. This is the
cheerful story told by the man below when he got
out. Probably a good many miners have met a
similar experience, but didn’t get out.

THE NEW-CHUM AND THE ANT-HILL;
Or, Didn't Know ’Twas Loaded.
The official bank holidays in Japan make
up the following incongruous list: New Year,
Anniversary of the Emperor Komer, German
Emperor’s Birthday, Festival of Spring Equinox,
Queen Victoria’s Birthday, Festival of Harvest
Deities, Mikado’s Birthday, Anniversary of the
Taking of the Bastille, ‘‘The Glorious Fourth,”
Anniversary of the Death of Jummu Tenno,
Christmas Day and Chinese New Year’s Day.
The recent holidays have brought a large
number of visitors to Sydney from the country.
There has been more money spent than for some
years past. Country visitors, with confidence men
and spielers always in their eye, have, however,
felt the necessity for some safe place in which to
leave superfluous cash and jewellery, hotel bed-
rooms being notoriously insecure, and it is a
notable fact that the Sydney Safe Deposit has
this year been much more largely utilised than
formerly. Persons with cash can place it there in
security, and draw it out at any hour of the day.
as they may require it, thus relieving their minds
at a small cost. Sydney residents off to the
country for a spell have also utilised the Deposit
to a much greater extent for the storage of plate,
etc., and the rush of clients shows the place to be
steadily increasing in public favor. To country
visitors, who have not seen the Safe Deposit, we
would recommend a look through its vaults. They
are among the sights of Sydney. The Deposit is
situated in Ash-street, off George-street, at the
back of Paling’s.*
FRED. LANE, THE AUSTRALIAN SWIMMER, BEFORE A RACE, AND AFTER.
HIS NAME.
Who art Thou that cometh out of far Galilee,
With Thy nail-pierced hands and Thy thorn-crowned
brow—
Sweat of blood drops from Thy forehead to Thy knee :
In the name of God who made us, who art Thou ?
Sadly He replied—“ lam the Crucified !
I am He for whose garments the world’s cut-throats
have diced.
I die ev’ry day as on Calvary I died—
And my true name is Labor, though priests call me
Christ.”
V.J.D.

London Times says the information which led
Methuen to fight the Boers at Magersfontein
“ was deliberately false.” That only proves
that the Boers are playing the game sLrictly.
It is the business of every general to mystify,
mislead and surprise his opponent if he can,
and it is the business of the other fellow to
take care he is not mystified and misled. War
isn’t run on pure George Washington prin-
ciples. It is the biggest deceit of all the ages,
and is not a game to be played by guileless
men who believe all they are told.
Railway Commissioners’ galvanised-iron cooling
chambers at Coolabah, N.S.W.
Editor Bulletin. — l send you herewith
the photo of a “building” erected by N.S.W.
Railway Department, at Coolabaah, on the N. W.
line, for the storage of goods (including butter,
meat, and fruit). In this noble structure, which
is entirely of galvanised iron and is absolutely
without ventilation, the temperature sometimes
reaches 130, with an effect upon the aforesaid
perishable articles which may be easily imagined.
Also, the Sydney-Bourke traffic is surely of
sufficient importance to warrant the use of a
refrigerating-car. As things are, boxes of butter,
for instance, arrive here with the contents
streaming from the cracks. Useless for us small
men to interview the commissioners; they have
ear only for the big wholesale people ; in fact, the
railway freight system appears to be carefully ad-
justed, so that in this back-country the big firms
shall be able to undersell us and thus grab the
whole trade in the small towns.

He had been an officer himself,

To fight for England’s Queen and home.
The orderly saluted him
As he passed upon the step,
l Arid thought of the blow he would receive
* When the old man saw the list.

1.-The Managing-Director of THE BULLTIN is here seen putting up the shutters. 2.-The Editor deliberately rushed upon his own scissors, which he bribed the Office-Boy to hold. The latter vainly trying to conceal his emotions. 3.-The man who wrete the dis
loyal articles threw himself in front of a George-street tram. 4.-The Dramatic Critic and Libel Expert also sought a Spartan's death. 5.-The Printer's Devil rushed off and joined the auny. 6.-Martha Washington, ex-Serubber-in-Chief, sought another field of
endeavor in the street-cleaning department of the city. 7.-The Chancellor of the Exchequer proceeded to settle all outstanding accounts, especially with the Fighting Editor. 8.-His assistant disappeared up an alley with the "Cash in hand"-but that is a mere detail,
9.-Minion of the Law, who said he hadn't no authouity to interfere with nobody. 10.-As for poor old Hop.-well, the world is wide; bebold him here decorating the footpaths. 11.-The "Woman's Column" bore it all with the usual Christian fortitude of her sex. 12.-
The Mining Expert has gone prospecting in George-street. 13.-The Horse Poet, his eve in a fine frenzy rolling (the other being chronically black), kippled some patriotic verse, which he sold to the S.M. HERALD. 14.-The harmless (and now mnecessary) Office-Cat re-
signed upon the spot with the last available rat in her pośession. 15.-The Wild-ditto in now roaming up and down the earth seeking whom it may devour. 16.-A valued but indingant contributor called to demand his M.S. back. 17.-The Office Chaplain is here seen

Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
27 of this issue.*
The tallest snake-yarn yet from the seat
of war is that which tells how the telephone-
operator at Dundee kindly held the instrument
off the switch so that his pal at Greytown might
hear the noises of battle. Suddenly he exclaimed,
“For God’s sake, wait a moment; a shell has
dropped into the tent! ” The idea of a man
wasting valuable time telephoning that there was
a shell —presumably liable to explode—in his tent
instead of instantly getting fiat on the ground is
too strong.

Thus “ Compton ” :
Be that Continental criticism. Was talking to a highly
intelligent German (if it is permitted to N.S.W. Journal-
ism to imagine such a monstrosity) on Saturday, before I
the news of the Tugela incident came through. "No
one on the Continent,” said he, “ doubted the dogged
courage of British soldiers. That has been proved too
often. But that the British have no generals, capable
of conducting serious war, had been a Continental com-
monplace for years.” And now, certainly, “the times
give it proof.” “In ’66 and ’70,” he went on, “we
(the Prussians) undertook war on the big scale, with
three and four hundred thousand men in the actual
fighting line, and as many again in reserve; and ever
since then our generals have been trained in the manage-
ment of similar numbers of men. The same with
the military chiefs of Russia and France. No British
general since the Crimea has had 60,000 men undpr him
at once. They have done nothing but lead small expedi-
tions against savages, and while this makes the British
soldier a seasoned man who has smelt powder, it makes
the British commander careless, confident, and non-
strategic.” My informant wound up by assuring me that
it was not considered by Continental experts that a
British general could make any better showing against a
scientifioallv-trained French or German leader than he
himself against a champion pug.
A lady (Miss Flora Nicholls, of Auckland,
Maoriland) writes to Auckland Herald :
I have lately lived among them (the Boers) for over two
years, when I had every opportunity of closely watching
their innermost lives and habits. From first to last I saw
nothing but what was honorable, praiseworthy, and
admirable in the extreme. I have met Boers who through
drought having lost thousands of sheep and goats in one
season have lived on nothing but. goat’s flesh, goat’s milk,
and pure water, not one ounce of bread passing their lips,
because they could not see their way to be sure of paying
for it. Let me ask how many men, aye, and men who
are holding their heads high to-day in Auckland, have
come within coo-ee even of the example of these same
Boers when difficulties have arisen, and probably, through
their own fault, could not pay their just debts. Have
I they not retained their luxuries and continued eat-
ing their bread buttered on both sides, and laughed
yes, laughed, at their creditors! How many ot
Maoriland's farmers commence and end the day witn
prayer and praise, and ask God’s blessing on ever}
step they take through life's journey, as do these same
Boers? How many of them are to be. found regularl}
partaking of the sacrament beside their wives? Inen
reverence for that ceremony is so great that both sexes
wear black when receiving it. I suppose this willme
sneered and laughed at by many, but which is the better
—deep reverence or none at all ? lam acquainted wit
several wealthy Boers whose first object is to secure a
thorough and classical education for their sons, ana
daughters. So much for the “ unprogressive Boer, and
as to their hating the English, I tell you emphatically
they do nothing of the kind. Having suffered severely
from fever while amongst them, I owe my life to the
great loving kindness and tender nursing I received from
the Boer wives, who thought it nothing to drive 60 miles
to take their turn nursing me.
