POLITICAL
POINTS
Loyalty—love of the other fellow’s sove-
reigns.
Foung as is Premier Lyne’s team, one of
diem observed the other day, “I don’t read the
papers but my attention was drawn,” and so on.
Estimated that the Transvaal gold-war, if
carried on until May next, will cost ,£50,000,000.
And Australia’s share looks like being <£500,000
anyhow.
Apparently this will be the great deficit
year all over Australia. Every province is send-
ing away two contingents, possibly three, and not
one had the courage to raise the necessary funds
by extra taxation.
In the last four years Britain has annexed
2,600,000 square miles of land. And every mile
means more responsibility, more frontiers to be
protected, more niggers to be kept down, more
complications, more the “ White Man’s
Burden.”
Queensland is a country of magnificent
distances and magnificent means of communica-
tion. It took a batch of Federation pamphlets
from August 5 to Sept. 20 to travel from Brisbane
to Croydon — l/O days. The literature arrived 15
days after the vote was taken.
A FRIEND IN NEED.
BLACK : “Shay olchap, thishm wife on wire. Dy'e min tell'ner I
can't shpeak t'er? ”
WHYTE: “ Ce'Inly, of man. I'll teller yer speechless."
Dr. Carty Salmon, who was put in at the
tail of the M'Lean Ministry as an honorary wax
figger, has been promoted to be Minister for
Public Instruction, vice M‘Cay, indignantly
rejected by his constituents on seeking re-election.
Salmon is another weak patch on a rather preca-
rious garment, but he has a reasonably safe seat
for Talbot and Avoca, which is an important
matter.
S.A. political experts (who are numerous)
allege that the Holder Govt, is only designed to
last six months. Ex-Premier Kingston’s pro-
gramme is to get into the Leg. Council and per-
sonally steer his Household Suffrage reform
through that chamber. As Gordon, now Attor-
ney-General, opposes that measure, Kingston’s
advent will mean his resignation or a large
swallowing of his principles.
In Maoriland the general election and the
local option vote are taken together, and for the
third time in six years the Cold Tea crowd has
tone down. It didn’t capture a single district.
laoriland rose up like a lioness robbed of its
beer, and cast the ginger-pop gospel from it by a
larger majority than usual, but already the pallid
prophets are talking about how the Terewth will
triumph in 1902, and how the ungodly whisky;
bottle is doomed.
“D.G.”: Cabled that London Daily Mail
says Commander-in-Chief Wolseley was “urgently
and persistently advised to despatch an army-
corps to South Africa months ago.” This is a
case of the ass kicking the dead lion with a ven-
geance. Wolseley had no power to despatch an
army-corps anywhere without orders. Why not
put the blame at once on the right shoulders,
which seem to be largely those of Brummagem
Joe Chamberlain ?
The Philp (Q.) Government took in Drake
and dropped One-Man-One-Vote at the same time.
Drake declared, right up to the date when he
swapped his conscience for a portfolio, that One-
Man-One-Vote was the first essential of reform,
but circumstances—especially profitable circum-
stances —alter cases.
At the late Maoriland election more than
three-fourths of the votes polled were given to the
Seddon party. Yet the Tory papers come to the
surface with the good old remark that “ the vote
doesn’t represent the true feeling of the country.”
The “ true feeling of the country ’’ is one of these
dark and beery mysteries which defy analysis.
S.A. Parliament was prorogued at Xmas
after about the barrenest session on record. Not
one of the liberal measures of the Kingston Govt,
was passed, and S.A. democrats despair of secur-
ing any reform until the Tory Upper House is
remodelled. Almost the last act of that Fat
Man’s citadel wa.s the contemptuous rejection of
Household Suffrage.
Some day there is going to be an unholy
row in N.S.W. over the practice of sending to
Ministers copies of private telegrams containing
interesting news. The newspapers pay for the
news and the Ministry intercepts a copy of it on
its way. through, without payment. Which is
just as dishonest as if it claimed the right to inter-
cept Tooth’s beer-waggon in the street and have a
gratis drink. News-cables are quite as much
private property as beer.
Queensland ex-Premier Dawson seconded
Premier Phil p’s proposal for a second Transvaal
Contingent. Dawson wobbles on both sides. He
says that the war is an unjust war, and also that
it is necessary to help on the injustice to show
“the unity of the Empire.” It is strange what a
number of weak-kneed men find it necessary to
bolt from'their momentarily unpopular principles
to show the unity of the Empire ! If the unity
of the Empire can be manifested by the cowardice
and pitiable moral funk of politicians, it will be
the most united on earth.
Certain English Jingoes,
transplanted to Australia, and
without patriotism enough to
honor the land where they live,
are talking of opposing Hol-
man, M.L.A., of Grenfell, and
Griffith, of Waratah, for their
action in denouncing the send-
ing of Australians to the
shameful Transvaal war. Hol-
man and Griffith will never do
a more creditable action, or one
more in line with the interests
of Australia, if they live 100
years ; and if the Australians
of Grenfell and Waratah have
not pluck enough and spirit
enough to support them, then
those Australians deserve the
worst that can happen them.
Joseph Ward, white -
washed and rehabilitated, goes
back to the Maoriland Govern-
ment as Postmaster - General
and various other things; and
Brown Man James Carroll be-
comes Minister for Native
Affairs. Thelamen table Thomas
Thompson goes out and clever
Jerome Cadman, an honest,
hard-working, clever, but very
weary man, does likewise.
Cadman goes to the Upper
House to have a well-deserved
rest ; and if the dull, lumber-
ing Thompson went to the place
that many people thmk he
should go to then there would
be a profane sound about the
name of the place that Thomp-.
son went to.
Treasurer Forrest, of
W.A., when he proposed to
float a temporary 4 per cent,
million loan for two years at
98, was quite unable to realise
that he was really paying 5
per cent, for the money, and
barked indignantly for some
time at the Opposition speaker who talked of it as a
spercent, loan. Then a glimmer of information fell
on him, and he sat down in a rather sudden heap.
Bigjohn is commonly supposed to make up his
financial statement by means of chalk-marks on
the' back-door.
There is not one big locally-resident city
land-owner in South Australia. Within the
metropolitan boundaries the South Australian Co.
(wholly English) has £227,524 of unimproved
land values; the late Thomas Martin’s estate
comes next with <£156,484, and the City Council
third with .£86,252. After these the Waterhouses
and the inevitable Montefiores ; then the Society
for the Propagation of the Gospel in Foreign
Parts, <£63,554. The Society for the Propagation
of the Gospel in Local Parts hasn’t anything— it
doesn’t exist, in. fact,
In Maoriland the N.Z. Times and Otago
Daily Times have been honorable exceptions to
the prevailing war madness, inasmuch as they
have only yelled for a pint of blood where other
papers demanded a hogshead, and they have even
allowed correspondents to state the other fellow’s
side of the case in their, columns. Consequently
thb loyal push has risen up as one ass and threat-
ened to withdraw its advt. and stop taking the
paper, and certain correspondents who signed
their names in full have been threatened with the
boots of the hob-nailed patriot. The crowd that
says it is fighting for liberty is firmly resolved that
no one else will have any.
A BORN STRATEGIST.
SLIMY TIM : “ 'Owever is it you always pick up
half cigars, and I never can cop more than
buttends? ”
ARTFUL JOE : “ Easy enough, you fool! I always
shepherd them hairy-faced blokes from the
club. They can't smoke more than half a
cigar for fear of burning a hole in their
moustaches."
Some more Chamberlain disinterred :
These are not the main reasons why, as I submit, it is
impossible to maintain the annexation of the Transvaal.
That course is impossible for any Government caring for
the honor of this country. It is contrary to their Treaty
engagements, to the best traditions of a free country.—
Joseph Chamberlain, House of Commons, July 25, 1881.
But somebody svith a broken nose has since
whispered in Joseph’s ear.
J. M. Davies, brother of Matthew of the
Boom, being appointed to represent the M'Lean
Ministry in the Vic. House of 48 Rich Landlords,
had to go to his afflicted country for re-election.
Appropriately enough five liquidators signed his
nomination paper. The liquidators of Melbourne
owe a debt of gratitude to the Davies family, but
the sight of all these winders-up of dead banks
rallying round Matthew’s brother was a terrible
spectacle. It suggested five undertakers nom-
inating a corpse.
The explanation commonly given of
Britain’s want of progress in South Africa is that
she is no longer facing the Boers only, but a part of
the Dutch population of Cape Colony as well. It
isn’t a large part, of course, for it can only come
from that little section of British territory held by
the Boer armies, and places adjacent thereto. But
suppose every man, woman and child of Dutch
descent in all South Africa was in the field. They
are about 900,000, and the British population is
almost exactly the same, so it ought to be a reason-
ably fair fight even if there wasn’t one British
soldier on hand. The 900,000 Dutch inhabitants
of South Africa are about one-fifth the population
of London —about twice the population of Bir-
mingham—Jess than twice that of Liverpool or
Manchester or Glasgow or Melbourne- about
three times that of Dublin or Edinburgh.
What a handful after all 1
A LUCID
EXPLANATION.
LAWYER : “ The fight oc-
curred at the back of
your house? ”
WITNESS: “Yis.”
L.: “And which was the assailant?”
W.: “ Begor I’dno. but I think they both
was.”
L.: “ What! Do you mean to say that when a fight
was so close by, you didn’t go out to see it.”
W.: “ I dho. The kay of the dhure fell into the
crack in the flure, and there was only a bit av
brass wire for a glass pane in the back windak,
ah’ I couldn’t see through it, ah I couldn't go
out the chimb’ey bekase av the fire.”
L.: “ Stand down!”
In India, the British Government draws
the best of its native troops from the Punjaub,.
and, in former days, when their term of service
had expired, the men went back to cultivate their
hereditary farms. Now, the three-ball man
and the money-lending Parsee have done their
deadly work, and they have no farms to cul-
tivate —the Bombay usurers have grabbed
them, and the Sikhs are abandoning their
country, and Britain finds it hard to get
soldiers. Britain has taken the bull by
the horns swiftly and thoroughly. Vice-
roy Curzon has introduced a bill into
the Indian Council that is said by the
local press to be so sweeping in the
thoroughness of its destruction of the
three-ball man that the whole tribe of
shent-per-shenters is crying to the Em-
press to veto the bill and save their tribe
from utter destruction. But the Empress
wants soldiers more than .silver bugs,
and the yell will probably be disre-
garded. If Australia had only the sense
to do something like this.
There is much plain horse-sense
about S. A. political methods. Amotion
of censure there can be begun and ended
in ten minutes. The formula simply is that,
as soon as the Assembly meets,the Opposition
leader moves the adjournment without giv-
ing any reasons. That means a general
motion of censure, and as no debate is
allowed it is about the suddenest poli-
tical proceeding: in Christendom. Ministers
don’t have to go for re-election when they take
office, so the new Ministry may be completed next
morning and fired out again in the afternoon.
Considering how anti-Dutch Maoriland is,
it is a solemn thing to reflect that that province is
still officially named “New Zealand a sort of
warmed-up rehash of old Zeeland, in the Nether-
lands, after which Abel Tasman named it.
In Maoriland every male and female
householder, whether a direct ratepayer or not,
has a vote at municipal elections ; also mayors are
elected directly by the people. Last session a
Bill was nearly carried in N.S.W. giving the
people of Sydney power to elect their own mayors,
but even this small fragment of reform dropped
down a big hole somewhere and was lost.
Highly respectable barrister Moule scored
a point during the Vic. Assembly’s debate on the
ousting of the Turner Govt. Foster, Minister of
Mines, artlessly complained that M‘Lean’s attaok
was a most ungrateful proceeding, seeing that “ he
had received more than his fair share ” of Govern-
ment assistance for his constituency. Where-
upon Moule shrieked. “Corruption! It is the
worst thing that has been said against the Govt,
yet.” Foster was seemingly still of opinion that
the ingrate ought to have considered himself
“squared.”
This is the first time for 15 years that
there has been an election in Melbourne province
for a Fat Man to represent it in the Vic. House
of 48 Rich Landlords. For 15 years the Fat
Person selected by a little local clique has had a
walk-over. And o* her districts are in much the
same condition. The narrow franchise, the faofc
that only landlords of great circumference in the
bank-balance are eligible, and the other fact that
the Council is never dissolved, and only expires
by small instalments, so that it can’t be all cleaned
out at once, have destroyed public interest in Vic.
Council elections. To all intents and purposes
there are no Council elections.
Maoriland’s second attack of war fever is
worse than the first. Seddon, whether he ap-
proves or not, goes with the tide. The New
Woman, with blood in her eye and her bonnet all
askew, haunts the highways cheering the Old
Woman's husband on to go and be blotted out by
Boer artillery. As Parliament isn’t sitting, Sed-
don consulted the members individually by tele-
gram, and they, almost to the last man, demanded
warm red Blood. George Fisher, in particular,
wired back that he boiled with honest indigna-
tion, and if he were a younger man he would go
to Africa himself. Yet George is about 20 years
younger than Joubert, and Joubert didn't plead
age as an excuse.
When Drake fled suddenly from his posi-
tion as leader of the so-called Queensland Opposi-
tion, and joined the enemy, and took a stained
and dishonored portfolio in the Boodledrake
Government, he didn’t even inform his own
party of his intended flight. The subject was
hardly one to bear explanation, and the party
consisted only of about two men ; still these
two were busy denying the alleged defection
of their cherished Drake for hours after he had
left them and joined the enemy, and when
it dawned on them that the stories were
true, they were almost mad enough to bite
their late leader. It is a way politicians have
in Queensland. Party politics in that
mottled land are a strange and stinkful
thing. It is practically the only English-
speaking country where the leader of the
Opposition is expected as a“ regular thing
to desert his party and sell himself to the
Ministry.
At present, the signs ;ire that Victoria’s
new Ministry won’t outlast January, and
that the new Cabinet will include Turner,
Peacock, Trenwith, and possibly Styles,
Cameron, and one or two of the old Ministers.
Leprous dogs now roam Calcutta streets.
Persons bitten are threatened with a dreadful
fate—not hydrophobia.

A National Cash Register protects
the cash
The register is a place of safe deposit for the in-
- coming cash, that can be opened only with sign and
signal. It is an automatic receiving and paying
cashier under mechanical bonds for faithfulness and
accuracy.
It protects the customer.
It shows instantly to every customer a record of
his transaction and gives him a printed voucher for
the same.
It protects the shopkeeper.
It makes an open record of every transaction and
preserves these records for inspection, furnishing
ready data for balancing the day’s work.
It provides the readiest means of discovering errors
and locating responsibility for them.
It puts dishonesty in danger of detection.
It protects the books-
It is a witness against the one who “ forgot to make
the entry.”
It protects the assistant-
It safeguards the rights of employees by making
every assistant responsible for only his own trans-
actions and filing a record of the same which can be
referred to in case of doubt.
If an error appears, it is better for some one to be
convicted of a mistake than for anyone to be sus-
pected of crime.
It makes occasional purchasers
regular customers-
The paper check which it issues for each sale is a
drawing-card. The printed promise on this check of
w
a premium when cash purchasers have reached a
certain amount will bring the customer hack in order
to reach that amount, until coming has become a
habit and the occasional customer has become a
regular patron.
It causes credit customers to buy
for cash-
With its check system offering a discount for cash,
it encourages cash buying and so reduces the risks of
accounts. The system may also be extended to favor
weekly payments instead of long accounts, and so
solve the somewhat difficult problem of the good
but slow credit customer.
Prices from £5 to £6B.

on application to

m ELLIS’S
(Registered) SIMPLE FOOT GEAR.
SEND
2d. STAMP
FOR TAPE
MEASURE.
CATALOGUE
POST FREE
ON
APPLICA-
TION.
G
With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel GO,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have along thin foot, or

WRITE FOR CATALOG UK
Miners returned from the Transvaal give high
praise to the system of mine and boiler inspection there,
and refer to this as one thing that isn’t likely to be im-
proved by the changes which will follow the war. And in
proof of it they quote the special correspondent of Lond.
Times, who said in July—“ The mine-manager who in-
terests himself in the preservation of his personal rights
is liable to be harried and worried out of existence by the
attentions of mine inspectors, claim inspectors, boiler in-
spectors, and machinery inspectors.” So the war is a war
against mine and boiler and machinery inspection, too.
And still some people wonder what white miners had to
gain by allowing things to remain as they were.
A Strofjg co. is being formed to work some
property on the abandoned Wynyard field (S. India). In
the old days (say 20 years ago) many Australians were
sent out from Vic. * to this field. Brough Smythe
Sheridan, who lately managed a Westralian mine, was
■one of the pioneers.
* * » *
“ If there be one enterprising municipality in
Australasia,” writes The Bulletin’s London correspon-
dent, “ let it gather up its rubbish and reap shekels arid
glory thus: Shoreditch is just outside London City’s
limits, and is presumed to be mostly costers and their
* mokes,’ but the vestrymen have' heads longer than any
donkey’s ears. In the past 12 months they have collected
and burnt 23,728 tons of rubbish, the destruction of which
generated 1,031,348 units (Board of Trade) of electricity to
light 151 arc and 302 incandescent street lamps and sup-
ply besides 413 private customers at 4jd. per unit—the
lowest rate in the kingdom outside Manchester and Edin-
burgh. The first year’s surplus was £2063, and this (the
second year) finds it £7745. In addition £llB6 has been
saved by not having the rubbish carted away. The waste
steam from the ‘ destructor’ plant heats the public baths
and washhouses at an estimated saving of £lBOO yearly.
Even the poorest tenements use the light, the cost in two-
room sets being Bd. a week. Lord Kelvin, the celebrated
electrical expert, eulogised the scheme at its inception,
and magnificently have results verified his words. No
more putrefying rubbish-heaps to windward of your
town for Mr. Run-‘em-up a year later to erect weather-
board fever-villas on, but a big profit out of dust and dirt!
A current of electricity generates heat in the metal that
■carries it—heat generates electricity—rubbish stimulated
with coke or coal is a cheap fuel and gives much heat—
hence much electricity and—profit. Go thou and do
likewise.”
V * ♦ #
Christchurch (M.L.) water-supply is artesian,
and three men were recently hard at work sinking a pipe.
The wag of the party surreptitiously dropped a few hand-
fuls of brass filings down the pipe and waited serenely
for results. Presently the fluid came rushing up, and
poured out the glittering filings before the startled gaze
of the navvies. Then and there they held a solemn coun-
cil of war. The wag said he would stay and protect the
find. One went off to make good terms with their
employer, who was about 20 miles out of town, and the
other rushed to a lawyer to take legal advice how to
secure “ the mine.” The employer hired a special train
to get there quickly and tore through the city in a cab to
engage a mining expert. Then a wash-up was made, and
the language that was used caused a dreadful smell
throughout Christchurch for a week afterwards.
* » * •
A well-known Sydney jeweller writes to say
that he is more than ever convinced of the advantages of
advertising. He has a standing advt. in Tiie Bulletin
about clocks, watches and jewellery for sale, and the
other day it evoked this letter from a small N.S.W.
country town : “ Dear sir,—Hearing that you are a pur-
chaser of Cigar Ash at 21s. per ounce, herewith you will
please find a tin containing’ one ounce of the best Havana
Cigar Ash. Trusting to receive cheque by return post, I
am, &e.” Query : Whence conies the wide-spread delu-
sion that some unknown person, living round the 114th
turning to the right or thereabouts, is always ready and
even anxious to give more for cigar ash than the original
cost of the cigars, said ash being in great demand at the
North Pole or elsewhere, for purposes unknown ?
* * * *
Rainfall at Wilcannia, the centre of the most
extensive tract of pastoral country in N.S.W., during the
past four years :—1896, 7'39in. ; ’97, 7'24in. ; ’9B, s’42in. ;
’99, 4’76in. The fall has diminished every year. For the
four years together it has amounted to only 24'81in. ; and
even then it has been so scattered that two-thirds of it
have been almost valueless. Also, it has to be remem-
bered, the heat is so intense, the winds so scorching, and
the evaporation so great that 24in. would not be worth
there more than 12in. on the coast. Altogether, the far
western country as a whole has never before, in the 45
or 50 years during which it has been settled, been in so
deplorable a state as it is in to-day.

One Krug, who runs a Perth (W. A.) loan office,
advertised all last December that he was lending monish
without interest or charges for that month only, because
it was the last December in the century. Krug’s feelings
when he discovers that there is another December before
the century ends ought to be worth publishing.
* * *
Some extracts from a certain “Mining Ex-
pert’s ” report, as sent bv him to N.S.W. Chamber of
Mines :—“The principal Geological feature of the Mineral
Lease is that of a large Subteraneous Upheaval or Ex-
trusion giving rise to a Distinct Formation of a different
character to the Country Rock. On either side towards
the Base Farce Bodies of Conglomorite oxidete trap rock
occur whilst higher in the Formation Horne Blend Schist
is present were (sic) the Terregunious Crust is removed.”
“ Terregunious Crust ” suggests something good to eat,
whilst lower down *he “Expert” speaks of “Ferru-
genious Quarts ” spelt with an “s” —suggestive of some
new and refreshing potation. The “ Expert” says that
he “ made some exhaustive assays ” —the result being :
“ From 20 assays made in duplicate to check an average
of 19dwts. 12grs. of gold per ton of 22401b5.” Observe the
detail ! And the samples were taken from a face 30ft
wide, being quarried in a cutting. Now lOjflwts. from a
30ft. reef in a quarry would turn most people’s heads ,
but the expert keeps quite calm and only ventures so far as
to say : “ I am of oppinion that 17dwts. will pay well with
careful treatment and management, having in view the
Large Formation, &0., &c.” The “ Expert” winds up his
geological remarks with a sentence worthy of a place in
“English as she is spoke”: “There are several other
geological features which is unnecessary of detail in this
Report.”
» * * *
Notified that the A.J.S. Bank has closed its
Cobar branch, leaving the town with only one bank, the
Commercial. Which recalls an incident. On a pay
Saturday afternoon a man dressed as a bushman appeared
in Cobar with £5-notes of a bank having no local branch.
He bought at several shops, in the dusk and dark, and
changed a fiver at each. In one, that of a chemist and
bookseller, be hought 7s. 6d. worth and tendered the £5.
The shop attendant, not likmg the look of it, the buyer
offered another out of a roll, and as a senior-constable,
who was present in plain clothes, offered to give t'4 19s.
for it, the shopkeeper gave change. On Monday morning
the bank announced to depositor that a fiver in his little
lot was had ! Everyone then began to look up the notes,
and no less than 45 were found to be bogus. The parties
—it was proved afterwards that two men were engaged—
were never caught. Their meanest exploit consisted in
arousing about midnight an old, struggling couple, who
kept an accommodation-house on the Bourke-Cobar road.
The old woman grilled chops and made tea, and had a
fiver given her. She could only scrape up about £4 ss.
change, so the pair handsomely told her she could keep
the balance for the extra trouble given.
• » * »
Dear Bulletin.—Apropos of the large nugget
found recently at Marble Bar (W.A.), this is the true his-
tory of Father Long’s famous “ Sacred Slug ” : Father
Long entered into an agreement with two miners to work
a show on the Kurnalpi road, the conditions being that
the priest was to pay the men £4 per week each, and, in
the event of a “ strike,” all were to share equally. After
working a week or two, they decided to take their finan-
cier down, if possible. With this object in view, they
procured a boulder of great tonnage, and subjected it to
a treatment which made it appear like gold to the non-
practical eye. It was brought to the good, hut surprised,
Father late at night, and left with him on the under-
standing that the men should be advanced £2OO each,
also that the find should not be divulged until the
remainder of the “gold” had been taken from the show.
With this store of wealth on hand, there was no difficulty
in obtaining the desired amount, which was paid, and
thereupon the two men boarded the “ Flying Sandgroper”
for Perth or Klondyke, unknown to Father Long, who
labored under the delusion that they had gone back to
work. In the meantime, a rumor spread like wildfire that
a large nugget had been deposited in the local R.O. pres-
bytery, the consequence being that Father Long was ap-
pealed to for definite information. Having pledged him-
self to secrecy, be withheld all knowledge of the affair until
the diggers were almost in a state of frenzy. The slug was
taken to Perth, where the fraud was discovered almost at
the same hour at which Father Long disclosed the locality
of the find from the balcony of Donnellan’s Hotel,
Konowna, to about 5000 people. A mad rush was made
for the scene of the alleged find, and the things that were
said and done there were unfit for publication. Father
Long has since gone to the land whence no traveller
returns, the worry and anxiety attendant on the lament-
able nugget being at least partly responsible for his
death.—Don.
* * * »
Miners are still being sought for at Broken Hill;
managers in their progress reports repeatedly work in a
little advt. to this effect. Local men also send away their
reports; hut few of them get into print. Sample one,
lately to hand “ Really a minimum should be fixed for
a fortnight’s ‘ take.’ The other day on one mine, accord-
ing to the sheets, one party had hot earned Cd. each per
shift; and they were paid off at this, too.” But, after
all, the contract-system is not half so big a curse on a
semi-tropical mining field as night-shift (12 midnight to 8
a.m.). Imagine trying to sleep by day in a galvanised-iron
hut with a thermometer registering from 105 to 120, and
perhaps half-a-dozen youngsters home for the holidays !
At the end of a week of this most men are not worth
much more than 6d. a shift. During the summer the
night-shift men can nearly always be told by their appear-
ance.
» * » *
The bank smash of ’93 broke up the Darling
River cockies so far as taking bank-notes in payment for
anything is concerned, Tp offer a note now,is to con-
vince the dweller on the bone-strewn plains that you have
early information that the bank is going, and want to get
out in time. The Darling.cockies in ’93 sold £l-notes of
reconstructed banks for 10s. and 12s. each.
A “mining-expert ” up North recently bad sold
to him, dirt-cheap, a smalt parcel of first-water “dia-
monds” averaging about ljcts., and it wasn’t till the
stones were submitted to a Sydney firm of buyers that
they were found to he merely colorless zircons, which re-
semble diamonds somewhat in brilliancy .and crystallo-
graphic form. The hardness-test is with them unreliable,
as zircon will scratch quartz-crystal or glass readily. It
was this same colorless variety Of the gem which caused
the Nundle “ diamond ” rush a few years back.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
Steeplechase on concluding day (Monday) of
Sydney Tatt’s meeting was shriekingly funny. Four
started. Lady Lygon baulked at the first fence, and was
not got over till the others had gone round once ; later,
she stopped again and then withdrew. Finbar got rid of
his rider at the brush jump in the first round, and Murra-
brine came a cropper over the logs. He also went within
an ace of mangling his rider (Regan), who, however,
pluekily remount d. Meanwhile The Toff (owner,
Graham, up) had been sailing along at a great pace and
clearing all obstacles without much effort, though his
rider was on the verge of falling off once. In the last
mile The Toff held a lead of over two furlongs from
Murrabrine, the only other nag left in. Once
Graham brought his mount to a standstill, and most
people thought he had broken a stirrup-strap. The Toff
now had ten lengths to the stood, but he came down
badly over the last fence, and Murrabrine finished alone.
An investigation by Tatt.’s committee resulted in a
decision to recommend the A.J.C. not to allow Mr.
Graham to ride attain, on che ground of alleged incom-
petence. It was the most farcical ’chase ever seen at
Randwick, and should about put the set on that class of
event. Steeplechasing has been going from bad to very
much worse in Sydney for a long time.
* * * *
Said that owner Dick Wootton of Sydney Tatfc.’s
Cup winner Vocalist put £6OO of his own money on him,
and as he was heavily supported by stable connections
and the public, Ikey must have had a torrid time of it.
Vocalist is a 4-year-old by Gozo-Carrnen, and was bred at
Tucka Tuulta (N.S.W.). Wootton is the only one who has
ever raced him. As a 2-yeai-old Vocalist ran four times
unsuccessfully, and last season competed half a dozen
times, only winning once—a welter mile at Rosehill.
* * • * *
Kogarah (Sydney)-trained Mora, the sensation
of recent Caulfield and Melbourne Cups, has struck
trouble at last. Heavily backed in right quarter for
Sydney Tatts. Cup (two miles), on New Year’s Day, she
was taken to the front by Cis. Parker, at a mile and a
quarter, as if engaged in a six furlongs sprint, and,
galloping brilliantly, looked like running away with the
race. She rounded the home-turn holding a six-lengths
lead, but tired very fast. Halfway down the straight
Vocalist caught and passed her, creating a margin of six
lengths between himself and the mare ere reaching the
winning-post. Owner (Teddj' Knight), trainer (Dick
O’Connor), and jockey (F. Fielder) were to have explained
Mora’s latest effort to the A.J.C. committee on Thursday.
Whether “on it” or not last week, someone backed Mora
heavily that day, sending her out a 10 to 9 on favorite.
The stable hasn’t got many well-wishers among the great
army of punters just now.
* * • •
Canzoni (imp.)—Hester b.in. Coralie got away
with Sydney Tat t.’s Carrington Stakes (Saturday last),
just as she did with A.J.C. Villiers on Boxing Day, by
not appealing on the scene till well inside the distance,
and t hen romping over everything—this despite her 101 b.
penalty, which made the total weight up 8.13. From be-
ginning to end the pace was a cracker, as is evidenced by
the time, 1.15. Yet fast as this time is, it has been
beaten on half a dozen different occasions in the last 10
years. Precaution, helped by a strong wind, covered
the 6 furlQngs in 1.13)4 ! Loch Marie has a fraction of a
second more to her credit. Last-named nag was the
only one, prior to Coralie, to annex the Villiers and Car-
rington, and, strangely enough, both these winners
carried greater weights than any of the beaten horses.
Loch Marie’s was 8.11.
• • • •
The A.J.C. asks leave of Randwick Borough
Council to reduce a piece of hilly ground outside the
course but within the borough, from which people may
spy the races gratis. The Council snortingly refuses.
* * * *
It was thought the new Forest Lodge (Sydney)
Pony Club would have given the trotters still another
show, but, so far, it has made no sign. A trot on the
programme always gathers in a large section of the pub-
lic who would not otherwise put in an appearance at a
pony meeting. In the past the proprietors of the courses
have been more to blame than the trotting owners, in
countenancing the most barefaced swindles. Should the
Forest Lodge people take up the game, it is to be hoped,
in the interests of the sport, that from the start the club
will make itself a terror to the evildoer whatever bis
social standing—or the length of his nominations.
• • * •
The life of the average pony-owner fairly
bristles with excitement. At a recent up-country meet-
ing a certain owner went nap on his nag, and made all
preparations for a midnight flit from the hotel where he
was staying if the good thing did not come off. Land-
lord got wind of the affair, and prepared the gates with
extra-strong padlocks with the intention of making all
safe. The good thing dub - eventuated, so the padlocks
were not required ; but pony-owner had prepared for the
emergency by sawing far enough through a couple of
rails at the bottom of the hotel yard to allow of a good
shove releasing panel, owner, and geegees.

“Gun” : One-time-sensational nag Brockleigh,
which died atTumut (N.S.W.) a few days ago, was won
by owners, C. Parker and W. Fisher, in a raffle when
two years old. Parker gave Fisher a “ monkey ” for his
Bhare, and profited immensely thereby. Brockleigh pre-
viously belonged to Chippendale (then sec. Newcastle
J.C.), who would have sold him for £5O.

• • • *
As a result of severe and timely criticism Maori-
land racing stewards have been more active than usual
lately in passing out cronk gents who have been urging
the dishonest horse at a hurried crawl round the course.
Pokomoko, knocked out for two years at Takapuna, with
lessee and rider, was a promising hurdler, who had
actually been hacked for Auckland Two-Mile Cup. When
racing officials take action on their own initiative and;
don’t wait for the crowd to yell and smash things, it’s a
fairly good sign. , * *
The well-bred and speedy trotter, Honest
Wilkes, died in M. L. recently. He was bred in Victoria,
by J. J. Miller, crossed Tasman sea in ’95 as a four-year
old, and in four years won only four races. Reason was
that he could outpace anything for about a mile and
a-half, and then want of staying power cruelled him.
Wilkes was actually disqualified at Christchurch once,
the stewards being of opinion that he h-.Ja’t trotted on
h.s, merits, but they have long since discovered that over
IJ4 miles he hadn’t any merits to trot on.
« * * *
“X.”: On present form, writer (who is not a
Sydney man) would gleefully back N.S.W. to beat Eng-
land on Sydney Ground. Just as S.A., when she had
Giffen, Lyons, Hill, Darling, Reedman, and Jones at their
best, was nearly invincible years ago, so N.S.W. happens
at present to have eight men in her team simultaneously
at their best. The eleven is. almost equal to an Aus-
tralian combination, except that the bowling is weak.
Still, look at the batsmen in the N.S.W. team : Trtiniper
and Noble, twin champions on present form, Iredale,
Gregory, Donnan, M’Kenzie, and Hopkins ! There is no
doubt that the team put out by N.S.W. this season is the
best yet selected by an Australian province.
* * * ♦
Three Sydney cricketers of the team that fought
Melbourne 43 years ago are still alive—Beale, Gilbert, and
Hilliard.
* * * *
The famous Gregory family still supplies crack
cricketers for N.S.W. Charlie, who went to Tas. t’other
day with N.S.W. second XI., is the latest. He’s taller
than brother Sid. and lays the wood on in more brilliant
fashion. Playing against Tas. last year he carried his
bat out for 40. Appearances indicate that he is a comer
of the first quality.
* • * •
Ex-Australasian amateur-champion swimmer
Percy Oavill, now one of the world’s three best pros.,
came home to Sydney last week after two years’ absence
in England, France, Spain, and South Africa. He has
filled out a lot, added about 2st. to his weight, and gained
experience that should be valuable. Percy says he is a
much better swimmer now than when he went away,
which is saying a great deal, for he owned one or two
world’s records then. France proved the most fruitful
hunting ground in the way of money-making. During
three months the crack swimmer gathered in £2O weekly,
half of which came from the French cycling organ, Le
Vklo, for teaching the youth of “ gay Paree ” free. They
had to be put through in big squads, and took to the
game with such aptitude that many of them improved 20
seconds in 100yds. Greasley, Caviil, and Nuttall (cham-
pion of the world) met on the Seine over 500 metres.
Greasley won by about 2ft., Caviil being second and
Nuttall third. But for getting out of his course the
Australian must have had an easy victory. Cavill's
performance in this race brought him—in preference to
the other two—the offer from Le Vblo. He had pre-
viously in England beaten Greasley four races out of five,
and could not make a match with either him or Nuttall
afterwards, so that really Caviil could make out a good
claim for the world’s supremacy. He says that, on the
whole, Australian swimmers are better than those of
Britain, though the latter country holds pride of place in
the old world. He purposes visiting Yankeedom shortly
in quest of the needful, and will go thence to England,
with the object of again endeavoring to lure champion
of the world “ Joey ” Nuttall into a match.
• • * *
Quarter-mile swimming supremacy of N.S.W.,
contested at Bathurst on Monday, was won by Dick
Caviil, the baby of that remarkable family so prolific in
watery champions. Dick is not 16 yet, and this is his
biggest performance so far. He won by 12yds. from
Craig (Balmain) in 6min. 7 3-5 sec., beating the time put
up at Bathurst by previous holder, Lane. Among
those who finished in the rear were Bond (win-
ner of half-mile championship a few weeks ago)
and “Mud” Bishop (champion sprinter and holder
of world’s record for straight-away 100yds.). Dick,
with a frame moulded on the same lines as, the
“old man’s," is far more sturdily built than any of his
crack brothers, and, as he is susceptible of much im-
provement in style, may possibly do even better than
they have done.
* * * *
A likely future swimming champion is young-
Claude Corbett, son of old-time champion, Mr. “ Billy ’
■Corbett. Young Corbett, who is only “ a handful,”
already takes his place on scratch with Bell, the
champion of the Rockdale (Sydney) Swimming Club.
■On Xmas morning the pair started off scratch in a
medley race, and they finished first and second, Corbet.t
missing his kick-off in the last turn, and thereby spoiling
his chance. In the next header competition Corbett
defeated Bell.

Most important boxing match made in the pro-
vinces for many a day was that clinched last week be-
tween Melbourne Tim Hegarty and N.S.W. Hugliie
Napier for featherweight supremacy of the continent and
£lOO a side. Fight is fixed for 29th inst., at Sydney.
Hughie was a good ’un eight or nine years back, especi-
ally when he whipped George Powell in a “go ” that the
old York-st. (Sydney) Club arranged for Powell's especial
benefit. Hughie, however, is considerably older now and
looks every day of it. Tim (in the early twenties) is
probably 10 years younger than his prospective opponent,
and has the reputation of being an out-and-out world
beater at the weight, which would indicate that Napier’s
■chances are on a par with Buckley’s. Yet the Sydney
■milk-trade men, who are among them providirur Hughie’s-
■stake, are no fools in betting, particularly' on the pug.
game. Long years of selling water as cow-product make
milkmen a dodgy class.
* * « *
“ Old Timer ” : The one-time champion Aus-
tralian heavy-weiyht bruiser, Tom Lees, is still on top of
the earth, though dead to the sporting world for years.
Report had it that he went to the Klondyke. Last week:
word came from ’Oine that on Nov. 14, Tom (now 42),
met one Scales (25), in a match the best of 25 rounds.
Beales treated Lees to a terrible gruelling, and knocked
him clean out before the second three minutes expired.
The whipped man was a back-number as a fighter eight
.years ago. His defeat of Yic. blacksmith, Farnhain, was
the best performance he ever put up. Writer
remembers match between Jackson and Lees, at
Foley’s old “ White Horse ” Hall, George-street, Sydney,
■close upon a dozen years since, when the big black
■couldn’t prevail upon his white adversary to stand up
■and fight half-a-minute, but had to chase him about the
24ft. ring for 36 odd rounds and hit wherever he could.
Several times Lees went sprawling face downward as the
result of a weighty punch on the back region while
■dodging out of a corner. That started Jackson’s career
as a champion, and caused Lees to drop to his proper
level. The battle referred to drew a £7OO gate, and.
Foley received £lOO for the use of his hall.
* * * *
Late news is to the effect that “ feather” cham-
pion of the world black Dixon crawled all over English
■crack Curley in a 25 rounds affair at New York, Nov. 2.
This is the third in quick succession of England’s top-
■notchers to go down before Yankee cracks. Dixon is the
biggest wonder in the game ; he has been at it a dozen
;years, fighting as often as foemen came—occasionally'
three times a week—and kept his end up right through.

M.L. bruiser “Torpedo” Billy Murphy (the
tailor pug.) is still in the game, or at least was up to a.
month or so since, when he broke his right arm over the
hard head of Larry Gleason, at Sioux City (U.S.A.) Billy-
had his man licked just prior to the accident happening..
It’s marvellous how this mass of whipcord and wire keeps
■going. Seems like a lifetime since writer first heard of
Murphy-. He always was a terrific smiter. Nine or ten
years ago a similar accident occurred to his left arm, and;
dozens of times the knuckles of both hands have been
badly knocked about and fractured. Billy says he’ll be
ready for another “ dust-up ” three months hence. Grit
and inclination are there to the full, but will Nature
■stand it? The erstwhile world's champion featherweight
■ must be close on 40.

The wrestling Dunns, as fit as fiddles, after »
spell of hard mining graft, are doing tolerably well in
ALL. in “gate” and side-wager contests. At Grey-
mouth, Harry Dunn beat a local Caledonian in five
different styles, in 22min. 37sec. An hour was the
stipulated time. Dunn, who was conceding his opponent
7st., is now offering to wrestle any man on the planet.

* » * •
Famous long-distance cyclist Teddy Hale, now
engaged “down under” in the exceptionally exact-
ing task of biking 100 miles daily (Sundays excepted)
for 12 months, completed his first 10,900 miles Nov. 23,
and was going as strong as at the outset. The daily task
has been tackled and accomplished—no matter what the
weather—since July 31.
* • » *
The next challenger for the America Cup may
possibly hail from St. John’s, Newfoundland, where a
Roman Catholic priest is devising a craft which, it is
believed, will revolutionise the art of boat-building.
The soundness of the holy man’s theory is said to have
been fully demonstrated in a race to Bermuda. Sir
Thomas Lipton says he will not contest the Cup until
1901, but that if anyone wishes to make a bid for the Cup
during 1900 he will place the Shamrock at his disposal for
trial matches. Sir Thomas now gives the total cost of
the Shamrock at £lOO,OOO, not including the large ex-
penses contingent upon running the races.
• • • •
“ Colocynth Have no real belief in gamblers’
talismans, but three extraordinary occurrences have
recently come within my own knowledge. First: Sat in
at a game of poker—not draw—from early evening till
midnight without getting a bankable pair. Recollected
an old muinbo-jumbo about burying a thrum at a
chimney corner at midnight, and excusing myself from
the tabie conformed to the superstition. First hand I
picked up on my return was a heart-flush, and I rose the
board out of its boots, and had them all working for me
for a fortnight. Second : A man of my acquaintance, an
insurance manager, who doesn’t know a horse from a hoe
and had never gambled in his life, had obtained as a
curiosity a sovereign which had been for months under
water in the strong-room of the wrecked steamer Catter-
thun. On impulse, he registered the stained “yellow-
hoy ”to Tattersall and drew a £lOO prize. Third : My
egg - merchant’s brother, a sea captain, shot a black
Thomas cat which had been killing his chickens. Shortly
after sailed on a twelve months’trip to Chili and Frisco
and returned home to find his wife the mother of twins.
They weren’t his twins either.
• * * •
With the holiday season coming on, and num-
bers of people either going themselves or sending their
families to the mountains, the enterprising burglars’ har-
vest has begun. Provident people, however, are provid-
ing against his visits by storing their valuables—plate,
jewellery, papers, etc. —at the Sydney Safe Deposit, Ash-
street (back of Palings), and business there is proportion-
ately brisk. We would recommend Bulletin readers to
call and inspect the massive vaults, and ascertain terms
for storage. Safes may now be rented upon reduced
terms, and boxes are received for storage at very moder-
ate rates.*
* * * *
The lighting of Sydney Cricket Ground by
Acetylene gas, at the recent night carnival of wheelmen,
was a success that will make the Acetylene Gas Co.
famous all over Australasia. The great light shone like
day—an installation of 3000 burners throwing out splendid
beams without interruption or flicker. Public and
cyclists alike were highly pleased ; and congratulations
have poured in to Tyree, the energetic managing director
of the co.—the man who designed the plant. It is ex-
pected that, after this sensational success, many country
towns will adopt Acetylene gas for lighting their streets.*

Melbourne’s terrible Evening Herald,
the other day, published two-thirds of a column
of bubbly matter, headed “Selections from our
special war correspondent’s letter.” In all, there
were 12 extracts, and nine of them ended with
“ Extract from the letter of our special war cor-
respondent, Major W. T. Reay.” Reay was the
editor of the Herald before he went with the Vie.
contingent, and no doubt will resume the post
when he returns. And, as he is desperately proud
of his shoddy little title, it is doubtless good policy
on the part of his late subordinates to mention
nine times that he is a whole major.
In a wild endeavour to prove Victoria’s
loyalty and devotion to the Empire, her public
men and newspapers have persuaded that province
to make a pretty bad exhibition of itself. When
the first contingent for South Africa was called
for, hundreds who had, according to newspaper
skite, trampled over each other to volunteer, didn’t
turn up, the excuse being that they missed the
early trains ; while 6 per cent, of those who were
passed and accepted by the military and medical
inspectors practically deserted —in other words
they didn’t eventuate when wanted. These facts
were covered up as far as possible, and when the
second contingent was asked for local public men
and newspapers again set out on the boost.
McLean, the new Premier, rushed the War Office |
with an offer of 300 mounted men. Invitations
were issued to the 800 mounted riflemen of the
province to volunteer, but only 131 of all ranks
sent in their names. Up to this point, however,
nothing cowardly could be alleged, for it was
distinctly to the credit of the other 6G9 mounted
riflemen that they didn’t volunteer. The mean-
ness came whet), after having their names pub-
lished as volunteers, only 119 of the 131 turned up
for inspection. Nine per cent, was a pretty large
disappearance at this early stage of the business.
The excuse made by the Jingoistic crowd was that
most of the men are farmers and have the lame cow
and the bogged sheep to look after. Then out of the
119 who paraded only 93 were found fit for service
which looks as if the alleged troops of Victoria
weren’t healthy. Fearing more disappearances,
authorities had the selected men sworn in imme-
diately after they were passed. Last time the men
were not sworn in until a few days before their
departure ; they were treated for some weeks
as volunteers and gentlemen, with the result
that they left with impunity. Despite all the
official promptitude there was one loss. Just
before the Bible was handed to him one of the
men who had gone through the farce of volunteer-
ing, having his name published, being locally lion-
ised and being passed medically and otherwise,
suddenly stood out of the ranks, and as he marched
off said, “I’m not going; I’ve changed my
mind.” So 92 out of 800 warriors are going to the
front—at least the authorities have so far fixed 92
that they will be liable to severe pains and
penalties if they desert.
It is pretty generally known that if solid
alkaline substances are allowed to remain in the
pores of the skin they will act injuriously ; and it
is for this reason that we should choose evaporat-
ing alkalies for toilet purposes. One that can be
confidently recommended is Bcrubb’.s Cloudy
Fluid Ammonia, which serves an infinity of
purposes, being a refreshing and pungent addition
to the bath, cool and cleansing as a wash for the
head, and a water softener of great value.*

Another Boxing Day outrage (the Gatton
horror took place on Boxing Day, 1898), is re
ported from Queensland. A little girl named
Le Blowitz has been murdered and mutilated
(apparently by a sex-maniac), near Childers, in
the Bundaberg district; and, so far, there is no
clue to the murderer.
The Union S.S. Co.’s splendid steamer
Waikare left Sydney on Saturday with a load of
passengers for a trip round the Maoriland sounds.
This is becoming the regular summer health resort
for the hot, tired Australian. There are glaciers,
and eternal snows, and icy cataracts, and stupen-
dous frosty peaks, and big, black, cold shadows
on deep, blue, cold water, all within three days of
the hot, oozy cities of Melbourne and Sydney,
and even for the man who can't get away this is a
cheerful thing to reflect upon.
R. A. Crouch, who opposed J. M. Davies
for Vic. House of Landlords, is the most live man
who has threatened to invade that tomb for many
years past. He advocated the minimum wage,
the Turner Factories Act, and State banking ;
also, he told the 48 Rich Landlords that they were
a quarter-century behind the times, and hinted
that what they most wanted was decent burial
under a nice, clean monument. There was a
great shaking of wise bell-toppers all down Col-
lins-street the day after Crouch’s first public
appearance, and men with large sums of money
carefully invested at 4 per cent, declared that the
foundations of society were being undermined.
But the good old Council property-vote stood by
Davies, who got 2723 votes to Crouch’s 1474.

At Poverty Point.
In Auckland (M.L.), the late Martin Simonsen was once
exceedingly wrath because he could not give a Sunday
concert, owing to the theatre being engaged by the
Y.M.G A. At the Sunday morning's rehearsal, Martin
warned bis company to put all their things away and lock
their dressing-room doors, because, as he said, “ Vhen
deni Christian young mans is aboudt, I von’t be resbon-
sible for anyding dat’s inizzing.”
At a recent meeting of the Sydney Musicians’Benefit
Association, the president (G. R. Allpress), in remark-
ing upon the value of =uch societies, observed that, had
such a one existed in Melbourne, it would probably have
saved poor old Simonsen from suicide. And no doubt a
little opportune help would have gone a long way.
A Melbourne bard has written a new song—“ Remem-
ber Majuba.” It is altogether behind the limes : Tugela
River and other places now require all the loose remem-
brance the country has about it.
“ F.”: J. C. Williamson gives as one reason why Sydney is
abetter theatrical town than Melbourne, that thexpress of
the former city devotes more attention to the theatre,
the truth is, that there is no such thing as dramatic
criticism in Melbourne. The usual “notice” of a play
in the daily papers is an outpouring of unveracious
slobber, in return for an advertisement. On one or two
occasions when the dog reporter has been put on to do
a “ notice” instead of the poultry expert, or the sporting
Writer instead of the military man, he has signalised the
experiment by making a splash and telling the truth in a
weak incompetent way; but after one try he has been
suppressed.
Seventy-seven-year-old Sims Reeves, erstwhile king of
English tenors, is said to be in pretty straightened cir-
cumstances, although he must have made sacks of money
in his time. Reeves made his first appearance as a bari-
tone (Rodolfo, in the “ Sonnambula "), but, after study-
ing in Italy under Mazzucato, he made his real debut in
Italian opera at the Scdla, Milan, as Edgardo, in Lucia,
with Catherine Hayes in the name part, and aroused
enthusiasm in the most critical audience in the world.
The frequency with which Reeves used to disappoint his
audiences gave rise to the notion that he “ toped,” but
those who knew him could vouch for his abstemiousness.
Offended dignity, not drink, was often the trouble.
Latest song from the 'Awx :—
“Melb.”: Lnnnen was the original O’Blooligan, in
Little Christopher Columbus.” Sinclair is a mixture of
Teddy and Bert Gilbert. I fail to discern the slightest
evidence that he can act at all. What is acting? A
man’s enduing himself with some character apart from
himself. Mr. Sinclair changes a lot cf clothes. The
wonder that grows is how a man can put on so many
clothes in the course of an evening. Well, Heury Lee
showed what could be done in this regard. But he acted,
too, in a wonderful style. Sinclair makes me think more
of a gymnast than an actor. Also, he makes me think of
a tailor’s shop.
Melba was less reckless than some of the theatrical per-
sonages who gave entertainments in aid of the Soldiers’
Widows and Orphans’ Fund. Canny David Mitchell's
daughter announced that a “ proportion ” of her concert
receipts at Albert Hall would be devoted to the fund.
Rather vague that. Proportions run in all sizes.
Charles Arnold’s diplomacy in billing himself as the
“Great Anglo-Dutch Comedian ” served him very well in
the Transvaal until the Boers got their orders to go to
the front and fight. Then they suddenly discovered that
our friend, Hans the Boatman, was more Anglo than
Dutch (by the way, he is a Swiss), and he had to clear out
of the country with his co. on top of a wave of derision.
They were two visitors to a menagerie. One said to
the other : “You see that tiger? It’ll eat off your hand.”
“ Will it? I’ll try itand, placing a biscuit on his palm,
he passed it into the cage. The tiger promptly swallowed
two fingers and a thumb. “ There,” said his companion,
“I told you it would eat-off your hand, but I never
thought you would be fool enough to give it the chance.”
Irving’s Robespierre, at the Knickerbocker Theatre,
New York, made a tremendous hit the first night.
Clement Scott, for the Herald, and Winter, for the
Tribune, agreed that he never acted better.
Fred Sinclair’s parody of Oinquevalli’s business, now
visible at Melb. Royal, is rather a barren show, after
Whitburn’s boisterous burlesque over the road. Whit-
burn retained his burnt cork, and went into green tights
and hob-nailed boots like sheep-trucks, and yet contrived
areally clever physical caricature of “Cinky,” whilst
knocking together a quantity of rattling pantomime.
William’s performance with the penny pink balloon sup-
posed to represent the 401 b. cannon-ball was the liveliest
kind of farce, but it did not please the house like the
cheap indecency of the common yahoo.
“J.A.C.”: Once saw an amateur performance of
“ Robert Macaire ” in Kutnara, M.L., and, at the close of
the last act, when the protagonist was endeavoring to
escape through a window, he got jammed, and presented
to the audience a pleasing view of his Antarctic regions.
Then two gendarmes rushed on, and taking an unfair
advantage, fired point-blank at the target thus presented.
Shortly afterwards Robert|was led in on a supposed dying
condition with—two dreadful wounds on his shirt-front ! \
Bland Holt’s company had anew experience recently
at Gisborne (M.L.) A lady who had no one to leave the
baby with brought it to the theatre in a perambulator,
went round unobserved to the back part of the building,
and left baby there in the vehicle with, the family dog
watchin"- over it. The yells of the deserted one drew at-
tention about the middle of Act 1., and thereafter the
members of the co., as they happened to be off duty, took
turns at nursing it and saying “ ketchy-ketohy ” to it.
The heavy Bill Sikes murderer, who had only a small part
that night, got most of the nursing to do.
Jusb as The Bulletin was going to press
it was announced that a portion of the Australian
Contingent had been mistaken by the British for
Boers, and fired upon with fatal consequences.
How many fatal consequences not explained.
Asa result, the Australians have been ordered
to wear helmets instead of the slouched felt hats.
The Boers wear slouched felt hats—and some of
them shave, in spite of the illustrated papers.
Anyhow, there is not much glory in being mis-
taken for a beastly Boer and shot from behind by
your own friend Tommy, burning to avenge
Majuba Hill.
The bravery of some of those “ patriots ”
who yell “ Gorsave ” and “ Soldiers of the Queen ’
the loudest is amazing. At Broken Hill, the
other night, a holiday crowd which had mixed its
“patriotism ” with its beer (the weather had been
114* in the shade for a couple of days) resolved to
“wipe out” the peaceful German Club, which
hadn’t committed any worse offence than to prefer
lager to tanglehoof . A German who happened to
fill the doorway was turned over on his ear by a
large number of patriots, and the crowd poured
in, some hooting, the rest still yelling “Soldiers
of the Queen.” Just then one solitary Oft. con-
stable turned up, cleared the club-house, and
faced the howlers, ,vvho now (to show what good
citizens they were, no doubt) began to yell—
“ Down with the copper ! ” This brought out the
constable’s revolver ; and the way that crowd
suddenly remembered that it was getting late was
amusing. Twice afterwards a crowd—mostly the
same crowd—“ proceshed ” to the club, and just
as often three constables were enough to send it
off.
Vanity is the saviour of tho human race :
If we could see ourselves as others see us, the
rivers would be full of suicides.
A public meeting was held at Hobart Town
IIhII to raise a “Patriotic Fund.’’ It was
attended by a handful of people, mostly members
of Parliament (the general election being near)
and civil servants, who are expected to be to the
fore on all these occasions. Wisely, the plate or
hat wasn’t sent round. It would have been awk-
ward to announce that “ a sum of .£4O was col-
lected in the room.” Premier Lewis came down
with records from the Govt offices to show that
£2(500 was raised for “patriotic” purposes in
Tasmania when the Crimean war was on. Pro-
bably that .£2OOO won’t be got this time. Maybe
it’ll be £260. All the old officials and carrion
crows of the Pension department, who fed and
fattened on loyalty, are dead and gone, and the
average Tasmanian native has more interest in
apples than bullets.
I’m off in the morning to Australia,
I can't stand it any more.
Marriage with me has been a failure;
I must have been a mug—oh, Lor’ 1
I’ve led a very wretched life,
E’er since the day that I got wed ;
How can a man live happy with his wife
If she’s always eating biscuits in the bed ?
THE MECHANICAL STAFF
—“ detervc great credit for," &c. —Theatrical notice.
[FOR THE UBLLETIN.]
No papers print our portraits with an interviewer’s frill,
We get a grudging mention at the bottom of the bill,
We never paint up pretty to pose beneath a lime,
But it’s plug and push and persevere and battle all the
time.
We are just the Mechanical Staff,
And we sweat on the actors’ behalf.
Our chisels and hammers build up all their drammers
And never get rounds or a laugh.
Our wardrobe’s tattered dungarees and ruins of a shirt,
Our make-up lined with dust and fluff, our rouge assorted
dirt,
But silken stars are hustled when we’re racing with a flat,
And interfering satin skirts must pay for what they’re at.
“ Whatl paint on yer yellow sateen ?
Very sorry, Miss, thought you’d have seen !”
(Curse these women-run shows —will put in her nose
And see how we set his pet scene 1)
We’ve worries chasing us to bed to dream on till we’re
mad
With puzzling how to realize the Guv’nor’s latest fad,
And day-long night-long jugglery with battens, screws
and nails,
And his blarst and damn-you ‘Thank you’ when his
pet idea fails ;
Some sensation that’s never been done
Ten catastrophes banged into one,
A new way of thrilling grown kids at a shilling
Thunder-sheet and Rpd-flre by the ton 1
Oh, the hot confusion on a roaring opening night,
Every man with work enough for fifty fingers left and
right
Hurry-scurry—wear and worry—screw and scramble,
' pull and maul
For a set not seen three minutes, just to get the Guv. a
call !
They’re thundering the roof off the place—
But the din is out-done by two bass
Curses, creamy and gory on us props of his glory
’Cos the lime wasn’t fair on his face 1
They say our speech is snappy, but how can you expect
God-bless-vou elocution when a set is nearly wrecked.
And drunken flymen lower prisons in a festal village
green,
And we have to swear in whispers ’cos it’s someone’s
dying scene?
When it's weeping and border lights blue,
With slow music to humour it through,
We are tearing our hair, and as loud as we dare—
“ Where the hell is your cut-cloth in Two ? ”
