The Upheaval of Ponder.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Let it be granted that Ponder was a small man,
physically and otherwise ; that Ponder possessed
a wife, young and pretty ; that Ponder was under
the pleasing, though mistaken, impression that he
loved his wife ; also that his love was returned,
though this last is a matter of no moment, for
Ponder was small and not worth consideration.
Now Ponder, being a commercial man, was
often detained late in the city, and on such occa-
sions would adjourn to a certain restaurant for his
tea. When a man has meals regularly, or even
irregularly if fairly often, at the one place, it is
desirable that he shall sit always at the one table,
to be attended by the same waitress —whereby
they become acquainted, even friendly. Now
Ponder attended to this matter and also brought
the girl flowers and chocolates, and sometimes
even took her to the theatre, which was pleasant,
though unprofitable.
Ponder was of a poetastic turn of mind, and had
christened his charmer “ Rosebud,” which was an
absurd misnomer, for the lady aptly answered
Squire Western’s description of Lady Bellaston
in Fielding's “ Tom Jones,” which description is
terse and graphio but here unprintable.
Rosebud’s real name was May, and Ponder
thought that, duly prepared and “ presently ”
approached, she might and would, as in time she
actually did. All of which caused the small man
considerable expenditure of time and money, also
several severe tremors, when of an evening cer-
tain of his friends accidentally encountered the
happy pair. But all subsequent insinuations and
ribald railings Ponder would meet with a superior
smile and the remark “ Purely Platonic, my boy ;
purely Platonic.” He indeed admitted to one
intimate friend that “ he had not as yet taken his
wife into his confidence in the matter,” nor had
he informed the charming Rosebud of the exist-
ence of said wife—which caused him much trouble
later on. For presently the lady thought it desir-
able that a marriage should eventuate, and so
suggested, whereupon the little man was obliged
to own up ? and there were recriminations and
scenes. Finally the girl was persuaded to be
“ reasonable,” and Ponder promised to look after
her and “ treat her well ” —which is an exceedingly
elastic phrase, and may mean much or little.
It was when Ponder, junior, had arrived and
had begun to be an ever-increasing expense that
Rosebud’s demands became more and more press-
ing, so much so that Ponder at length threatened
to restrict her to the legal allowance for an “un-
attached ” youngster.
Then the lady showed her hand—and it was by
no means so soft and velvety as Ponder had
thought it, and had often told her. His little
Rosebud turned out to be armed with many
thorns. In his foolish moments —and they were
frequent—that gentleman had addressed certain
very loving epistles to his charmer, and these she
had treasured with care. So when he had pro-
pounded his ultimatum the girl laughed in his face
and remarked that she was going over his way
that afternoon and would take “ baby ” and the
letters and call on the lawful Mrs. Ponder. She
had no doubt the ensuing interview would be
interesting, especially as Mrs. P. was childless.
Popder thought so too ; in fact, he was so sure of
it that he capitulated at once, and Rosebud re-
turned to her bower satisfied —for the time being.
Matters progressed smoothly thereafter for a
little while, until once more the limitations of his
income goaded Ponder to rebellion. The girl
tried tears. Ponder hardened his alleged heart.
She must return his letters, and he would pay
over a lump sum and so much a week for the
child—no more. The girl agreed and went home,
to bring the incriminatory correspondence next
day.
The following morning Ponder was in his office
awaiting the lady, when a well-dressed, well-set-
up young man was shown in.
“ Good morning. You are Mr. Ponder ? ”
“ That is my name. What can Ido for you ? ”
“Well, it all depends. I just dropped in to
tell you that I’m thinking of commencing divorce
proceedings against my wife, and — ”
“My good man, what has that got to do with
me ? Why don’t you see a solicitor ? ”
“And, as I was going to say when you inter-
rupted, I’m also thinking of naming you as co-
respondent. I thought you might like to know.”
Ponder stared at the man blankly, that indivii
dual maintaining an appearance of stolid indiffer-
ence.
“ You see,” he went on slowly, “my name’s
Lenoir; my wife’s first name is May. She tells
me you call her Rosebud.”
Ponder sank back in his chair and collapsed.
This was the very last thing he had thought of.
As he slowly pulled himself together the man
observed in a casual, irrelevant sort of way, “You
didn’t know she was married, did *y° u > Mr.
Ponder ? She looks such a confiding, innocent,
little thing. I always thought so myself. Ah !
looks are deceptive ; she knows more than me and
you put together.”
Ponder roused himself. “What do you want?”
The man coolly looked Ponder up and down,
then returned, still in the same careless tone:
“ Well, you know, if I elected you co-respondent
your letters would make very pretty exhibits,
wouldn’t they? I’d get heavy damages, besides
the costs. Yes,” he mused thoughtfully, “ it’s
awkward—for you—but, of course, it might be
staved off-it might be ”
Then the harassed Ponder roused himself and
tried to grapple with the situation. He argued
long and vainly, endeavoring to purchase future
immunity with present cash down, but his tor-
mentor savv his weakness and was obdurate.
“ It’s no go ;no go. However, I won’t worry
you too much, I’ll ”
Well, the final result was just a little cheque
now and again.
Like an insect pinned to a card, Ponder lived,
and squirmed, and suffered ; not having the pluck
to end it either by braving the bully, as a few do,
or hy flight or suicide, as is more often the case.
Day by day he grew thinner and greyer until he
seemed almost on the point of utter collapse.
About this time, also, he noticed a change in his
wife’s demeanor; there seemed growing up
between them a want of confidence, as of a secret
kept back. Sometimes he thought she knew of
his faithlessness, and was waiting for him to
speak. Once he had made up his mind to do so,
then drew back, afraid he might lose her alto-
gether.
At length, as winter approached and business
grew slack, Ponder packed his portmanteau,
kissed his vvife, and slipped away on a Maoriland
steamer for a month’s holiday—a month of rest
and respite. -
When he stepped on the Melbourne wharf on
his return he looked a new man. The holiday
:had cleared ' his brain and restored him mentally
and morally, as well as physically. He returned
resolved to make a clean breast to his wife and
face whatever might be the outcome. Arriving
in the morning, he walked firmly and strongly up
the street to his office. In his inside room was a
small pile of private letters ; on the topmost one
the handwriting of his wife caught his eye. He
had not heard from home during his trip, her
letters having apparently missed him, but she
knew by what boat he was returning, so he quietly
sat down and opened the note without misgivings.
It was the sort of screed usually penned by a
woman when she takes the step that closes the
door of respectabilitythe step that to that
woman seems so large, to a few men lairger still,
to many a mere trifle. The writer indulged in no
recriminations, no reproaches; she simply told him
that he did not understand her and that she was
incapable of appreciatinghim, and that she accepted
the proffered love and protection of his friend.
Ponder was not just then in the humour to ap-
preciate the delicate sarcasm of the missive; it
appeared _ to him simply as a common-place,
ordinary instance of treachery and faithlessness.
Partly owing to his newly-recovered strength of
mind and partly to the fact that he did not really
love his wife, Ponder was not greatly stirred by
the information. He sat awhile in his chair,
apparently studying a fly-spot on the paper-
weight ; then he slowly and deliberately lit a
cigarette—a cigar would have sickened, a pipe
have killed him. Finally, he leant back in his
chair and laughed out loudly, boisterously.
In the middle of his merriment, came a knock
at the door.
“Come in ! ” It was his friend, Lenoir.
Ponder eyed him silently.
“Morning, Mr. Ponder. You’re looking well.
They told me you were away for a trip. I
thought of calling upon Mrs. P., but it wouldn't
have been the square thing between friends,
would it? . . , And you’ve treated me well, so
far.”
At the mention of his wife, Ponder broke out
laughing again, but otherwise he might have been
part of the wooden chair he was occupying. This
indifference seemed to disturb Lenoir somewhat.
“ Nothing to say, old man ? ” said he. “ Baby’s
well; growing into a fine lad. I just called about
some small items that want looking to.”
He put his hand into his coat pocket apparently
to get a paper, and at the same moment Ponder
grasped him by the coat-collar, and, before the
surprised visitant could realise what was happen-
in J7> he was forcibly run out of the room and
rushed down the steps across the pavement into
the gutter.
“Now,”said the breathless little man, “if ever
you show your monkey face in my place again I’ll
thrash you till I break every bone in your miser-
able carcase; and if you or your wife write so
much as a single line to me, I’ll put the police
after you. Now get! ”
Then Ponder, who, for once in his little life,
had risen to the occasion and played the man, re-
turned to his office and to his former level of dull
mediocrity, and as he never emerged therefrom
his subsequent history is uninteresting.
FRANCIS CROSON.
At Dinner.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We were having dinner at the parlor (or two-bob)
table in the Canecutter’s Curse, the leading hotel
of Twowharves. Just as we started, in blustered
a hot, fat commercial-traveller. He stared us all
out of countenance and then said : “Ididenear
th’ dinna bell.”
No one replied. He pulled and poked at almost
every dish on the table. He severely criticised
the beef, the vegetables, the bread, and even the
salt, until Mary, the busy little parlpr-maid, was
greatly impressed. She stood afar off and
watched the great man with secret awe, evidently
believing that the Governor could not be a more
important individual. The drummer grumbled
and growled and spluttered half-way through
dinner, then suddenly : “ Wot brand’s this ’ere
jam ? ” he snapped.
( ‘‘Moir’s, I think, sir,” said Mary timidly.
“ That’s what missus generally gits fa th’ parlor-
table.”
“ Well, look ’ere, me girl, w’en I dine I do dine.
I ham accustomed to ’ave the very best of hevery-
think! So bring me a fresh tin, ’n’ I’ll see fa
meself.”
. The tin was brought. The commercial opened
it and flopped the marmalade out, unbroken, on a
plate.
“ Ah— that’s somethink like, ya know ! That’s
the only marmalade fit for a i/entlemari 1 ”
Then for about ten minutes he was silent, save
for the horrible noises he made in stoking liitn-
sel f.
Then a long, grave individual arose at the head
of the table and rang the bell.
“What is the brand of this beef, Mary?” he
asked when the girl appeared.
“ I don’t know, sir.”
“Hum! ha!—also haw !—can I view the rest
of the carcase, with a view to discovering the
brand and establishing identity?”
“I dunnOj sir,” and Mary giggled.
“ Well, can I see the butcher? ”
“ Please, sir, I can’t say. Per’aps he ain’t at
home. He kills a Toosdees and Thursdees.”
“ Well, then, inform the landlord that I desire
at once to be informed of the brand of this beef,
and also of the brands of the bread—sugar- tea—
salt —butter —fly-paper—the name of your milk-
man ; and, in addition— ”
But here the drummer rose, and shambled out,
scowling.
JIMMIE PANNIKIN.


Another Bad Liver.
ANOTHER NOYOCASTRIAN
FINDS A
Welcome Friend in Need.
The Case of
Mr. W. T. SCOURGE.
(a remarkable recitation.)
“I used to be one of the happiest, jollieafc
fellows alive, always ready for a joke or a
laugh,” said Mr. V\ illiam Thomas Scource,
of Euston Terrace, close to Croydon Railway
Station, Adelaide when he was interviewed
by our reporter; “ but when I fell into bad
health all my lightheartedness vanished. I
could see or think of nothing to smile at—
gloom and despondency became my portion,
and my life was a misery without a single
ray of brightness.”
“ Will you tell me about your trouble,
Mr. Scource ?” enquired the man with the
note-book, his interest fairly excited.
“It was this way,” said Mr. Scource.
“At that time I was working at employment
that made my hours and meals very ir-
regular. I had always been a strong man
for my size, and, with the carelessness of good
health, took no count of it; but I have no
’oubt tins was the primary cause of my later
suffering, for gradually 1 fell into a bad
slate of health. When I got up in the
morning I would ieel more tired than when
I went to bed, and my limbs would hang to
me limp and languid. My mouth always
had a nasty taste, and my tongue was white-
coated witli fur. My appetite left me ; often
and often I have taken my lunch to work and
brought it home untasted ; and when I did
eat it was quite common for me to retch the
whole of it up again directly afterwards.
My complexion became sallow and yellow
as an Indian. I suffered constantly from a
pain in the side under the heart. I think
it was the wind off my stomach that used to
get there. The spot got so tender that I
could not bear even the weight of a waist-
coat over it. I suffered with a racking pain
in the top of the head, a bilious headache,
and a pain between the shoulders and down
the neck. But my worst trouble of all was
the pain across the loins ; it would take me
so sometimes, when I had laid down to rest
and tried to move, that you could have heard
me Sing out a mile away. In getting up in
the morning this pain would render me
quite stiff till my back had been well rubbed.
My eyes in the morning were quite bloodshot,
and when I looked about objects appeared
to whirl round and round, and I could see
little dancing stars. My night’s rest was
broken, and gave me no refreshment. I was
slow to doze off, and when I did I was soon
awake again, startled by some horrible
,di earn. In my sleep my mind could conjure
up nothing but horrors—something dreadful
was happening to me—l was falling off a cliff,
or tumbling down a well without a bottom,
and just as I was about to be crushed, the
nervous fear of the moment woke me up. I
became very thin in body, and the trouble
at my heart caused me much anxiety. It
would go all of a flutter, and when I went to
take a long breath I was caught up short
with a sharp, pricking pain. One of my
worst symptoms w’as mental despondency ;
the bile in my system seemed to cloud my
brain, and make all things dismal, hopeless,
and distressing. When I was away from
home I was forever thinking some misfortune
would happen there ; and these groundless
fears made such an impression on me that
directly I returned I would enquire anxiously
of the wife as to their truth. But to find
mv fears w ithout foundation on one day was
fas ©from preventing me dwelling on the
possibilities of like calamities the next. And
when you consider that lam, when in health,
naturally of a light-hearted and jovial dis-
position, you will understand how serious
my case had become. It is true I never
gave up work ; but many and many a day it
would have been better for me if I had.
When in good health I am not afraid to do
a day’s work beside any man in the country ;
but a long course of the symptoms I haver
described brought me very low. Aud 1
noticed I was always worse on Sundays and
during hot weather, and continually I was
belching up nasty wind. This wind, as 1
think 1 told you, would gather round
heart, giving me great discomfort till it had
shifted or come away. To help this I often
took a cup of hot tea. The food I did eat
did,me no good and gave me no strength,
and when I vomited it up, I noticed that it
was in just the same state as when swal-
lowed. It appeared as though my stomach
was for the time paralysed, and unable to
perform its functions. And all this time,
remember, I was stiff and crippled with the
horrible pain in the small of the hack. A
pain they call the kidney pain (a popular
error—it is not “ kidney pain” ; it’s a reflex
liver pain.— Ed.), that would catch me at
times after a sudden movement, with a sharp
agony like a knife going into me. You car
see that altogether my condition was such
as to cause me the gloomiest forebodings,
apart from the despondency caused bv *’
complaint itself.”
“ Yet, I understand recovered, ml.
Scource?” enquired the pressman.
“Yes; and I will tell you how it came
about. 1 was looking over the paper one
day, when I saw a published testimonial
from a person who had been cured of symp-
toms like my own. It was that of a New-
castle patient. Now I happen to come from
Newcastle, New South Wales, myself, and
know thegentleman. Thisgave me confidence
that the testimonial was genuine. '* The
medicine mentioned as curing my friend was
Clements Tonic ; so I immediately bought a
bottle, and I am pleased to say that my ex-
perience was only a repetition of the thou-
sands of others who have tried that great
remedy. Clements Tonic started to cure
me from the jump, so to speak. First I got
a good appetite, then a good night’s rest ;
the heart trouble, the bilious headaches, the
wind and indigestion disappeared. The
pain between my shoulders and side went
away; aud--greatestcomfort of all—the stiff,
horrible pain across the loins' gradually eased
and disappeared. There is no doubt that
Clements Tonic fitted my case, and found
out and relieved all my bad symptoms with
a rapidity hardly credible to one who has
not experienced it and proved it.”
“ You state, Mr. Scource, that by reading
a testimonial you were led to try this remedy.
May I hope that you will do as you have
been done by, and pass the glad tidings on
to others.”
“ If you wish me to give you a testimonial
that will be published, I do not object.
Priut it as you like ; though I am far from
being anxious to see my name in type.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION
I, William Thomas Scource, of Euston Terrace,
Croydon, Adelaide, in the Province of South Aus-
tralia, do solemnly and sincerely declare that I have
carefully read the annexed document, consisting* of
four folios, and consecutively numbered from one to
four, and that it contains and is a true and faithful
account of my illness and cure by Clements Tome,
and also contains my full permission to publish in
any manner my statements - which I give voluntarily,
and without receiving any payment; and I make this
solemn declaration conscientiously believing the same
to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of an Act
made and passed in the sixth year of the reign of
his late Majesty King William the Fourth, intituled
“An Act to repeal an Act of this present Session of
Parliament, intituled ‘An Act for the more effectual
abolition of Oaths and Affirmations taken and made
in the various Departments of the State, and to sub-
stitute Declarations in lieu thereof, and for the more
entire suppression of voluntary and extra-judicial
Oaths and Affidavits, and to make other provisions
for the abolition of unnecessary Oaths.’ ”

A Tight Place.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
For a brief experimental,
Mild and gentle,
Sort o’ spree
Saltbush William was in Sydney
With others of his kidney ;
Every man an Ananias,
Seasoned liars
Conscience free!
But the wily William wore his
Simple smile, and told us stories
Of the kind in which he glories,
Same as me!
“ Now, you chaps are pretty gritty
In the city,”
Murmured Bill.
“ But you go to North Australia
And you’ll find your courage fail yer;
You’ll get scared the same as I did,
That’s provided
There is still
That same tribe of fighting niggers ;
Them as scared away the diggers
When they cocked their rifle triggers
Dead to kill.
“ ’Twas them niggers cut the cable,
Sure as Abel
Murdered Cain !
Thought it was a kind of fencing
That the white man was commencing.
And as soon as it was mended
They upended
It again !
What d’ye say ? ’Twas Cain killed Abel ?
Have yer way about the fable ;
Still them niggers cut the cable ;
That was plain.
“ Well, the Darwin line’s important
And it oughtn’t
To be snagged,
Or that blanky Kruger’s capers
Can't be printed in the papers !
And the Government, it figures
That them niggers
M list be bagged ;
So they sends me out from Cooper's
Creek with five-and twenty troopers ;
If I ever see such supers
I’ll be lagged !
“First we camped at Charlotte Waters,
Fairish quarters
In the main ;
Then we crossed Mac Donnell Ranges,
Where of c mrse the country changes.
When we saw them niggers camping
We was tramping
’Cross the plain.
They was sort o’ yellow color,
Like the Chinese, only duller ;
Thick as flies in Cunnamnlla
After rain!
“Well, at first they didn’t spot us,
But they got us
In a bit.
And they came along a-yelling
In a way that beats the telling ;
And you never heard such howling !
Trooper Dowling
Took a fit!
On they came, them savage whoop ts,
And I calls upon the troopers,
But the crowd of scurvy supers
Turned and flit!
“ They was city bred and frightened
And I mightn’t,
If I'd known,
Ever ventured with the beggars
Out against them savage niggers,
For a kiddie could have cleft me
When they left rue
All alone !
I ain’t anxious to disgrace ’em,
But for cows you couldn’t place ’em !
Fancy leaving me to face ’em ”
On my own ! ”
“Asit happened I was saddled,
And skedaddled
You can bet!
With them niggers rushing after,
Full of howlin’ shouts o’ laughter.
And, a-thing that very strange is,
In the ran ves
I was S''t !
And my confidence was shaken,
For, in hopes to save my bacon,
’Twas the ivromj ravine I’d taken ;
Talk of !
“ Straight ahead of me a chasm,
Yawning chasm !
Thousand feet!!
On each side of me a mountain,
,) ust how high I wasn’t countin’,
But completely perpendic’lar!
’Twas a tickler
And a treat!
And them yelling fiends behind me ;
Bound to catch me ; sure to find me ;
Couldn’t miss me ! Well, you mind me
It was sweet !
“ On they came, a swarm—Great Csesar !
Like the bees a-
Round the comb.
Yelling ! Howling ! Screaming ! Roaring !!
I could feel the sweat a-pouring,
And the horse could go no farther,
Laved in lather,
Flaked in foam ;
It was certain death - no ransom ;
And I wasn’t game to chance ’em !
So 1 whistled for a hansom,
And came home ! ’
N.S.W.
W. T. GOODGE.
A Reformed Character.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Well, Mr. Philipson may be very much in
earnest —he must be, judging by the way he per-
spires—but his greatest admirer would not call
him entertaining. Highly respected, and re-
spectable? Oh, of course. His wife is the
managing partner in the concern, though. Look
at her. Pretty woman once, and a clever one
still. You would not think there was any
romance about that couple, would you ? He was
a different man when I knew him, 20 years ago,
and circumstances were different, too. Yes, he
is a tedious old political bore, but he will bein the
Legislative Council in less than two years. I’ll
tell you his story when we get round to the club.
It’s 20 years old now, and nobody would believe
it anyway. Mind the step.
“ When * King Khama,’ as they called him,
was over in England, the Exeter Hall people
made a great fuss of him. The British public,
who know Australia well by the fact that the besb
varieties of tinned mutton grow on the gum-trees,
had an idea that he was a monarch of a size some-
where between the German Emperor and the
King of Spain. As a matter of fact, he was the
titular chief of a small South African tribe called
the ‘ Bhamangwato’ somewhere in Bechuanaland,
just about big enough to fill a small Australian
township. There were dozens such in Griqua-
land and British Bechuanaland in my day, and I
daresay there are now. Philipson did a kindness
to one of these petty chiefs named ’Mpandu, who
lived somewhere near Vryburg, and it was about
the best investment he ever made.
“ Amongst all the hard bargains we had to deal
with in the mounted police, Philipson was about
the hardest. He had a knack of picking up the
native ways and dialects that made him useful,
but he was enough to turn a man’s hair grey in
his other little ways. He used to drink pretty
steadily all the time. If he was rarely quite
drunk, he was seldom sober enough to swear by.
And he was the deuce and all among women. It
is one of the marvels of any man’s life to see the
unconscionable scallywags that win the admira-
tion of womankind. A man does not need to be
good-looking, or clever, or rich, or successful.
If he has two certain qualities, and those two
only', he will make female friends, and wreck
reputations, as long as he is sober enough to talk.
What are the qualities ? A pleasant speaking
voice and a sympathetic manner.
“’Mpandu, Philipson’s native friend, was not
popular with the missionaries, or with the police. It
generally takes a taste for religion and liquor to
bring out the worst qualities of a Bechuana, but
’Mpandu had gifts, and developed into a first-class
nuisance on liquor alone. He got into serious
trouble over some diamonds that were not his,
and Philipson saved him from a long holiday on
the Cape Town breakwater. When one considers
what an unmitigated rascal the chief was, it makes
his gratitude to Philipson the more remarkable.
Gratitude is not common among Kaffirs.
“We used to have occasional visits from Imperial
officers in those days ; and, until you got used to
it, their patronising ways were a little trying. I
was sub-inspector of mounted police—a semi-mili-
tary post which always involved rough and
dangerous work—and Colonel Warburton used to
get on my nerves a bit. The trifling fact that we
had forgotten more about native warfare than he
ever knew, and generally saw more active service
in a year than the average Imperial officer does in
a life-time, did not diminish the number of frills
he put on in the least. If he unbent a little in
his lighter moments, he had a daughter who kept
up the standard all the time. She was a pretty
woman, and knew it. Her father’s official position
and her own brains made her the most sought-
after lady in Kimberley and the parts adjacent.
Everybody who was anybody danced attendance
on Miss Warburton, and quite a number of us pro-
posed to her. No, I was not of the number.
“ His native cheek, and respectable connections
at home, gave Philipson a precarious sort of footing
as a visitor at -the ,YVarburtons’, and he could
m ike a little go a long way. Warburton came to
u, ■ in a towering rage one day, and said that he
had reason to believe one of my trebly-dashed
troopers had dared to insult his daughter, and
demanded his bead on a plate instantly. I soothed
him down, and Philipson obligingly solved the
difficulty. He did what even the easy standards
of Cape Colony would not tolerate, and fled.
His usual methods with the fair sex were effectual
and safe, so that I take it he must have been more
under the influence of drink than usual. He took
refuge with his friend ’Mpandu, who received him
with open arms, who pressed him to his infected
chest; and they had a high-class spree, lasting
three days.
“ ’Mpandu was rich enough to buy a new wife
—when he wanted one —and did not see
anything to object to in his friend’s little
escapade. I did not want to catch Philipson,
for various reasons ; and although ’Mpandu (un-
officially) let me know where he was, his arrest
would have stirred up too many scandals to make
it good policy. It was on one of the many occa-
sions when ’Mpandu came to tell me where I could
capture Philipson, if I wanted him, and after a
rattier unsuccessful attempt, on my part, to .make
him see the sinfulness of not paying his hut-tax
regularly, that I dropped a remark that set
’Mpandu thinking. I said a good wife would do
a den t o steady Philipson. ’Mpandu said 1 Ehwa,’
which means ‘ yes,’and other things too numerous
to menti'-n. One of the other things cropped up
aft'-rwards, and staggered me, I confess.
“’M nandu -et about finding a wife for his friend.
Not a native wife, although you might have
thought he would, but a white wife, which was a
mtc i larger order. There were lots of ladies in
Kimberley whose absence would have been gain,
and 'Mpandu could have laid his commodious
black hand on them easily enough. But he did
not. want the damaged article. The sudden dis-
appearance of Miss Warburton showed what he
did want, although I did not connect the two
facts till long afterwards.
“The fir>t Warburton heard of it was when he
get a letter from the safe side of the Free State
border to say that his daughter and Philipson
were married. He wanted to declare war on the
Orange Free State. ’Mpandu met me with a
genial smile, a month afterwards; said it was a
privilege to entertain such a lady as the Colonel s
daughter in his humble kraal, and intimated that
she had been his guest for a week. When I asked
if she went there of her own free will, he said he
was thirsty, or words to that effect.
“ Well, Mr. and Mrs. (?) Philipson got down to
Natal- at her expense, for she had some money in
her own right—and from there to Melbourne.
She kept him straight, as much to his own sur-
prise as anybody’s, I daresay. And you saw the
result to-night. The women, of course, said she
planned the whole tiling. The men said Philip-
son compromised her to induce her to marry him ;
and a’so (incidentally) that lie should have been
tarred and feathered. ’Mpandu said nothing
worth mentioning.
W. P. HAMILTON.

The same remarks that apply to milk apply equally to butter. Pasteurised butter is made from the very finest
Cream, consequently the butter lias a fine aroma that only Pasteurisation can give. There are a few, we are
pleased to say, only a few, idiotic folks who will not be convinced of the value of the process ; but it is satisfactory
to kuow that these are getting less daily. The difference in price between Pasteurised, beautifully-flavored butter and the
ordinary make is only per lb. It would pay consumers well to give six times this difference. Heads of households should insist
that their grocer supplies Pasteurised butter, and if he will not, send your orders to US, and we will deliver it at your doors.
Good butter such as we have described is appreciated always, whereas at afternoon teas or for ordinary use
at any price. The famous “ Berry ” brand Pasteurised butter can be obtained only from this Company.
